3.1
The backcasting approach put forward by Robinson (1990) in his publication ’Futures Under
Glass: A Recipe for People Who Hate to Predict’. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .
18
4.1
Overview of number of solutions that fall within 13 diﬀerent categories. The overview ex-
cluded duplicates or very similar solutions that were streamlined and grouped in the process
of presenting the results. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .
26
4.2
Ranking approach to prioritise pathways according to four criteria based on a scale from 1 to
3. The approach is taken from Group 13 which additionally included the criteria of certainty
(Frederikke Linulf et al., 2020). . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .
27
5.1
The quantity of available literature based oﬀof keyword searches on ’Web of Science’ for many
diﬀerent ﬁelds indicated by the diﬀerent sub-ﬁgures. The quantity of the literature available
is dependant on the ﬁltering settings used in web of science. Thus, for each keyword search
the literature was reﬁned into three categories: Overall topic, titles containing the keyword
and overall topic reﬁned to studies in the ’environmental science’ ﬁeld. The keyword searches
can be grouped into four categories. (a) Future techniques including backcasting, forecasting,
scenario analysis and foresight thinking. (b) The ﬁeld of backcasting is divided into the three
main types being, backcasting from scenarios, principles and participatory backcasting. (c) The
scenario space and types of scenarios are divided into exploratory, predictive and normative.
(d) Additional literature that was explored to further the knowledge required to understand
relevant tools to aid the DTU backcasting methodology. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .
32
5.2
The backcasting methodology used in Geurs and van Wee (2004) to determine sustainable
transport policy measures in the Netherlands. The methodology is an adaptation of the original
backcasting approach deﬁned by Robinson (1990). . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .
42
5.3
Depiction of policy package and policy path approach as discussed in Giaoutzi et al. (2012).
Depiction originally extracted from Banister (1998). . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .
54
6.1
Overview of consulted experts and approached authors to explore the topic of backcasting.

case study; academic contact; and recommended contacts from the European Environment
Agency. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .

7.1

i
==PAGE:6==
8.1
The DTU backcasting methodology designed to ﬂexibly support policy framework for local,
regional, national and international governance. The numbering and ﬂows indicate order and
direction in which the seven steps of the methodology should be conducted. . . . . . . . . . .
82
8.2
The seven steps of the ’DTU backcasting methodology’ with each of the mandatory or potential
sub-steps. The sub-steps in black and white box’s are optional to reduce the resource intensity
of conducting the methodology, however, if conducted they have potentially fruitful impacts
on the robustness of the study. The arrows in the ﬁgure represent the direction in which the
steps and sub-step should be administered. This includes the iterative feedback loop as a result
of the ’evaluation and iteration’ element of the methodology. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .
83
8.3
Adapted version of the futures cone by Hancock and Bezold (1994). Within the space lies
possible scenarios of the future. The closer to the centre of the cone the more realistic the
scenarios. This is not to be confused with probability, as forecasting the future is inherently
uncertain. For this reason, a business-as-usual scenario is not illustrated in the cone, instead,
multiple baselines are considered. Within the possible scenarios, there are preferred, realistic,
normative and non-normative scenarios. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .
91
8.4
Taken from (Gray et al., 2020), Outlining four exploratory scenarios based on two of the signif-
icant and uncertain drivers in the study. Each scenario was developed in detail and validated
by stakeholders. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .
96
8.5
Depiction of concepts for target setting until 2050 based on (1) linear target setting, (2) am-
bitious target setting, (3) reluctant target setting and (4) variation of previous target setting
approaches.
. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 103
8.6
Heat map of the DTU backcasting methodology indicating where participation can utilised
and where it has the most value. ’Red’ indicates large beneﬁts from participatory involvement,
’amber’ is signiﬁcant, ’yellow’ moderate and ’grey’ not beneﬁcial. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 119

==PAGE:9==
Abstract
To achieve the 2050 sustainability goals, Europe must shift from common practices and undergo large scale
transformative change. This thesis focuses on the use of Backcasting in a European policy context and explores
how a methodology can be used to achieve the goals on sustainability. The analysis conducted includes;
a teaching-based research activity; literature review, covering the state of the art; and communication with
many experts in the ﬁeld of backcasting. It was found that there is a gap for a ﬂexible backcasting methodology
which can be tailored to ﬁt the purpose and context of applications. This rendered the generation of the
’DTU Backcasting Methodology’ which aims to provide a ﬂexible backcasting approach. The DTU backcasting
methodology consists of seven steps which facilitate detailed pathway generation, supported by examples from
a case study on the European Union (EU) energy sector. The generation of the DTU backcasting methodology
makes backcasting more accessible, advancing the knowledge of foresight and future techniques in the realm
of EU policy. Further, it combines multiple approaches, techniques and tools observed in the ﬁeld into one
method and explores in detail, elements of pathway generation, which is seldom discussed in the literature.
However, to fully facilitate a ’one-size-ﬁts-all’ methodology, additional improvement is required concerning
the complexity of the methodology and chosen delimitations.
v
==PAGE:10==
Terminology
The topic of backcasting is an exhaustive foresight and future planning area which has remarkable familiar-
ity to other topics and their established terminology. Thereby, terms such as scenarios, paths, pathways and
images of the future have found diﬀerent interpretations in the future planning community and can lead to
confusion and misunderstanding when applied in the wrong context. In order to provide a coherent termi-
nology in the following pages, deﬁnitions of terms has been conducted and applied to streamline and easily
compare literature and case studies. Further these deﬁnitions can be referred to if needed. The applied termi-
nology is presented as follows:
Desirable: The objectives of the study have been met.
End-point: The term end-point is used as a synonym of ’image of the future’ or ’future image’. The end-point
is at the end of a scenario respective to time and includes a description of the state of the system under con-
sideration.
Exploratory scenarios: is any kind of scenario. They do not have to be desirable. Exploratory scenarios can
be chosen on the basis of dissimilar elements (e.g. technology-, management-steered scenario, ...). Exploratory
scenarios are part of scenario techniques and or not inherently part of a backcasting approach. They are used
to explore the possible/feasible scenario space.
Normative scenario: is a scenario in which the objectives of the study have been achieved. Thus, they are
desirable from a backcasting perspective.
Path: A path describes the direction of action into the future. However, it is not designed to arrive at assigned
target year of the objectives. This terminology is frequently used in the FSSD and ABCD-methodology since
pathways are not considered. Not to be confused with the terminology pathway.
Pathway: A pathway is deﬁned as a sequence of events that lead up to the objectives of the study, i.e. their
set target year.
Preferable (normative) Scenarios: Scenarios which are both normative (relative to set objectives) and pre-
ferred by participants/stakeholders.
Realistic (normative) Scenarios: are normative (relative to set objectives) scenarios that are practical to
achieve in the future.
Scenario: Consists of an end-point and a pathway towards that end-point. The deﬁnition of a scenario can
vary between literature (John Robinson, Personal Communication, 2020; van Asselt et al., 2000). In context of
this thesis the use of ’scenario themes’ in combination with pathways is suﬃcient to be classiﬁed as scenarios
(Kosow and Gaßner, 2008).
Scenario theme: Guiding principle in which the scenario is structured. This does not necessarily describe a
future image but rather describe the unfolding of events that lead to the scenario end-point. Example: ’tech-
nology’, ’management’, ’lifestyle’. Scenarios and future images are closely related.
Solution/ solution elements: Pathway elements which are positive in achieving the objectives of the study.
The solutions are not strictly adjacent to certain categories (e.g. technology, policy measures, etc.). Therefore,
the term solutions refers to every action or event that contributes to achieving set objectives.
vi
==PAGE:11==
Sustainability principles: This term is in relation to the principles of sustainability as deﬁned by the FSSD
Sustainability conditions: This is in relation to deﬁnitions of sustainability described in the sub-step ’sus-
tainability conditions’. This is similar to the sustainability principles, however, since the sustainability condi-
tions can be deﬁned by the practitioner of the study, the term conditions is used to diﬀerentiate between the
two.
Objectives: The objectives relate to the visions, goals and targets of the backcasting study.
Vision: the overall mantra or guiding principle describing the desired state for chosen end-point in time.
Goals: are closely linked to the vision as they elaborate in more detail what has to be achieved to translate
the described vision into reality.
Targets: relate the goals to quantiﬁable achievements that have to be reached as set out by the scope of the
study.
Multiple incommensurable baselines: multiple baselines based upon a range of identiﬁed trends without
preconceiving the likelihood of occurrence.
Flexible platforms approach: In relation to the concept that The Natural Step takes towards prioritising
solutions to form paths into the future.
Flexible pathways approach: Inspired by The Natural Steps ﬂexible steps ﬂexible platforms approach. The
ﬂexible pathways approach intends to apply the ﬂexible platforms approach whilst consider pathways towards
the objectives.
Solution Package: a group of solutions that complement each other. This is inspired by David Banisters
work on policy packages (Banister, 1998).
’What’ in the context of pathways: refers to the description of solutions but not their implementation.
There are levels of ’whats’ which can be used to add more detail. For more information see section 7.9.
’How’ in the context of pathways: refers to an implementation option of a solutions. For more information
see section 7.9.
vii

The Development and Use of Backcasting as Methodology to meet the 2050

==PAGE:14==
Chapter 1
Introduction
Globally, we are facing one of the largest and most urgent challenges of the century, the likes of which are
on an unprecedented scale. The global population is projected to grow to 10 billion by 2050, while resource
use globally will double by 2060, with growth in energy demand of 30% by 2040 and increased water demand
of 55% by 2050 (EEA, 2019a). Besides these eye-opening projections, the current state of the environment
facing a loss of biodiversity, land desertiﬁcation and increasingly unpredictable weather patterns is alarming
today. The consumption and production culture prevalent in much of today’s western culture, particularly
in Europe, is on the verge of causing persistent problems aﬀecting our ecological and social systems (EEA,
2020b). The challenge we face is to achieve our long-term sustainability goals and live within the limits of
our planet. Achieving these goals in sustainability requires that "transformative and bold steps [...] to shift
the world on to a sustainable and resilient path" are taken as recognised by the United Nations Agenda for
Sustainable Development (United Nations, 2015).
Backcasting is a strategic planning method which falls under the foresight and future techniques ﬁeld. It
moves away from forecasting which looks at probable futures and instead asks what future do we want and
then working ’backwards’ to determine how can it be achieved. Backcasting is an eﬀective planning tool since
the future is inherently uncertain. Society should move away from prediction based planning and consider
pathways towards desirable futures. By envisioning the future we want to achieve, we can determine how it
can be achieved. To paraphrase Martin Luther King, change starts with a dream. Backcasting is particularly
relevant in environmental studies since urgent large scale action is required to facilitate a sustainable and
balanced lifestyle in the future.
The scope of the thesis lies within a European policy context with the addition of a long-term sustainabil-
ity vision as anchored in the European Green Deal (European Commission, 2019a). This thesis project is in
collaboration between the Technical University of Denmark (DTU - Danmarks Tekniske Universitet) and the
European Environmental Agency (EEA). The work conducted in the following seeks to provide a foundation
of how backcasting could be framed on an European Union (EU) level and provide these insights to the Euro-
pean Environment Agency. As an agency of the European Union, the EEA provides sound and independent
information on the environment. Further, the EEA is the major information source for those involved in de-
veloping, adopting, implementing and evaluating environmental policy.
The recent publication, ’The European environment — state and outlook 2020’ by the EEA (2019a), indicates
that the world is currently facing urgent sustainability challenges with current projections indicating fun-
damentally unsustainable futures. Currently, the approaches towards strategic planning are predominantly
focused around projections and eﬀorts to bend the trends. The state and outlook report, calls for increased
1
==PAGE:15==
Chapter 1. Introduction
DTU Environment
eﬀorts to tackle this urgent problem and emphasises that in the next decade there needs to be a change in how
we operate, new answers are required to solve the issue that the last 40 years of business-as-usual are leading
us towards. In particular, there is an urgency for approaches which tackle the questions: what needs to hap-
pen as well as how it needs to be done? Current approaches to tackle the climate issue are often short-sighted
and are undermined by ever-changing politics which rarely facilitates long-term frameworks. In the literature
backcasting is a tool which has been used to deal with transformation change, however, there are few studies
which attempt to analyse on a European scale. Furthermore, there is yet to be a coherent framework that aims
to structure backcasting on an EU level. The development of such an approach is especially relevant with
the introduction of the European Green Deal by the European Commission in December 2019. The European
Green Deal serves as the core political document to transform the European social and economic community
to face climate change and urgent threats facing future generations. It aligns with the other long-term pol-
icy goals as outlined by the Sustainable Development Goals and the Paris Agreement. In text, the European
Green Deal aims to "transform the EU into a fair and prosperous society, with a modern, resource-eﬃcient and
competitive economy where there are no net emissions of greenhouse gases in 2050 and where economic growth is
decoupled from resource use" - European Commission (2019a). This vision will cause a fundamental change in
all sectors of life and will be accompanied by a range of impacts on the environment, society and industries. To
achieve such a transition, innovation of incomparable dimensions is required which requires the right tools
and techniques to provide grounds for discussion transparently and productively. Further, the Green Deal
provides the notion that the involvement and commitment of the public and other stakeholders are crucial
to achieving the green transition. Backcasting, in a participatory setting, can facilitate discussion with trans-
parency on how to act now, whilst still considering the long-term vision. Further, the backcasting ﬁeld is
dominated by participatory approaches, since designing the vision of the future must be a democratic process
to form collective desirability.
The literature related to the ﬁeld of backcasting is relatively small compared to the ﬁeld of scenario planning
which is closely related to the topic of foresight or future studies. These categories have been the main focus
of research in terms of planning for future scenarios for the dominant fraction of time. Since the initial work
by Amory Lovins in 1976 related to backwards-looking analysis and subsequently, its birth as ’backcasting’
in the work by Prof. Dr. John Robinson in 1990; the area of backcasting has found increasing attention in the
literature. Today, there is a considerable quantity of literature available on diﬀerent backcasting techniques
and approaches for future planning. The literature includes theoretical and practical documents including
philosophical discussion as well as instructional procedures for the application of backcasting. Thereby, three
key backcasting ﬁelds can be identiﬁed, being: backcasting from scenarios, backcasting from principles and
participatory backcasting. These topics have found an expansion in a variety of combinations by introduc-
ing and joining further scientiﬁc techniques and concepts such as the Framework for Strategic Sustainable
Development (FSSD), modelling tools or assessment and analysis techniques. In recent years participatory
backcasting has gained immense focus due to its relevance in facilitating stakeholder processes, which are
crucial when planning for the future with a long-term vision.
This thesis studies the concept of backcasting in a wider application of strategic planning, to determine how a
backcasting methodology can be used to achieve the 2050 goals on sustainability. Moreover, the work intends
2
==PAGE:16==
Chapter 1. Introduction
DTU Environment
to further the development in the ﬁeld of backcasting by drawing upon a multitude of tools and techniques
to form an adaptable and ﬂexible methodology that can be used across a range of contexts and purposes. The
research questions posed in the context of this thesis therefore are:
• What is the state of art in the context of backcasting applications for strategic planning?
• What are relevant concepts and identiﬁed gaps regarding the topic of backcasting for long-term policy
support and what conclusions can be drawn from the ﬁndings?
This thesis studies the concept of backcasting in a wider application of strategic planning, to determine how a
backcasting methodology can be used to achieve the 2050 goals on sustainability. Moreover, the work intends
to further the development in the ﬁeld of backcasting by drawing upon a multitude of tools and techniques
to form an adaptable and ﬂexible methodology that can be used across a range of contexts and purposes. The
above outlined questions are related to the aims and objectives of this thesis. The aim is to elaborate on posed
research questions and to further identify relevant areas of research in the ﬁeld of backcasting, especially
regarding the scope of the thesis. The objectives in this thesis are to reﬂect learnings from application of
backcasting in a teaching-based research activity, establish a state of the art, explore the topic of backcasting
from a research as well as from a practical perspective and in the process of doing so, establish a network of
experts in the ﬁeld of backcasting. This leads to the formulation of understandable lessons learnt and related
identiﬁed gaps in knowledge. These ﬁndings inspired the generation of a dedicated backcasting methodology
which provides suﬃcient guidance whilst remaining ﬂexible to accommodate for a range of purposes and ap-
plications in diﬀerent contexts. As a ﬁnal objective, these procedures shall be documented in a comprehensible
and understandable way to provide transparency on the processes in relation to this thesis and its results. A
number of delimitations are taken to focus the attention of the thesis on stated scope. These delimitations
relate to:
• theoretical background of sustainable development and sustainability conditions
• ethics in backcasting
• exploration of modelling
• related topics in future and foresight studies
• (to an extent) assessment in relation to backcasting (economic assessment, risk analysis, uncertainty
analysis)
• conduction of a full-breadth case study
In chapter 2, the methodological procedures applied in the context of the thesis are outlined including proce-
dures on case study reviewing, teaching of backcasting in a teaching-based research activity and consultation
of experts. Following the methodological explanations, a basic overview on the topic of backcasting is provided
in chapter 3, highlighting the historical background, basic principles and relevant methodological concepts re-
lated to backcasting. This is followed by the presentation of results gathered from the teaching-based research
activity, review of case studies and consultation of experts in respective chapters 4, 5 and 6. These chapters
accumulate to present reﬂections on the lessons learnt regarding relevant aspects of backcasting in an EU
context in chapter 7. The main part of the thesis is the developed methodology presented in the subsequent

chapter 8 titled ’DTU backcasting methodology’. This chapter elaborates on a detailed backcasting structure
for application in a European policy support context. It further exempliﬁes the application of the proposed
methodology in the context of the EU energy system in example boxes for each individual structural element.
This is followed by chapter 9 on available tools and techniques to enhance the assessment aspect of the pro-
posed methodology. The thesis arrives at the discussion of the DTU backcasting methodology and relevant
key learnings as gained from this thesis in chapter 10. This is ﬁnalised by the conclusion in chapter 11, bibli-
ography and additional sections attached in the Appendix.

==PAGE:18==
Chapter 2
Method
In the following chapter, the methods and approaches used to develop the backcasting methodology are de-
scribed. The approach of developing an improved backcasting methodology in this thesis requires a high
degree of knowledge on the topic supported by expert insights as well as creative and innovative solutions.
Thus, a medley of literature reviews, teaching-based research and expert involvement were utilised through-
out and are presented in the following.
2.1
Literature Review
Before the start of the master thesis, a special course was carried out with the title "Origins of Backcasting"
which was supervised by Associate Professor Steﬀen Foss Hansen. The work was conducted simultaneously
to a teaching-based research activity (see section 2.2). Thus, an initial literature review was carried out in
the context of the teaching-based research activity which included studying relevant and prominent method-
ologies. In regards to the origin of backcasting, the literature review focused on: the identiﬁcation of main
authors in the ﬁeld; the history of backcasting in regards to scenario analysis and planning; and the ﬁrst scan
of available case studies. Further, the deliverable of the teaching-based research activity included an elabora-
tion on the basic structure and elements of backcasting, including two prominent backcasting methodologies,
the ’Robinson approach’ and the ’ABCD-methodology’.
Following the work carried on from the teaching-based research activity as well as studying the origins of the
ﬁeld, a strong base for understanding the ﬁeld of backcasting and surrounding topics was utilised. However,
backcasting is an extensive ﬁeld and thus contains many applications, each of which incorporates diﬀer-
ent tools, techniques, methodologies and additional supporting research ﬁelds. Thus, an extensive literature
search on backcasting whilst focusing the attention on sustainability relevant topics was conducted. The
approach to determining relevant literature started by identifying the key authors in the ﬁeld. Due to the
prior work conducted on the history of backcasting a backward reference search approach was utilised and
particular authors were identiﬁed. The authors were studied due to their eﬀorts in advancing the ﬁeld with
numerous methods, approaches and/or techniques. To identify additional literature a backward and forward
author search was conducted to identify much of the knowledge each applied towards their ﬁeld. In doing
so, additional authors and studies were identiﬁed, hence prompting further research for the literature review.
Despite this, there were remaining gaps in the knowledge regarding the surrounding ﬁelds of future tech-
niques along with additional supporting literature on topics out of the scope of the study. To ﬁnd relevant
information on these topics a forward literature search was conducted to examine and consider other topics
of interest. Often this was achieved by conducting keyword searches in combination with ﬁelds of interest.
5
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 2. Method
DTU Environment
The search engines used for conducting the literature review were ’Web of Science’ and ’Google Scholar’. The
list of key search words includes: ’backcasting from principles’; ’backcasting from scenarios’; ’scenario anal-
ysis’; ’forecasting’; ’foresight’; ’future techniques’; ’exploratory’; ’normative’; ’projective’; ’predictive’; ’case
study’; ’participation’; ’ABCD’ and ’Robinson’.
To understand the array of diﬀerent backcasting methodologies and how a backcasting method can be de-
veloped to ﬁt within a European policy framework, a range of published backcasting applications within the
environmental ﬁeld were reviewed. In particular, the review pays attention to goal formulation, deﬁnition of
the current state or business-as-usual, pathway generation and assessment criteria. It recognises cases where
the application of a method has been conducted and what lessons learnt can be extracted and translated in
the context of the master thesis. The selection of case studies was based on the relevance of the case study in
regards to backcasting as a methodological concept and whether or not the methodology was applied in a case
study format. This was evaluated against time constraints in this thesis. In total, 15 backcasting case studies
are included in this thesis, allowing for deeper insights into the disparity of all the applications. The reasons
for selecting these case studies is their relationship to an environmental topic; their contribution and unique
approach towards backcasting; and its relevance in an EU policy context. In particular, emphasis was put on
exploring various methodologies across a range of contexts and scopes. Covering an array of applications
made it possible to see the limitations and beneﬁts of using diﬀerent backcasting methodologies. However, in
order to present the ﬁndings of the reviews, ﬁve case studies were hand-picked to illustrate the main ﬁndings
from the literature review in detail. The case studies and reasons for their selection are indicated below:
1. Backcasting as a tool for sustainability transport policy making - The paper by Geurs and van
Wee (2004) was chosen as it utilises an adapted version of the Robinson approach discussed in chapter
3. Furthermore, political implementation is considered within the scope of the study.
2. Sustainable cities in a backcasting perspective - This paper, authored by Broman et al. (2013), makes
use of the ABCD-methodology and backcasting from principles. Both of which are embedded in the
Framework for Strategic Sustainable Development (FSSD). This is a method that is commonly used in
the backcasting ﬁeld and as such this case study was included. In addition, the authors of the study make
use of a ’resource kit’ and system dynamics. An element that may be useful for an EU wide context and
scope.
3. Backcasting in transport: scenarios for the future of European long-distance transport - This
particular paper by Schippl and Leisner (2009) is one of few Europe wide backcasting studies. Since the
case study explored in this thesis has a similar scope, it was deemed vital to include.
4. Applying backcasting and system dynamics towards sustainable development - The use of sys-
tem dynamics and system thinking is something that must be considered in the ’DTU backcasting
methodology’ in order to be suitable for EU wide application and complex systems. As such the pa-
per by Musse et al. (2018) was chosen to be included.
5. Scenario analysis as a foresight tool in agriculture - Giaoutzi et al. (2012) present a comprehensive
6
==PAGE:20==
Chapter 2. Method
DTU Environment
backcasting study with an EU wide scope. In addition, they incorporated the concept of policy packages
taken from Banister (1998) and is a tool that can be applied to support backcasting in a policy context.
2.2
Teaching-Based Research
This part of the methodological approach applied in the context of the master thesis elaborates on two ele-
ments. First a brief presentation and consideration of the basic relationship between the types of teaching and
knowledge generation is presented. Second, the description of the speciﬁc activity carried out for the purpose
of this thesis is outlined.
2.2.1
Curriculum Design and Research-Teaching Nexus
The basic understanding and terminology used in the following, regarding research-informed teaching (RTI) is
adapted from Charlet Annie and Shemim (2019). Figure 2.1 depicts the relation between the design of a course
curriculum and the research-teaching nexus as it is presented in Willcoxson et al. (2011). In a ’teacher focused’
setting the exchange of knowledge takes direction from knowledge-owner (the teacher) to the recipient of the
knowledge (the students). In the case of a ’student focused’ setting, the students are challenged to solve a
problem or task following the basic concept of problem-based learning (Willcoxson et al., 2011). This puts
the emphasis on personal acquisition of knowledge and thereby inspiring and stimulating the learning and
solving process (Jonassen, 1997). Further, Figure 2.1 can be separated into ’emphasis on research content’ and
’emphasis on research process and problems’. The design of the curriculum of the course determines which
form of teaching is applied as represented by the 2x2 matrix in the ﬁgure.
Figure 2.1: Relation between curriculum design and research-teaching nexus as presented in Willcoxson et al.
(2011).
7
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 2. Method
DTU Environment
The approach in the context of course "12240 - Environmental Management and Ethics" is inspired by Will-
coxson et al. (2011). The authors invert the existing understanding and postulate that teaching and learning
activities can directly fed into research. It can be argued that the concept of the mentioned course is a mix
of several research-informed teaching types. For example, the curriculum of the course required the students
to document and discuss their ﬁndings in a ’research-tutored’ fashion. However, the main focus of the cur-
riculum was on the problem solving aspect (’research-based’), which the students had to address in an open
and creative manner. Thereby, the approach represented in this thesis can be considered as a ’teaching-based
research’ approach (Willcoxson et al., 2011).
2.2.2
Teaching-Based Research Activity
The activity was carried out with a bulk of 80 students in the course "12240 - Environmental Management and
Ethics". The course is taught by Associate Professor Steﬀen Foss Hansen and Postdoc Lauge Peter Westergaard
Clausen. The authors of this thesis took part in the course as teaching assistants, whilst preparing a document
on the "Origins of Backcasting". The course took place over three weeks in January 2020 and included the de-
livery of a political memo and cover. The students were asked to form groups of four and to equally distribute
across one of three topics: energy, mobility and food. Thereby, a total of 20 groups participated in the course.
The course took outset in the report by the European Environment Agency "The European environment —
state and outlook 2020" (EEA, 2019a), which describes the current state of the European environment and
provides an outlook for the future. The students were asked to address the resulting gap between the current
state and set EU goals and targets until 2050 by applying backcasting.
The focus of this teaching-based research approach was directed at how the students coped with generating
pathways towards the 2050 European goals and targets as described in the provided literature (see Table
2.1). This gave insight into how to intuitively approach pathway generation and furthermore allowed to
create a workshop-like setting which due to its participatory element is considered part of the presented
thesis document.

The students were provided with the basic knowledge about backcasting from principles, including the ABCD-
methodology (see section 3.4.3), which is easy to comprehend in a short amount of time. Additional literature
on backcasting was provided for the students to explore (see Table 2.1). Further, the students received on-
going lectures on topics as environmental ethics, environmental management, EU regulation & law as well
as decision-support tools like stakeholder analysis, DPSIR (Driving forces, Pressures, States, Impacts and Re-
9
==PAGE:23==
Chapter 2. Method
DTU Environment
sponses) and cost-beneﬁt analysis. The instructions provided on how to structure and generate pathways was
intentionally limited to investigate which approaches and concepts the students would come up with. The
generation of pathways is not at the core of the ABCD-methodology, however, this concept was chosen as it is
easily comprehensible in its basic structure. As a basic guidance to document the outcome of work an Action
Plan Format was provided.
The delivery of the course included a three-part memo and a cover letter. The memo consisted of Part 1
"Introduction and background", Part 2 "Presentation and analysis of the diﬀerent pathways and decide on
priorities" and Part 3 "Discussion and recommendations". In Part 1 the students were asked to extract the
essential information about the current European state of the environment regarding their chosen topic from
provided literature. Further, they were asked to elaborate on the existing goals for the target year 2050, which
were described in literature and political documents (see Table 2.1). This element basically resembled the
steps A (Visioning) and B (Current State) of the ABCD-methodology. Part 2 of the delivery included the
"Presentation and analysis of the diﬀerent pathways and decide on priorities", which represents step C and
D of the ABCD-methodology with the addition of actual structured pathways. The students were asked to
design 3 to 5 diﬀerent pathways towards the identiﬁed 2050 goals. The sketched pathways should include 3-5
year milestones, targets and actions to be taken. The chosen actions should be documented in an Action Plan
Format in order to answer the following questions:
• WHY should this action be taken?
• WHAT does the action consist of?
• WHO should carry out the action?
• WHO will be aﬀected by the action?
• WHEN should the action be carried out?
• WHERE should the action take place?
• HOW can it be determined whether the action taken is successful?
Additionally, the students were asked to apply taught decision-support tools towards at least three criteria:
ethics, environment and economics. Finding ways to accommodate for trade-oﬀs between systems and to
integrate system thinking in their analysis was a further requirement for Part 2 of the political memo. It was
required that the ﬁndings are based on scientiﬁc literature or other credible sources of information. The "Dis-
cussion and recommendations" were presented in Part 3 of the delivery. In this part students were asked to
provide policy recommendations based on their previous work and assessment. Reﬂections and discussion of
encountered limitations was considered an additional element of the examination. To provide a brief statement
for political discussion purposes a cover letter was produced by the students, summarising vital information
for politicians. This information included: "Recommendation"; "Problem"; "State of Aﬀairs"; "Solution and
Process"; "Economics" and "Press Strategy" related to the chosen topic.

students were asked to reﬂect on their use of backcasting in form of an attachment to their delivery or as part
of an online survey. The results from this section are delimited to the groups that focused on the EU energy
sector due to the scope of this thesis. Chapter 4 elaborates on the results of this activity.
2.3
Consultation with Experts
Backcasting is a ﬁeld in which stakeholder and expert participation is a key technique to gather information
and insights (Robinson et al., 2011). Therefore, the discussion with experts is considered key to developing the
DTU backcasting methodology. Following the approach by Finkbeiner (2016) which is adapted from Patton
(2001), there are four diﬀerent approaches to conducting interviews. Table 2.2 displays these four concepts
which are deﬁned as "informal interview"; "general interview guide approach"; "standardised open-ended
interview" and "closed interview". These four types diﬀer in regard to the degree of freedom the interviewer
has to guide and steer the interview.

Four types of interviewing techniques as presented in Finkbeiner (2016) (adapted from Patton
(2001)).

Following the description of interview techniques in Table 2.2, the approach taken in context of this thesis
can be described as "general interview guide approach". Initially, experts were identiﬁed through the previous
steps of general literature review on the topic of backcasting and case study reviews as well as proposed by
supervisors and contacts at the European Environment Agency. First point of contact was the exchange of
emails, which partially lead on to scheduling online meetings. A full list of contacts and forms of communica-
tion can be found in Table 5 - Appendix. The interviews were conducted in an open-format with preparation
of open-ended questions. This more structured way of guiding the interviews allowed to gain insight into ex-

perts’ knowledge and expertise in an appropriate way and allowed to explore related topics besides the main

The interviewing process was applied to collect opinions from various experts across the ﬁeld of backcasting

Chapter 2. Method

2.4
Methodological Development
Reviewed case studies and existing methods show variations and diﬀerent approaches of conducting back-
casting studies. However, it was observed that the methods proposed and applied by diﬀerent authors are not
adaptable in nor designed for the context of European policy-support. Therefore, it is proposed to generate
a ﬂexible method that can be adapted to the needs of the administers of studies in such contexts. The pro-
posed structure therefore includes 7 structural elements which contain mandatory and optional activities or
sub-steps. The structure was generated based on an approach as described in the following.
After the initial literature and case study review, the teaching-based research activity and consultation with
experts; brainstorming sessions were held to determine a preliminary structure for the backcasting methodol-
ogy. This structure took into consideration the key lessons learnt from the prior steps, which is document in
section 7. Once the overall structure was determined the individual structure elements were explored, looking
at possible ways to conduct each section. This was done by applying variations of the structure elements of
the proposed backcasting methodology step-by-step and reviewing the results. In this context examples were
carried out on the European energy sector, which are provided to further the understanding in chapter 8. The
approach taken to generate scenarios in step 3 ’future techniques’ and subsequently to assemble the ’solution
bank’ in step 4 relies on analysing existing case studies (see result section 5) as well as on generated solution
elements gained through the teaching-based research activity (see result section 4). As a conduction of work-
shops in participation with stakeholders was not possible in any of the proposed steps of the DTU backcasting
methodology, the work carried out by the students is considered to be representative for a participatory and
workshop-like setting. Taking a pragmatic approach allowed more time to explore variations of the methodol-
ogy, which is the primary deliverable of the master’s project. The established network of experts was further
consulted throughout the generation of the methodology to overcome diﬃculties and ﬁll persisting knowledge
gaps. In this regard a number of delimitations were taken which are discussed in Chapter 10.

==PAGE:27==
Chapter 3
Backcasting Background
This section is designed to provide information on the historic background of backcasting, which entail pro-
vides the necessary theoretical background required for the remainder of the report. It starts by explaining
backcasting, its history and how it diﬀers from forecasting as a future planning technique. This is then followed
by exploring some of the encountered prominent methods of backcasting which provide a strong foundation
to interpret alternative methodologies.
3.1
What is Backcasting?
Backcasting is a future studies technique that looks not at the predicted futures but desirable futures. It is a
change in mindset along with the bases that the most likely future is not necessarily the most desirable future.
Thus, planning for the future can be more eﬀective by thinking about what future is desirable and to determine
how it can be achieved, given the present situation. This is the principle of backcasting studies and as such
all backcasting studies start by identifying or determining a set of objectives (Börjeson et al., 2006; Quist and
Vergragt, 2006). Due to its development out of scenario analysis, this was originally done using scenarios. This
was the process of thinking of images of the future, in which the objectives of the study had been achieved
and then determining how to achieve these futures. Since 1976, backcasting studies have taken a variety of
shapes and forms with a range of diﬀerent methodologies and applications. As such to truly understand the
concept of backcasting, it is best to understand how it developed throughout the years.
3.2
History of Backcasting
Backcasting is a future studies technique which can be traced back to 1976 when used to look at energy tran-
sition in the United States of America (Lovins, 1976, 1977). Lovins is the ﬁrst known case of what was called
at the time ’backwards-looking analysis’. However, the term backcasting was coined in 1982 when Robinson
(1982) took inspiration from Lovins’ work and further developed the concept. To understand the history of
backcasting, it is often easier to start with forecasting, a timeless method.
Throughout history, the future studies and planning ﬁeld has been dominated by forecasting techniques (Brad-
ﬁeld et al., 2005). This is the concept of trying to predict what is likely to happen in the future based upon
past and present trends (Börjeson et al., 2006). This is then used to inform our actions in the present. It could
be argued that the concept of trying to predict the future is part of human nature and is something that the
human race has been trying to for millennia. Oracles and prophets being some of the earliest ’forecasters’
(Houston, 1986). Being able to predict the future was advantageous, whether it was the accident Babylonians
14
==PAGE:28==
Chapter 3. Backcasting Background
DTU Environment
trying to predict the weather back in 650BC or Aristotle in ancient Greece with his studies of meteorology
(Frisinger, 1972; Graham, 2002). Modern forecasting has been more focus on technology, economics, and nat-
ural systems; however, it can be observed in a multitude of diﬀerent disciplines. The issue with forecasting is
that the future is inherently uncertain and the majority of forecasting case studies are more often wrong than
right (Robinson, 1988; John Robinson, Personal Communication, 2020).
The longer the time horizon of a study, the harder and more uncertain is it to predict events. Thus, there was
a need for other future planning approaches. How could you get the most information to inform the future
given that it is inherently uncertain? This was the question Herman Kahn tried to answer back in the 1960s.
During the cold war period, Kahn developed the concept of scenarios and scenario techniques. Kahn’s deﬁni-
tion of a scenario as cited in Martino (1993) is:
"Scenarios are hypothetical sequences of events constructed for the purpose of focusing attention on causal processes
and decision-points. They answer two types of questions: (a) Precisely how might some hypothetical situation come
about, step by step? and (b) What alternatives exist, for each actor, at each step, for preventing, diverting, or fa-
cilitating the process?"
Scenario analysis does not always take into consideration the likelihood of a speciﬁc scenario to occur. In-
stead, they are used to map out what is possible. Since the future is uncertain the best way to plan for it is
to determine what could happen and inform planners of potential events that may occur and their impacts
(Cooke et al., 1991). This can oﬀer a more holistic approach to future planning. In the case of scenario analysis,
all possibilities of scenarios can be considered: from predictive, exploratory, desirable (normative) as well as
in-desirable. By considering how each scenario can propagate, it can deliver valuable information on how
certain actions should be acted out and others avoided. The problem with this is that there are nearly inﬁnite
possibilities for the future and to manage the resource intensity these scenarios must be generalised or speciﬁ-
cally selected. This is so to map out the entire scenario space or a particular future area of interest respectively.
What if only the desirable scenarios want to be considered? This would be beneﬁcial to determine what actions
should be done to achieve a preferable/desirable future. This is the approach Lovins and Prof. Dr. Robinson
pioneered. By excluding non-desirable scenarios a higher degree of resolution in the normative scenarios area
can be achieved. This resolution allows to gather more information on how to achieve such futures (Börjeson
et al., 2006). Although backcasting emerged out of the ﬁeld of scenario techniques, since then, it has developed
to resemble the action of aspiration towards objectives. Three key backcasting techniques can be identiﬁed:
backcasting from scenarios, backcasting from principles and participatory backcasting. Backcasting from sce-
narios is the approach Lovins and Prof. Dr. Robinson introduced and was already discussed. The use of
participatory methods with backcasting emerged in the 1990s with many studies in Canada, Sweden and the
Netherlands (Most notably the Dutch Sustainable Technological Development program) (Vergragt and Quist,
2011).
Participation is a vital component of many backcasting studies as it is essential to consult with stakeholders
before making decisions which will aﬀect their future. Contrary to participatory involvement backcasting
15
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 3. Backcasting Background
DTU Environment
studies are predominately expert lead John Holmberg, Personal Communication (2020). Participatory back-
casting refers solely to the use of participants, however, will either take a backcasting from scenarios or prin-
ciples approach. Backcasting from principles, however, deviated from the prior backcasting from scenarios
approach by negating the use of scenarios, indicating that they can limit the creativity and optimisation of
action plans into the future (Karl-Henrik Robèrt, Personal Communication, 2020). Backcasting from princi-
ples came to essence in and around 2000 as part of the Framework for Strategic Sustainable Development
(FSSD) advanced by Prof. Dr. Robèrt and Prof. Dr. Holmberg (Robèrt, 2000; Holmberg and Robèrt, 2000;
The-Natural-Step, 2017). More recent progress in the ﬁeld backcasting has looped back to Prof. Dr. Robinson.
Prof. Dr. Robinson is an advocate for participatory involvement and in his study ’Envisioning sustainability:
Recent progress in the use of participatory backcasting approaches for sustainability research’ makes a call for
what he entitles ’Second Order Backcasting’ (Robinson et al., 2011; Vergragt and Quist, 2011). Second-order
backcasting (typically from scenarios) is conducted with a high level of user input, often through the use of
interactive learning tools such as MetroQuest. The purpose of second-order backcasting is to design images
of the future through constant discussion and voting. This allows the users and participants to be invested in
the images of the future and thus creating a community vision. Although the choice of backcasting approach
may vary between studies, the use of backcasting in future studies is increasing and there is a growing shift
towards participatory involvement (Quist and Vergragt, 2006). Most modern studies encountered make use of
participatory involvement for these reasons.
3.3
Diﬀerence between Backcasting and Forecasting
To further enhance the understanding of backcasting it is relevant to understand the core distinctions to fore-
casting. As mentioned previously, forecasting studies are used to predict the future based on past and present
trends. This is then used to address problems associated with these futures. This, however, is based upon the
assumption that the future is predictable. Backcasting studies operate following the ideology that the future
is unpredictable and that likely futures are also not necessarily the most desirable. Thus, starting at the desir-
ably future and looking backwards to the present to see how it can be achieved can be an eﬀective alternative
(Quist and Vergragt, 2006; Broman and Robèrt, 2017).
Table 3.1 provides a summary of the diﬀerences between backcasting and forecasting by Dreborg (1996). This
table along with the work of Paehlke (2012) provides the basis of discussion on the diﬀerences between back-
casting and forecasting.
16
==PAGE:30==
Chapter 3. Backcasting Background
DTU Environment
Table 3.1: Comparison between forecasting and backcasting on four diﬀerent levels following (Dreborg, 1996).
Level
Forecasting
Backcasting
1. Philosophical view
- Context of justiﬁcation
- Causality determinism
- Context of discovery
- Causality and intentions
2. Perspective
- Dominant trends
- Likely futures
- Possible marginal adjustments
- How to adept to trends
- Societal problem in need of a solution
- Desirable futures
- Scope of human choice
- Retain freedom of action
3. Approach
- Extrapolate trends into the future
- Sensitivity analysis
- Deﬁne interesting futures
- Analyse consequences and conditions for
these futures in order to materialise them
4. Methods
and techniques
- Various econometric models
- Mathematical algorithms
- Partial and conditional extrapolations
- Normative models, system dynamics
models, Delphi methods, expert judgement
Dreborg compares forecasting and backcasting on four diﬀerent levels which entail (1) philosophical view, (2)
perspective, (3) approach and (4) methods and techniques. Major diﬀerences that can be highlighted are in
the diﬀerence of (1) context. Whereas forecasting is primarily used as a projective tool and as justiﬁcation
for decision making, backcasting should be considered as a tool for discovering and exploring prospective
futures. Regarding the (2) perspective of both approaches, it becomes apparent that forecasting is mainly gov-
erned by existing trends and their behaviour in the future. Comparatively, backcasting addresses current or
future societal problems rather than following trends and current developments. A further diﬀerence between
forecasting and backcasting is the consideration of diﬀerent (4) methods and techniques. Forecasting relies
mainly on the use of econometric models, meaning models based upon statistical relationships of price and
quantity, as well as the use of mathematical algorithms to describe behaviour. Following Geurs and van Wee
(2004) & Dreborg (1996) backcasting uses normative models and links important systemic aspects to assess
desirable futures.
Paehlke (2012) mentions an additional diﬀerence which was not considered by Dreborg. Following Paehlke
(2012) the role of values and therefore ethical consideration, which is not inherent in the concept of forecasting,
bares advantages in the application of the method. Following the argumentation of Höjer et al. (2008), it is
worth noting that backcasting would not be possible without the existence or use of forecasting. Without
being able to predict what is yet to come it is impossible to foresee problematic future dilemmas. To relate this
statement to a current societal and environmental problem, one could look at the matter of climate change. If
climate change was not predicted, there would be no need to mitigate the eﬀects since they would not have
been foreseen in the ﬁrst place.
17
==PAGE:31==
Chapter 3. Backcasting Background
DTU Environment
3.4
Prominent Methods
The use of backcasting methodologies change and are designed to ﬁt with the context and scope of each study
(John Robinson, Personal Communication, 2020), this became apparent when analysis the range of literature
available in the backcasting ﬁeld. However, there are a few methodologies that appear more frequently than
others. In particular, the approach developed by Prof. Dr. Robinson, the ABCD-method developed by The
Natural Step (Robèrt et al., 2002) and the use of participatory backcasting.
3.4.1
The Robinson Approach
The ﬁrst approach was put forward by Prof. Dr. Robinson in his publication from 1990 ’Futures under glass:
A recipe for people who hate to predict’. This methodology is useful for understanding subsequent methods
which were developed in the proceeding years. This work provided the groundwork for alternative method-
ologies and in some cases was directly adapted (for example seen in Geurs and van Wee (2004)). Prof. Dr.
Robinson outlined a six-step methodology which is displayed in Figure 3.1
Figure 3.1: The backcasting approach put forward by Robinson (1990) in his publication ’Futures Under Glass:
A Recipe for People Who Hate to Predict’.
Step 1: The ﬁrst step is to determine the objectives of the study. This includes three sub-steps: Describe the
purpose of the analysis; determine the spatial, temporal and substantive scope of the analysis; and determine
the number and type of scenarios that will be considered. This includes the decision on whether or not a
’Business-as-Usual’ (BAU) scenario should be considered.
18
==PAGE:32==
Chapter 3. Backcasting Background
DTU Environment
Step 2: Here one must set goals, targets and constraints for the scenario analysis as well as the exogenous
variables. Exogenous variables are factors which are outside of the scope of the system yet have a direct or
indirect eﬀect on the system and as thus are important to include in the backcasting study (Robinson, 1990).
These variables determine the background conditions for which the scenarios can be constructed. Examples
of exogenous variables could be: economic growth, demography and international relationships (Robinson,
1990; Geurs and van Wee, 2004).
Step 3: The third step is to describe the present system. Here the current consumption and production pro-
cesses must be considered. This includes the basic understanding of the system as well as the physical ﬂows
currently operating.
Step 4: In this step the speciﬁc exogenous variables are determined in quantitative terms. Furthermore, exter-
nal inputs of the scenario analysis should be identiﬁed, these include wild card and shock events. Wild-cards
are deﬁned as events with low probability yet have high impact (Barber et al., 2006). Including these into the
scenario analysis can give detailed information on resilience, vulnerability and timing.
Step 5: Once the prior steps have been concluded a scenario analysis can be conducted. Here, alternative
scenarios which adhere to the goals outlined in step 2 are formulated. First, consideration of the use of quali-
tative and quantitative approaches must be made as well as the use of modelling tools. Next, consider future
consumption and production states at both end-point and mid-point levels. The feasibility of these end-points
and mid-points assumptions then need to be assessed based upon criteria. The criteria can be based on tech-
nology, economy, environmental, social and political. For example, given a set end-point what assumptions
are needed in regards to technological progression over the scope of the study and thus, is it feasible? Next, the
scenarios can be generated. This is achieved by taking the end-point (and mid-point) assumptions made from
each of the criteria above and generating detailed descriptions of the future images. In cases where models are
utilised, this is done by inputting time-series data that will reach the end-point parameters. This step needs
iteration until internal consistency is met.
Step 6: Once the scenarios have been generated an impact assessment must be considered. The ﬁrst step
is to convey the information gathered from the prior steps in a coherent manner that is simple and informa-
tive. Next, the scenarios must be assessed in regards to the three pillars of sustainability: Social, Environmental
and Economic. This can render valuable information on plausibly of scenarios, potential impacts and political
feasibility. Once this has been considered, it is crucial to compare the scenarios with ﬁndings from step 2 to
see if they are in alignment. If this is not the case an iteration must be conducted.
Implementation: To achieve the deﬁned goals, the study must make its way into a policy-making context.
This adds levels of complexity to the study as other factors must also be considered, for example, behavioural
and institutional responses. Thus, the study is at best designed to be policy-relevant. However, after it is in
a political context a variety of policy design implementation issues may render reconsideration of vital ele-
ments, such as setting objectives. Nevertheless, the role of backcasting studies in this format help support the
decision-making process and are a useful integrated assessment tool.
19
==PAGE:33==
Chapter 3. Backcasting Background
DTU Environment
3.4.2
Participatory Backcasting
Participatory backcasting is an approach towards backcasting studies which make use of increased stakeholder
involvement. However, there is no distinct structured methodology to participatory backcasting. It should not
be seen as an alternative to backcasting from scenarios or principles but, rather as an additional element that
can further the development of backcasting studies. There are several cases where participatory backcasting
has been applied. Quist and Vergragt (2006) highlight this observation, however, states that by considering
numerous diﬀerent participatory studies, each with a variety of diﬀerent stakeholder engagements, it is pos-
sible to generalise a framework for participatory backcasting. This generalisation can be structured into 5
steps. It is important to mention that this is a generalisation and participatory backcasting studies (as well as
scenario and principle studies) vary from case to case. Nevertheless, the 5 steps are stated as:
1. Strategic problem orientation
2. Construction of sustainable future visions or scenarios
3. Backcasting (here the term backcasting is being used in the context of scenario generation using back-
casting techniques)
4. Elaboration, analysis and deﬁning follow-up and (action) agenda
5. Embedding of results and generating follow-up and implementation
The use of stakeholder consultation for vision setting has been heavily empathised, in many case studies, as
it is vital to consider that often stakeholders have diﬀerent deﬁnitions of sustainability. The speciﬁc use of
using participants during the vision generation is deﬁned as second-order backcasting (Robinson et al., 2011).
However, in many cases, desirable futures are already described and thus, stakeholders play a roll of deter-
mining how this future can be achieved. Participation is a tool that can be utilised throughout all backcasting
study, not just during the future visioning and generation. Quist and Vergragt (2006) goes on to state that in
many participatory backcasting studies there may be additional or alternative steps, however, they will still
follow the generalised approach outlined above. Furthermore, this process is presented linearly, however it
is dynamic and thus, can involve multiple iterations. Here, four types of stakeholders have been categorised:
companies, government and public interest groups, research bodies and the public. Similarly in another study
Kok et al. (2006) categories four stakeholder groups as business representatives, policymakers, citizens and
experts.
Another important element of participatory backcasting is the tools and methods used to engage the stake-
holders and guide workshops. There are a variety of diﬀerent approaches, which oﬀer a range of beneﬁts and
make use of diﬀerent environments and techniques to hold workshops. Other considerations of participatory
backcasting studies are the organisation and management tools for running an eﬀective stakeholder network.
This is vital for ﬂuid communication. The approaches may also vary depending on the stage of consultation
as well as the diﬀerent types of stakeholders that are being engaged.
20
==PAGE:34==
Chapter 3. Backcasting Background
DTU Environment
3.4.3
The ABCD-Methodology
The ABCD-methodology emerged as a backcasting tool out of the FSSD and the work from The Natural Step. It
is a four-step methodology consisting of: Awareness, Baseline assessment, Creative solutions, Devise a plan.
The information on the ABCD-methodology described in the following section gained from TheNaturalStep-
Canada (2020a) as well as relevant authors in the ﬁeld including Herbertson and Tipler (2008); Nathan (2018).
It is worth mentioning that variations of the ABCD-methodology were observed amongst institutions and
sources (John Holmberg, Personal Communication, 2020). The following description is a shortened sum-
marised version of the ABCD-methodology used in The Natural Step Canada, which was chosen due to its
easily comprehensible description and suﬃcient instructions.
The ﬁrst step ’awareness’ prompts for a vision of the future to be constructed, i.e. out of principles what is
the desirable future? Thus, consideration of scenarios is not studied in this method. Instead, basic principles
of sustainability must be designed and a criterion of success can be deﬁned, these criteria can be viewed as
system conditions. In the FSSD these principles of sustainability and system conditions are already deﬁned
and can be seen in Table 3.2. This visioning process is dependent on the topic of backcasting and may vary
depending on the context of the future.
Table 3.2: System conditions and corresponding sustainability principles used in the ’framework for strategic
sustainable development’ and thus, often used to guild step A of the ABCD backcasting methodology develop
by The Natural Step (TheNaturalStep-Canada, 2020b).
System Conditions
Corresponding Sustainability Principles
1. concentrations of substances extracted from
the earth’s crust
1. the systematic increase of concentrations of substances
extracted from the Earth’s crust (for example, heavy met-
als and fossil fuels)
2. concentrations of substances produced by so-
ciety
2. the systematic increase of concentrations of substances
produced by society (for example, plastics, dioxins, PCBs
- polychlorinated-biphenyl and DDT - dichlorodiphenyl-
trichloroethane)
3. degradation by physical means
3. the systematic physical degradation of nature and nat-
ural processes (for example, over harvesting forests, de-
stroying habitat and overﬁshing)
4. people are not subject to conditions that sys-
temically undermine their capacity to meet their
needs
4. conditions that systematically undermine people’s ca-
pacity to meet their basic human needs (for example, un-
safe working conditions and not enough pay to live on).
The next step is to understand the baseline situation. The vision for the prior step can be used to conduct
a gap analysis between the current state and the success criteria. Herbertson and Tipler (2008) describe the
gap analysis as providing creative tension for innovation. However, it helps to identify critical sustainability
issues and leverage points in the system. This then feeds into step 3 of the ABCD-methodology.
21
==PAGE:35==
Chapter 3. Backcasting Background
DTU Environment
In the third step of the ABCD-methodology, brainstorming exercises are utilised to determine creative solu-
tions for the problems indicated by the baseline analysis, making use of the leverage points and major issues
identiﬁed. These solutions have the intention of working towards attaining the visions in step 1 of the method-
ology. Backcasting from principles and as such, the ABCD-methodology does not refer to the term ’pathway’.
Backcasting from principles typically refrains from constructing pathways, which is commonly referred to in
backcasting from scenarios (John Holmberg, Personal Communication, 2020).
The ﬁnal step of the methodology prompts the users to decide on the best available option to guide towards
the vision, taking the solutions identiﬁed prior. This step looks at identify immediate solutions to form a path
towards the vision. A full pathway is not considered in this methodology. The intention when prioritising
solutions is to determine the most eﬀective and eﬃcient path, whilst paying attention to being ﬂexible and
maximising economic, social and ecological returns. This is done by starting with easy implementation, high
return solutions. However, it is not expected that the long-term vision is achieved immediately and as such
focus should be on the systemic short-term eﬀorts, which lead to the overall long-term perspective by creating
ﬂexible platforms towards the future. Using backcasting continuously to assess the solutions are making gains
in the right direction. The principles of sustainability are then further used to ensure that the actions are
moving in the right direction. This is a key element of the ABCD-methodology and the FSSD, stating "What is
considered realistic today never determines the direction of change, only its pace" (TheNaturalStep-Canada,
2020a). The future is ambiguous yet the use of sustainability principles will be constant and can ﬁt with the
ever-changing dynamics of future planning.
22
==PAGE:36==
Chapter 4
Teaching-Based Research Activity
The outcomes and results of the course "12240 - Environmental Management and Ethics" are presented in the
following sections. The main focus of results lays on the energy sector due to its importance in the context of
this thesis and the later examples in chapter 8. Due to the complexity of received deliveries and variations in
approaches taken by the students, no cohesive comparison can be presented. Rather a summarised presenta-
tion of the results is made, including the reﬂections by the students gathered from their reports at the end of
this chapter.
4.1
Results from Teaching-Based Research Activity
The results gathered from the teaching-based research activity can be presented by following each of the
delivery requirements in the context of the ABCD backcasting methodology. Therefore, the following section
is structured according to the basic steps of the ABCD-methodology. As mentioned before the main focus of
this chapter lies in steps C & D as these represent the core of the teaching-based research activity.
4.1.1
Step A
The results of the ﬁrst part of the political memo, which touches upon steps A & B of the ABCD-methodology,
are presented brieﬂy as they are less relevant due to the circumstance that the students were provided with
the main literature to satisfy this aspect of the backcasting approach. It can be stated that most groups have
adopted provided goals and vision for each sector and presented them well. It is remarkable that a hand full
of groups additionally introduced the Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) by the United Nations and in
some cases even the Paris Agreement to actively address climate change. The addition of these goals was
not required or encouraged by the supervisors, however, showed that the students took on a more holistic
perspective with a focus on sustainability and social dimensions. In regards to the later sections of the students’
work it can be said that the choice of these additional targets did not involve consideration of trade-oﬀs and
implications towards these goals, nor did the assessment of pathways ﬁnd consideration of these additional
goals.
4.1.2
Step B
Similar to the above-mentioned ﬁndings for step A, step B of the ABCD-methodology was carried out ade-
quately. The provided literature was well analysed and additional literature provided where deemed relevant.
The information presented on the current state varied regarding the category and the quality of data. Here,
ﬁrst deviations between groups and thereby impacts on the later steps of the ABCD-methodology could be
23
==PAGE:37==
Chapter 4. Teaching-Based Research Activity
DTU Environment
observed. Several groups have missed presenting core numeric values such as greenhouse gas (GHG) emis-
sions in the respective sector and instead relied on a qualitative description of the current state. Other groups
focused heavily on quantitative data which then allowed to draw a comparison to the target year of 2050 by
comparing in a business-as-usual manner. This basic logical procedure was necessary to subsequently ap-
proach step C - the identiﬁcation of solutions and generation of pathways. Without knowing the gap that
needs to be bridged between now and the future, the pathway generation is diﬃcult to comprehend. Regard-
ing the category of data that students chose to elaborate on, it can be said that only a few groups attempted
to connect relevant information across sectors. Thereby, the opportunity to identify trans-systemic relation-
ships, for example, impacts by changes in the energy sector on to the mobility sector were left aside. Further,
it was observed that data on societal factors such as access to mobility concerning social status was rarely
considered by the students. Therefore, it can be concluded that the analysis of the current state is of major
importance as it inﬂuences the available information and thereby, the way pathways will be formulated in the
following step.
4.1.3
Step C
To reiterate, the generation of pathways in step C does not conform with typical ABCD instructions. However,
this element was introduced to investigate which possible concepts and approaches the student’s engineer.
Therefore, several groups relied in some sort or form on backcasting from scenarios. Table 4.1 shows a list of
pathways for each of the 6 energy case studies. Similar to the results as displayed in the table, all 20 groups
worked by creating some sort of pathway category or scenario theme. Four basic diﬀerent approaches were
identiﬁed amongst the students:
• Theme based on the choice of political measure (soft to hard law)
• Theme based on the choice of political scale (top-down vs bottom-up)
• Theme based on categories (i.e. management, lifestyle (including ethics) and technology)
• Unconventional scenarios (e.g. Deep Ecology ethical approach by Group 17 (Pérot et al., 2020))
Partly, these themes were combined to generate cohesion between the social and political dimensions. As an
example, information campaigns were chosen to inform the citizens on certain topics such as energy savings
or emissions in the transport sector to spark behavioural changes. The information campaigns were combined
with soft law as to support and implement this approach from an e.g. top-down perspective.
Many groups took a rather simplistic approach to generate scenarios. A scenario dominated by e.g. technol-
ogy or strict policy measures did not necessarily consider societal changes that could occur in that time or
promising solutions that were ignored for the sake of the scenario. These dominant scenarios were rarely
recommended in the ﬁnal part of the delivery as it is questionable whether such scenarios achieve the set
objectives, nor if the chosen pathways are preferable. Rather, a combination scenario was built as the ﬁnal
scenario. Such an example is the third pathway of Group 4 (Holmgaard et al., 2020), see Table 4.1. The reason
for this choice according to some of the groups was that the combination scenarios provide a more realis-
tic image of the future, rather than one dominating scenario. Most groups that recommended dominating
24
==PAGE:38==
Chapter 4. Teaching-Based Research Activity
DTU Environment
scenarios only generated generic scenarios which limited the choice of scenarios for the recommendation in
the following step of the method. Similarly to choosing dominating scenario themes, several groups focused
on single solutions to be at the core of their pathway generation, e.g. genetically modiﬁed organisms in the
agricultural sector or the use of taxation as the only political measure on meat. These chosen solutions were
then supported by additional measures and actions to enhance the performance of the selected solution.
