Though most groups did not elaborate on their approach to generating pathways, analysing the structure of
their work allows a number of insights to be deducted. Groups 4 & 19 generated pathways based on set mile-
stones which were based on identiﬁed targets (Holmgaard et al., 2020; Kristjánsdóttir et al., 2020). Therefore,
the students generated intermediate targets and based the decision of actions and implementation options
according to meet the set intermediate target indicators. In comparison Group 13, investigating the EU trans-
port system, based the construction of pathways on relevant sectors for their chosen system (Frederikke Linulf
et al., 2020). The students divided the pathway into sectors such as rail, road, maritime and aviation to gener-
ate pathways and display the results. In the work of Group 20, it could be observed that a mix of previously
presented approach was applied (Nguyen et al., 2020). To generate pathways they addressed diﬀerent targets
and sectors of the agriculture system separately, as in: ’GHG-emissions’; ’bio-chemical ﬂows’; ’biodiversity’;
’land-system change’; ’diet’; and ’food waste’. Thereby, the students were able to identify speciﬁc action to
related categories and provide structure to the pathway generation. The approach taken by Group 6 is worth
mentioning in the context of generating pathways (Binderup et al., 2020). It included the idea of a ’solution
bank’ which contains descriptions and explanations of solutions found in the students’ work. This list of so-
25
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 4. Teaching-Based Research Activity
DTU Environment
lutions is then used to formulate pathways according to chosen scenarios. The students further assessed in
a qualitative way how each solution contributes to the diﬀerent goals and vision of their system. This idea
was adopted in the later development of the DTU backcasting methodology (see section 8) and allowed to
categorise and present the results from all 6 energy case studies in Figure 4.1. The solutions gathered from the
students’ deliveries were streamlined, duplicates deleted and categorised according to 13 diﬀerent categories.
These 13 categories were drawn from the reoccurring topics and themes related to the solutions, similar to
the approach taken by Doyle and Davies (2013) in presenting their backcasting workshop results. A full list
of all 101 solution elements can be found in section A.1 - Appendix.
Figure 4.1: Overview of number of solutions that fall within 13 diﬀerent categories. The overview excluded
duplicates or very similar solutions that were streamlined and grouped in the process of presenting the results.
The assessment carried out by the students by using taught decision-support tools such as stakeholder analy-
sis, DPSIR and cost-beneﬁt analysis showed varying degrees of quality. The biggest lack of assessment could
be observed in terms of economics. Those groups that attempted an economical analysis of a pathway ad-
hered to qualitative data instead of assigning monetary values to pathway elements. A possible explanation
for this lack of economic assessment was found in the reﬂection provided by the students (see section 4.2).
In general, the link between conducted assessment and argumentation delivered in the report is rather weak.
It was observed that the provided arguments were partially subjective and not well referenced or backed up
with adequate literature.
Also noticeable in terms of pathway generation is that out of 20 groups only 4 chose to generate 4 instead
of 3 (minimum requirement) pathways. This could be due to the time constraints encountered during the
work. Further, the groups struggled to include the ideas presented in the course regarding resource nexus
26
==PAGE:40==
Chapter 4. Teaching-Based Research Activity
DTU Environment
and system thinking to approach more holistic pathways. In general, it can be said that the consideration of
trans-systemic relations, trade-oﬀs and impacts were rarely if at all considered.
Lastly, it was observed that the formulation of pathways diﬀered across the diﬀerent groups in terms of the
quality and formulation of pathways. Even though an action plan format was provided to the students most
groups documented the details of a pathway in a loose manner and to a low level of quality. It was observed that
the formulation of pathways based on solutions included two levels of information. Several groups structured
pathways by providing information on what has to be changed, introduced or done to achieve a certain goal.
However, the students did not explain how these elements could be translated into reality. The ’how’ of the
implementation, meaning the associated political measures were missing. This can also be seen in Figure
4.1. It includes speciﬁc solutions for tackling diﬀerent categories without being speciﬁc enough about related
policy measures. The category with the most entries was the category of ’policy measures’, which includes
speciﬁcally designated political measures to act out. Further relevant information for the action plan about
responsibilities, scales of activities and how to assess if an action is successful was not considered by the
students to a suﬃcient level.
4.1.4
Step D
In the ﬁnal part of the assignment, the students were asked to give a recommendation of one pathway based
upon their prior assessment and comparison. As seen in the reﬂections by one of the groups (see section 4.2)
this aspect caused diﬃculties. A common approach taken was to compare pathways in regards to the three
assessment criteria in a qualitative way. Another approach applied by several groups was a ranking system,
where based upon the group’s opinion scores were assigned to the diﬀerent criteria for each pathway. An
example of such a comparison can be seen in Figure 4.2, taken from Group 13 which additionally included an
element of (un-)certainty in their comparison.
Figure 4.2: Ranking approach to prioritise pathways according to four criteria based on a scale from 1 to 3.
The approach is taken from Group 13 which additionally included the criteria of certainty (Frederikke Linulf
et al., 2020).
The argumentation in favour of a pathway was predominantly based on the prior conducted analysis and
comparison of designed pathways. However, several groups provided further reﬂections and reasons for their
recommendation. Several cases have recommended a certain pathway based on their perception. These per-
ceptions reﬂected upon whether a pathway was holistic enough to resemble a realistic future image. As
mentioned before, it was observed that combination scenarios and pathways were preferred in comparison
to pathways which were dominated by single scenario themes. It was argued against pathways that solely
27
==PAGE:41==
Chapter 4. Teaching-Based Research Activity
DTU Environment
relied on top-down or bottom-up approaches. Further, social and environmental pathways were chosen above
economically beneﬁcial pathways. However, it can be argued that the aspect of the economy was not well
represented in the students’ work as outlined prior.
4.2
Reﬂections on Teaching-Based Research Activity
As partly mentioned in the prior result section issues could be observed with the use of backcasting. The
following section elaborates on encountered diﬃculties and comments from the participants of the teaching-
based research activity. The 80 students who participated in the course "12240 - Environmental Management
and Ethics" were asked to provide optional feedback on the use of backcasting as part of their assignment.
Additionally, an online survey was opened for students to provide additional feedback if wanted. Besides the
additional feedback, in total 8 reﬂections from the deliverable were obtained which are summarised in the
following. A full list of provided feedback from the students can be seen in section A.2 - Appendix:
• The most prominent comment relates to the lack of guidance on how to generate pathways in step
C of the methodology. It became apparent that the students would have liked more information and
instructions on this section of the work. The formulation of pathways was closely linked to the consid-
eration of trade-oﬀs between solutions and elements of a pathway. One group stated that they found it
very complex and diﬃcult to assess these trade-oﬀs within a pathway, whilst simultaneously trying to
explore possible trade-oﬀs across boundaries of a system.
• One group mentioned in their feedback that the work on complex systems in a group-work manner was
beneﬁcial to generate in their opinion more holistic pathways. Following their comment, it was possible
to include diﬀerent perspectives on the topic and thereby to account for a multitude of diﬀerent opinions.
The same group also argued that this element occasionally took the discussion away from the actual
system in focus and lead to an element of distraction and deviation from addressing the actual system.
• It was mentioned by one group that they had wished for better communication with experts (in this case
the teachers of the course) to identify solutions and to formulate pathways. This highlights the need to
involve multiple groups of stakeholders in a participatory setting, whilst providing guidance and points
for reﬂections by a supervising body. It was gathered that in the absence of expert involvement many
of the groups engaged in shared dialogue.
• It became apparent that the students were unsure about the validity of conducted analysis by the use of
decision-support tools. One group doubted the quality of quantitative analysis as they identiﬁed diﬃ-
culties with applying cost-beneﬁt analysis across the three criteria: social, environmental and economic.
They argue that it is not easy to assign a monetary value to societal services or goods in a transparent
and just way. One group mentioned that they felt uncomfortable to make judgements about the as-
sessment of pathways as they believe that suﬃcient expertise and knowledge has to be linked to this
step.
• It was suggested by one group that the pathways need to consider an element of robustness. In their
28
==PAGE:42==
Chapter 4. Teaching-Based Research Activity
DTU Environment
opinion using backcasting has to consider extreme events ranging from a change in political leadership,
environmental catastrophes to scenarios of war.
• The feedback of one group showed that they considered it important and at the same time very diﬃcult
to assess the preference of stakeholders to diﬀerent pathways. The students believe that it is diﬃcult to
assume reactions from diﬀerent systemic and societal levels. One of the survey participants suggested
to include the possibility to approach the public and to reﬂect on their response to designed pathways.
Thereby, it could be assessed whether suggested pathways would ﬁnd recognition amongst the public
and ultimately in a political context. Further, it was argued that opinions and perspectives can change.
Especially, business-orientated entities might not be able to agree on long-term visions or that they
might deviate from agreed visions due to economic interests.
• The role of institutions was reﬂected upon by one group. The group argues that the pathways need to
withstand changes in political leadership to retain validity until the target year. Further, they argue,
that generating pathways should also consider an element of institutional change as the structure of
democracy and institutions might be subject to change in the future as well.
• It was mentioned by one group that they believe that there could be a threat of subjective pathway
generation and thereby causing a biased view towards the future. They argue that the participants
or experts involved in the pathway generation should follow objective guidelines on how to generate
pathways.
• One group elaborated on their case study, stating that the comparison of constructed pathways caused
diﬃculties. They drew the cause of these diﬃculties back to the inherent diﬀerences of designed path-
ways which were impossible to compare in all aspects. They state that the use of sustainability principles
and the criteria required by the curriculum were helpful, however, the complexity and broadness of path-
ways complicated the comparison. The students argue that there should be a fundamental consideration
of how to compare pathways according to a previously determined set of rules and boundaries.
• The comments and reﬂections on the use of backcasting as a method also included suggestions for im-
provement of the method as such. Two groups mentioned that they consider an element of monitoring
or evaluation of progress towards the set vision to be necessary to assure that pathways are veriﬁed pe-
riodically. Another group suggested to elaborate on the following questions in the context of analysing
the current state: "For what reason(s) has the current system become as it is?; What reasoning was
behind its implementation?", "What advantages does it oﬀer?", and "What will happen if the system is
changed or removed?". In their opinion, this will help to identify previous problems that might reoccur
if changes to a system are made.
• As positive feedback, it was agreed amongst most groups that backcasting is a fruitful approach in
tackling the challenges of the future. The students welcomed the creativity linked to backcasting and
considered the exercise as beneﬁcial in terms of learning as well as developing skills in discussion and
argumentation.
Some comments by the students can be related to the teaching in the course "12240 - Environmental Manage-
ment and Ethics". These comments relate to two main elements: time constraints and teaching of backcasting
29
==PAGE:43==
Chapter 4. Teaching-Based Research Activity
DTU Environment
as a method. Time requirements to conduct a cohesive study were considered high by the students while they
simultaneously were receiving and processing lectures on additional topics. In the context of a three-week
course, the curriculum seemed to overwhelm students and thereby valuable time was lost due to the amount
of work in preparing the deliverable. One group of students argued that they saw most of the time needed
for conducting the assessment of pathways. As a consequence and in respect to time constraints the level of
quality in the assessment of pathways varied according to one group of students. In regards to the teaching
of the ABCD-methodology, two of the groups suggested introducing real-world examples of a complete back-
casting study or of individual elements to elaborate on the structure and to enhance the understanding of the
methodology. Examples of studies were provided in the class, however, besides the supervision and ongoing
dialogue between students and teachers, no speciﬁc examples were presented in the plenum. Further, it was
suggested to enhance the understanding of decision-support tools as taught in the course (stakeholder analy-
sis, cost-beneﬁt analysis and DPSIR) by relating taught tools to a backcasting study.
30
==PAGE:44==
Chapter 5
Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in
Environmental Management
In this chapter, the results of the literature and case study review will be presented. This includes a summary
of ﬁndings regarding the conducted literature review followed by a comparison of reviewed case studies and
an in-depth review of ﬁve case studies. These ﬁve case studies are highlighting speciﬁc methodologies as
well as how and in which context they are applied. Additionally, structural elements and applied tools will be
drawn upon and highlighted in cases where they were deemed relevant for this thesis. Each case study diﬀers
in regards to scope and context and thus, oﬀers insight into, how a successful method can be determined for
an EU long-term policy framework backcasting approach.
5.1
Results from Literature & Case Study Review
As described in Chapter 2, a literature review was conducted out of which a selection of case studies ﬁltered
into a review of applied backcasting methodologies. Figure 5.1 provides an overview of available literature
on ’Web of Science’ concerning applied keyword searches and ﬁlter setting. The ﬁlters applied are searched
by ’title’, ’topic’ or a combination of ’topic’ and ’environmental science’. The results are categorised into: (a)
Future techniques ﬁeld; (b) Backcasting ﬁeld; (c) Scenarios ﬁeld; and (d) Additional literature topics.
Looking at Figure 5.1 (a), it becomes apparent that the available literature on backcasting is less represented
than for example forecasting or scenario analysis. Comparing the results for searching by topic, backcasting
only yields 372 compared to 180,328 hits for forecasting. This becomes even more remarkable when inves-
tigating literature on backcasting within the environmental science ﬁeld. Here, web of science generated a
total of 68 hits. Thereby, it becomes clear that research on backcasting within an environmental context is still
relatively unexplored. The same conclusion can be generated when looking at Figure 5.1 (c). The term nor-
mative scenarios yield a considerable small number, i.e. 933 in comparison to predictive scenarios with 7,656
hits. 5.1 (b) displays the relation between search words on diﬀerent typologies of backcasting as presented in
Chapter 3. The term ’backcasting scenarios’ yielded the most literature in the context of backcasting, in total
254. Part (d) of Figure 5.1 represents additional research topics that were investigated during the conduction
of this thesis.
31
==PAGE:45==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
(a) Future techniques ﬁeld.
(b) Backcasting ﬁeld.
(c) Scenarios ﬁeld.
(d) Additional literature topics.
Figure 5.1: The quantity of available literature based oﬀof keyword searches on ’Web of Science’ for many
diﬀerent ﬁelds indicated by the diﬀerent sub-ﬁgures. The quantity of the literature available is dependant
on the ﬁltering settings used in web of science. Thus, for each keyword search the literature was reﬁned
into three categories: Overall topic, titles containing the keyword and overall topic reﬁned to studies in the
’environmental science’ ﬁeld. The keyword searches can be grouped into four categories. (a) Future techniques
including backcasting, forecasting, scenario analysis and foresight thinking. (b) The ﬁeld of backcasting is
divided into the three main types being, backcasting from scenarios, principles and participatory backcasting.
(c) The scenario space and types of scenarios are divided into exploratory, predictive and normative. (d)
Additional literature that was explored to further the knowledge required to understand relevant tools to aid
the DTU backcasting methodology.
5.2
Overview of Reviewed Case Studies
In the following, an overview of reviewed case studies is presented. Information that was considered relates
to context; scope; country of study; applied method; and how successful the studies were. The information
displayed under "Outcome/Implementation" was gained either through available documentation in the case
study or by contacting the involved authors. A full list of authors that were contacted for this purpose can
be found in Table 4 - Appendix. Case studies reviewed and mentioned in the following are exclusively in the
32
==PAGE:46==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
English language. Thereby several national and local case studies might not be covered. Table 5.2 presents
an overview of all reviewed case studies, displayed in chronological order. In total, 15 case studies were
reviewed. The backcasting methods applied in the diﬀerent case studies show a range of backcasting methods,
which originated from the original Robinson concept and/or subsequent method (Robinson, 1982, 1990). 12
case studies were based on a backcasting from scenarios approach, whereas a total of 2 case studies can be
categorised by using backcasting from principles. Out of 15 case studies, 12 case studies refer to the use of
participatory methods.
Context
Looking at the context of reviewed cases, it becomes apparent that the applications addressed a broad range
of diﬀerent sectors and topics. This is remarkable as it shows the ﬂexibility of the underlying backcasting
approach. The topics of conducted backcasting studies include energy, transport, housing, agriculture and
city planning. It can be stated from presented tables that studies on energy (4), transport (4) and agriculture
(3) are forming the majority of case studies in the environmental ﬁeld.
Scope
In terms of scope, backcasting has been applied in the reviewed case studies for local and regional planning for
municipalities. It further found application in a national or international governmental context. Out of 15 case
studies, 3 cases applied backcasting outside of Europe. In total two studies were observed targeting a European
scale (Schippl and Leisner, 2009; Giaoutzi et al., 2012). Most of the remaining case studies applied backcasting
in a local, regional or national context in their country. During the literature review, it was observed that the
ABCD-methodology and the FSSD approach was commonly applied in businesses, examples of which have
not been included in the review (Göran Broman, Personal Communication, 2020; Damsø et al., 2014). Prof.
Dr. John Holmberg supported the point that these concepts can be applied on a wider geographical scale,
e.g. national backcasting study (John Holmberg, Personal Communication, 2020). In conversation with Asst.
Prof. Dr. Kristjan Jespersen it became apparent that the concept of backcasting is widely applied in the Asian
research community, especially around Japan (Kristjan Jespersen, Personal Communication, 2020). Asst. Prof.
Dr. Kok mentioned that in his opinion a lot of practical knowledge about backcasting is held by businesses
and can, if at all, only be found in grey literature (Kasper Kok, Personal Communication, 2020). However, in
the context of this thesis, a delimitation from such literature was taken. Further outreach and study review
are therefore considered vital to expand the knowledge on existing backcasting approaches.
Method
The encountered backcasting methodologies from reviewed case studies range from the very detailed descrip-
tion and step-wise procedure to little or no structural information in the case study. Therefore, in an attempt to
compare this broad range of encountered methodologies a summarised analysis is presented in the following.
Section 5.3 elaborates in-depth on 5 selected case studies regarding encountered methods that were deemed
relevant for the backcasting methodology in the context of policy-support in the European Union.
33
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
The description of backcasting methods was presented in diagrams, text or reference to previously established
methods (such as the ABCD-methodology or the Robinson approach, see section 3). Nearly all reviewed case
studies apply a visioning step as the ﬁrst element of their methodology. The visioning step was performed as a
main characterising element of a backcasting study as it provides the objectives and target setting element in
the method. Many cases addressed a problem in the ﬁrst step of their methodology which thereby included a
problem deﬁnition and analysis (Schippl and Leisner, 2009; Svenfelt et al., 2011; Giurco et al., 2011; Sisto et al.,
2018; Musse et al., 2018). The outcome of this step however can be linked back to the formation of a vision
or objectives in the same or a second step (Musse et al., 2018; Sisto et al., 2018). The approach was taken by
the diﬀerent case studies varied, as e.g. including expert and stakeholder groups in a participatory manner
to deﬁne the vision or adopting existing goals and targets from external organisations (Geurs and van Wee,
2004; Robinson et al., 2011). Giurco et al. (2011), Giaoutzi et al. (2012) and Sisto et al. (2018) apply an element
of system analysis as their ﬁrst step which created awareness and thereby helped inform the generation of a
vision and objectives. This element of analysing the current state could be observed as generally speaking the
second step of most encountered backcasting studies (Schippl and Leisner, 2009; Svenfelt et al., 2011; Damsø
et al., 2014). Geurs and van Wee (2004), Schippl and Leisner (2009), Svenfelt et al. (2011), Giaoutzi et al. (2012)
and Soria-Lara and Banister (2018) considered the conduction of a business-as-usual or baseline scenario as
beneﬁcial to identify the gap of change in comparison to established vision at this point of the method. In
general, it can be said that the third step would involve the establishment of normative future images (Schippl
and Leisner, 2009; Svenfelt et al., 2011; Giurco et al., 2011; Doyle and Davies, 2013; Damsø et al., 2014). Some
case studies utilised future images directly in combination with setting the vision and objectives of the study
(e.g. Musse et al. (2018); Sisto et al. (2018)). The description of desirable future images was done in silo work by
experts or by either approaching or including diﬀerent stakeholders to partake in the formulation. The infor-
mation available for constructing future images was scarce. Out of 15 case studies, only Svenfelt et al. (2011),
Robinson et al. (2011) and Doyle and Davies (2013) provide a detailed description of a future image (details in
section B.1 - Appendix). As an example, the future image described in Svenfelt et al. (2011) is heavily based on
technical details, heavily supported by numbers. However, descriptive information is available about diﬀerent
aspects of the future, i.e. role of professionals or management of rental spaces as can be seen in the following
box:
34
==PAGE:48==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
[Unedited] Future image (a): the building sector as a driving force for progress - Svenfelt et al. (2011).
This image focuses on what can be accomplished via technical solutions and changes in infrastructure to
enhance energy performance. Those who plan, build and manage buildings professionally have acquired knowl-
edge about energy-eﬃcient solutions. There are means of control and incentives that accomplish energy-eﬃcient
construction and renovation of the building stock; the buildings are equipped with energy-eﬃcient technological
solutions and are managed in ways that utilise the possibilities oﬀered by technology. Newly-built single houses
use 30 kWh/m2 and existing renovated houses 90 kWh/m2 for heating (current level 155 kWh/m2), plus household
electricity at 30 kWh/m2 per household (current level 46 kWh/m2). Property management is very ﬂexible so that
the heated space can be adjusted following the current demand. Oﬃce and house space are smaller, and instead,
additional space can be rented when needed. The changes do not to any great extent inﬂuence people’s everyday
life. They do not have to be conscious of their energy use, as all available appliances are energy-eﬃcient. Flexible,
smart homes enable people to base more work and free time in the home, enabling more eﬃcient use of waste heat
from people and electrical devices.
The ﬁnal commonly observable structural element in reviewed case studies relates to the achievement of de-
signed images or set visions and objectives. The information available on this component of the backcasting
methods is scarce and did not receive comprehensible description and instructions. The discussion of how to
achieve designed visions and set objectives was conducted in workshops through brainstorming tools (Schippl
and Leisner, 2009; Svenfelt et al., 2011; Doyle and Davies, 2013; Sisto et al., 2016; Soria-Lara and Banister, 2018;
Sisto et al., 2018) or use of modelling tools (Robinson et al., 2011; Musse et al., 2018). Again this work was
carried out in cooperation with experts and stakeholders or ’in-house’.
The participation of stakeholder in reviewed case studies varied depending on the type of stakeholder who
was involved as well as where in the method they were considered relevant for the backcasting application.
Table 5.1 presents an overview of involved stakeholders. The considered participants stretch across diﬀerent
stakeholder groups including the public, researchers, business representatives, politicians and representatives
of other social entities (such as NGOs). Across reviewed case studies it was visible that the term ’stakeholder’
held diﬀerent implications for the study. For example, the group ’public’ refers to a generic and broad group of
the society, which simultaneously can include (amongst others) residents as well as employees of a company.
However, it became apparent that the selection of stakeholders was based on the underlying purpose of the
backcasting application. As an example, the case study by Sisto et al. (2018) applied backcasting in a local
context for rural development strategies. This is reﬂected in the choice of participants that contributed to
the case study as local public institutions as well as important private parties were involved (see Table 5.1).
The consideration of where participants were involved in the backcasting process varied across case studies
and in part was not provided with suﬃcient information. It was observed in several case studies that the
participation of stakeholders was integrated across the whole of the backcasting study (Broman et al., 2013;
Sisto et al., 2016, 2018; Musse et al., 2018). The involvement of the public in the visioning step of a backcast was
adapted by four examples (Robinson et al., 2011; Giurco et al., 2011; Svenfelt et al., 2011; Doyle and Davies,
2013). Besides these case studies, the involvement of the public was not considered at an individual level,
rather in the form of representative institutions (Sisto et al., 2018). Robinson et al. (2011) especially apply
scenario gaming apps as a tool to engage with citizens in the scenario evaluation process. This concept can
35
==PAGE:49==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
be utilised to provide valuable information to the public on topics as sustainability trade-oﬀs of scenarios.
Robinson et al. (2011) and Giurco et al. (2011) speciﬁcally argue for the involvement of the public and diverse
business representatives in the generation of future images as their view on a normative future is relevant
for the subsequent methodological steps and outcomes of the backcast. The involvement of politicians was
observed in the context of policy development (Sisto et al., 2018; Soria-Lara and Banister, 2018; Musse et al.,
2018). The case study by Soria-Lara and Banister (2018) involved politicians to form policy packages and in
the following to appraise the generated packages in the form of a participatory multi-criteria analysis. The
consideration of system relevant entities from the ﬁnancial sector was only observed in Sisto et al. (2016).

DTU Environment
At this point several relevant methodological elements can be drawn from several reviewed case studies (ex-
cluding relevant case studies from section 5.3):
• Damsø et al. (2014) apply an additional methodological step ’transitional preconditions’ in the context of
an energy transition to eliminate solution elements that will not achieve the normative future designed
in the following steps. Thereby, a basic understanding of what elements will not contribute to achieving
the targets is established.
• Regarding the use of future images, it was apparent that only three case studies provided detailed images
of the future (Svenfelt et al., 2011; Robinson et al., 2011; Doyle and Davies, 2013), which are attached
as examples in section B.1 - Appendix. All three case studies provide insight on how to generate these
future images and what diﬀerent tools and ways of presenting them could entail. Svenfelt et al. (2011)
provide a rather technical view on energy consumption in buildings. Thereby, the case study produced
images that rely on values related to the energy sector as well as a description of the investigated system.
Robinson et al. (2011) present the use of models to depict the sea level rise of certain futures based on
their climate impacts. This visual way of depicting a future image can be powerful to convey a message
to receivers and participants and thereby, inﬂuence their preference towards the respective future image.
Lastly, Doyle and Davies (2013) present personal stories of how a scene would look like in the future by
including a subject in the narration. The inclusion of a character creates familiarity between the future
image and the participants of the backcasting study. Further, the authors include sketches and drawings
of relevant elements that are present in the future image, which increases the level of detail that can be
deducted from the future image.
• Doyle and Davies (2013); Sisto et al. (2016) provide a detailed description of their approaches to partici-
patory backcasting which deviates from the above presented methodological outline. Both case studies
provide a framework for participatory workshop-led social learning activities amongst stakeholders.
Both case studies as well as Robinson et al. (2011) utilise the tools of a survey, email questionnaires and
online-based media.
• Kanter et al. (2016) provide an indicator-based backcasting approach, which was not observed in any
other case study. Choosing indicator values and setting quantitative targets as end-points is the ﬁrst
element of the approach by Kanter et al. (2016). In a subsequent step, these indicator targets are then
compared with the current indicator levels, by which a gap can be identiﬁed. The backcasting element
then addresses the generation of intermediate targets by working backwards from set future indicator
levels. The development of pathways is guided by this established set of indicators from current levels
to desired future levels. The authors do not elaborate speciﬁcally on the instructions needed for these
steps, however, state that this method was developed to serve a two-fold purpose: "(i) developing realistic
national and sub-national targets that are in line with the SDGs; and (ii) building technology and socio-
economic roadmaps that enable countries to meet those targets" (Kanter et al., 2016).
• The element of assessing constructed pathways or trajectories towards established future visions did not
ﬁnd universal recognition. Geurs and van Wee (2004) and Giurco et al. (2011) and Soria-Lara and Banister
(2018) incorporated a separate assessment element as part of their proposed methodologies. Soria-Lara
37
==PAGE:51==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
and Banister (2018) apply multi-criteria analysis in a participatory setting to evaluate the feasibility
and preference towards scenarios by stakeholders. In comparison Giurco et al. (2011) apply a life-cycle
thinking approach to assess the resource needs and environmental impacts of designed pathways. Geurs
and van Wee (2004) address the impacts of designed scenarios from a holistic perspective, including
environmental, social and economic dimensions (more information in section 5.3.1. The validity and
feasibility of the constructed scenarios were addressed in many cases (Schippl and Leisner, 2009; Svenfelt
et al., 2011; Doyle and Davies, 2013) by involving stakeholders and experts in participation.
Outcome/Implementation
Regarding the last element of comparison from Table 5.2, it should be mentioned that the implementation/out-
come was guided by the intention of the backcasting application. In several case studies, the underlying in-
tention of applying backcasting was to generate concepts for regional or sector development and thereby to
support decision-making processes or even policy development, e.g. Giurco et al. (2011), Doyle and Davies
(2013), Broman et al. (2013), Kanter et al. (2016) and Sisto et al. (2016). The example by Soria-Lara and Banister
(2018) was directed at providing policy-support concerning sustainable transport in Andalusia, Spain. For this
reason, the backcasting methodology included multiple workshops to gain insight on visioning, identiﬁcation
of policy measures and formulation of policy packages; and assessment of policy impacts. As discovered from
personal communication with the Asst. Prof. Dr. Soria-Lara, the resource-intensive backcasting study did not
ﬁnd consideration at a political level due to a shift in the political leadership of the region during the conduc-
tion of the study (Julio A. Soria-Lara, Personal Communication, 2020). This highlights the barriers to connect
the policy-relevant information and learnings gained through backcasting and its consequent inﬂuence of po-
litical actions. Contrary to the intention of supporting the policy domain, backcasting found application to
serve social-learning purposes (Bagheri and Hjorth, 2007; Musse et al., 2018) or to spark further research on
identiﬁed solution elements (Bagheri and Hjorth, 2007; Broman et al., 2013). This was the case for the work
by Bagheri and Hjorth (2007) as the focus of the case study lays on system dynamics models in combination
with backcasting. The purpose of applying backcasting in the case of Schippl and Leisner (2009) was to gen-
erate an open discussion rather than identifying "optimal" pathways towards a vision (Jens Schippl, Personal
Communication, 2020).
