Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
5.3
Detailed Review of 5 Backcasting Applications
In the following 5 case studies were handpicked to elaborate on relevant concepts and approaches within
the ﬁeld of backcasting. The case studies have been selected due to their detailed representation of speciﬁc
backcasting applications and thereby covering a range of diﬀerent backcasting methodologies. Further, they
provide insight on backcasting in a political context, use of system dynamics models or relevant tools or
concepts such as a ’resource kit’ or ’policy packages’ (for further explanation see section 2.1). The case studies
are presented in such form that relevant steps of the methodology are presented, accompanied by reﬂections
and comments by the authors. For case studies 4 & 5, additional description of the method are attached in
section B - Appendix.
5.3.1
Backcasting in a Governmental Context - "Sustainable Transport Policy in
the Netherlands"
The case study by Geurs and van Wee (2004) explores how backcasting from scenarios can be used as a tool
to advise sustainable transport policy measures in the Netherlands by 2030. The case study uses a nuanced
method similar to the Robinson approach and is a good example of how the method can be applied in a gov-
ernmental context. The authors do not reﬂect on the structure of their adapted methodology nor the results
from the case study. However, for the sole purpose of reﬂecting, some observations can be made throughout
the following paragraphs.
The main diﬀerence of the method (see Figure 5.2) compared to the Robinson approach can be found looking
at the last two steps of the method. Whilst Robinson (1990) outlines the determination of implementation
requirements as an additional step in his method (see section 3.4.1), Geurs and van Wee (2004) include this
element before undertaking an impact analysis. Thereby, the structure proposed by Geurs and van Wee (2004)
suggests an inversion of the last two methodological steps of the original Robinson approach. It can be ar-
gued that the inclusion of implementation requirements before the impact analysis is related to the fact that
the choice of policy measures will inﬂuence the level of pathway impact. Another reason for the change in
structure could be the inclusion of ’policy time paths’ which will be elaborated on in the following section.
Therefore, conducting an impact analysis at the end of a backcasting study is relevant to provide a holistic
assessment of generated pathway options.
41
==PAGE:55==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
Figure 5.2: The backcasting methodology used in Geurs and van Wee (2004) to determine sustainable transport
policy measures in the Netherlands. The methodology is an adaptation of the original backcasting approach
deﬁned by Robinson (1990).
In regards to the individual steps of the method as displayed in Figure 5.2, a few observations can be made. Step
2 of the method looks at specifying goals, constraints and targets. The objectives around sustainable transport
deﬁned in the EST are largely qualitative, thus since no quantitative goals were determined the study takes
quantitative international guidelines from the World Health Organisation (WHO), Intergovernmental Panel
on Climate Change (IPCC) and United Nations Economic Commission for Europe (UNECE). Furthermore, a
goal must have a deadline, thus a target year of 2030 is deﬁned. The adaptation of existing international goals
and targets are ﬁtting for a project on an international scale, however, do not take into consideration the eco-
nomic, social and environmental situations speciﬁc to the Netherlands. The adaptation of goals for this case
study seem relevant but if applied elsewhere, for example in a less economically developed country, the same
targets may be unrealistic to achieve. This needs to be considered when the scope of the study changes.
As seen in other studies like Schippl and Leisner (2009), Svenfelt et al. (2011), Giaoutzi et al. (2012) and Soria-
Lara and Banister (2018) the application of a BAU scenario can be used to inform the current state. Geurs
and van Wee (2004) apply the BAU scenario to forecast the situation until 2030 in step 3 of the method. The
authors assume that there is no diversion from the current trends based on the data and information available
at the time of the study. A BAU scenario was conducted to allow a comparison relative to the end-point goals
providing information on the scale of change that is required to achieve the goals.
42
==PAGE:56==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
Once the prior steps have been concluded a scenario analysis can be conducted in step 5 of the method. Here
alternative scenarios which adhere to the goals outlined in step 2 must be constructed. In the case of Geurs
and van Wee (2004) this was accomplished by using expert judgement, existing literature, model simulations
and brainstorming sessions, as observed in a multitude of mentioned case studies (see section 5.2. As a result,
three scenarios were determined:
• Scenario 1: a high technology scenario which is dependent on technological improvements.
• Scenario 2: a mobility management scenario which is dependant on changes in transportation man-
agement.
• Scenario 3: a combination scenario which takes aspects of both technology and mobility management
to meet the goals.
However, in the remainder of the case study, only the combination scenario is described. This is because it is
identiﬁed as the scenario with the best possible implications on the mobility system. On one hand, no further
analysis has been conducted on the remaining two scenarios and thus, it is hard to conclude whether the
combination scenario is in-fact the most preferable. On the other hand, the combination scenario is beneﬁcial
since it is an optimal combination. It allows for more freedom in decision making as there are multiple solution
elements to choose from when proceeding towards the future. It can be argued that this reduces the risk of
lock-ins. Besides, since only this scenario has been considered, it reduces the workload required to conduct an
impact analysis and consider implementation requirements. This approach of generating scenarios based on
a scenario theme such as "technology" or "management" was observed in several other case studies (such as
Schippl and Leisner (2009), Giurco et al. (2011), Svenfelt et al. (2011) and Giaoutzi et al. (2012)). These types of
scenario themes dominate the generation of the subsequent pathways and do rarely depict a realistic scenario
(John Robinson, Personal Communication, 2020).
To achieve the deﬁned goals, implementation measures must be identiﬁed to determine how that goal can be
achieved. In the case of Geurs and van Wee (2004) a list of possible policy tools are identiﬁed in step 6 of the
method. This informs decision-makers about the availability of measures and thereby provides freedom and
an element of ﬂexibility in decision making. Flexibility is important in long-term planning since the future
is uncertain (Börjeson et al., 2006). However, to further reduce the risk of lock-ins, additional scenarios (and
thus policy tools) could have been presented to provide more options. Some of the policy measures included
in the study are tradable CO2 permits, policy pricing instruments, regulations, infrastructure policy and other
instruments including educational campaigns. The consideration of policy instruments is addressed in rela-
tively few of the reviewed case studies. Giaoutzi et al. (2012) and Soria-Lara and Banister (2018) are prime
examples regarding an approach called policy packaging, which is elaborated on in section 5.3.5. In step 6 of
the proposed method by Geurs and van Wee (2004), no particular action plan or pathway is outlined. Instead,
a discussion is presented on how ’policy implementation time paths’ need to be taken into account. These
time paths take into consideration the time required for implementation including the barriers (political, ad-
ministrative and social) that need to be overcome.
43
==PAGE:57==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
The ﬁnal step of the methodology is to conduct an impact assessment. In this instance, it is done to assess the
economic, social and environmental implications of achieving the combination scenario. The three considered
pillars of analysis ﬁt nicely into the context of sustainability. The use of analysis tools are informative and
should be explored when generating the analysis for the backcasting method applied in this case study. It is
hard to further comment on the analysis since the authors have not been transparent on why such tools have
been used. The social assessment was conducted qualitatively using many social factors such as health and
safety, material wealth and cultural diversity. The economic assessment utilised impact path analysis. This
uses input-output tables to determine macro-economic changes. According to Geurs and van Wee (2004), this
approach is comparable to using system dynamics models (similar to those applied by Schade and Schade
(2005), Bagheri and Hjorth (2007) and Musse et al. (2018)) to assess economic adjustments. Finally, an envi-
ronmental assessment was performed by comparing emission reductions to the BAU scenario.
5.3.2
Resource Kits to Support Backcasting: "Sustainable Cities in a Backcasting
Perspective"
The report by Broman et al. (2013) provides a conclusive report about the ambitions of applying backcasting in
an energy system and market analysis context to support sustainable development until 2030. The work was
speciﬁcally designed for district heating systems in Sweden and included the conduction of workshops and
meetings according to a participatory approach with a dedicated reference group along a three-year project
period. Broman et al. (2013) ﬁnd that their work contributed to cross-disciplinary learning and knowledge
sharing. This cross-learning was especially noticeable for the energy aspect of the system under investiga-
tion. Their work ended up being of support to regional development programmes of considered study areas.
The case study was chosen based on its use of the Framework for Strategic Sustainable Development in com-
bination with a four-step backcasting method. Arguably the method is identical to the ABCD-methodology as
presented in 3.4.3 and therefore, does not ﬁnd an explicit description in the following paragraphs. Further, the
case study outlines useful tools such as a system dynamics model and a resource kit which will be presented
in the following.
The FSSD is used in support of planning and applying sustainability practices. In the report by Broman et al.
(2013) it is presented as a ﬁve-level model, which includes the levels: (1) system, (2) success, (3) guidelines,
(4) actions and (5) tools. These ﬁve partially interacting levels are utilised to approach complex systems. The
levels can be described as follows:
1. System level: At this level, major functions of investigated systems are described and reﬂected upon.
This means that elements like e.g. interactions between human society, their behaviour and the envi-
ronment, knowledge on system behaviour, stocks and ﬂows of the system are explored.
2. Success level: Here, the deﬁnition of success and the objective of the project are determined. For
example, deﬁnitions of what a sustainable society could entail are discussed at this level. Broman et al.
(2013) set objectives and the deﬁnition of success according to a principled deﬁnition of sustainability
instead of deﬁning concrete scenarios. This principled deﬁnition of success is described as follows:
“To be functional for strategic sustainable development, the set of principles must be necessary (but not
44
==PAGE:58==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
more) to avoid unnecessary restrictions and to reduce distraction over elements that may be debatable
and suﬃcient (and not less) to cover all aspects of sustainability. In addition, the set of principles should
be general to make sense for all stakeholders and thus allow for cross-disciplinary and cross-sector
cooperation, concrete to inspire and guide innovation, problem-solving and actions, and distinct (non-
overlapping) to enable comprehension and facilitate the development of indicators for monitoring.”
3. Strategic guidelines level: At this level, strategic guidelines are introduced to achieve the set objective.
Consideration of ﬁnancial, social, and ecological resources are made at this level.
4. Actions level: At level four concrete actions and measures are planned and put into an action pro-
gramme which is in line with the previously set strategic guidelines.
5. Tools level: Decision support, monitoring and conduction of actions require further methods and tools,
which are introduced at the tools level of the FSSD. These tools are used in line with presented levels
before. This means that tools and methods chosen for the project need to be in support of chosen
actions (4), to assure that strategic guidelines (3) are followed, to arrive step-wise at the objective (2)
and to operate within the system (1).
Regarding the success level of the FSSD, Broman et al. (2013) argue that in the context of backcasting it is more
helpful to work based on a principled version of success than a ﬁxed detailed scenario. A principled version
of success is a loosely determined success condition which does not necessarily need to include quantitative
information (Holmberg and Robèrt, 2000). This approach following the opinion of the authors is beneﬁcial
as ﬁnding an agreement between stakeholders and interest groups in an abstract future scenario proves to
result in diﬃculties. Furthermore, it is argued that rapid technological and cultural evolution complicates the
backcasting conditions over time and therefore, a principled version of success is to be preferred as it can be
achieved in multiple ways. It could be stated that one limitation with principled deﬁnitions is the opportunity
for greenwashing as a result of the freedom available. Since the deﬁnition allows ﬂexibility, there is a chance
that solutions may be marketed under the umbrella term of sustainability, yet validation of sustainability is
considered in-house and thus is subjective. The Natural Step Germany counter this argument by stating that
by adhering to the outlined procedure of the ABCD-methodology in combination with sustainability princi-
ples such greenwashing can actively be counteracted (TheNaturalStep-Germany, 2020). An example of such
greenwashing techniques was not observed in reviewed case studies.
Broman et al. (2013) consider the use of models to describe the system in step B of the ABCD-methodology.
Thereby, the authors were able to simulate and act upon its optimisation in form of a computer programme
called MODEST, which stands for “Model for Optimisation of Dynamic Energy System with Time-dependent
Components and Boundary Conditions” in the following steps of the ABCD-methodology. This model was
specially designed for the energy sector and fulﬁls set-out criteria by the authors. As an example, these in-
clude validation, model conditions and generalisation for relevant variables in the energy sector. However, as
observed with other forms of system dynamics models social dimensions were excluded (Musse et al., 2018;
Detlef van Vuuren, Personal Communication, 2020). The use of the model and its result are not transparently
presented in the report to allow for any further conclusions about the usefulness of models as a tool. As an
45
==PAGE:59==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
argumentation for the use of models Broman et al. (2013) state that a tool which combines the aspect of back-
casting and forecasting is valuable when identifying the “low hanging fruits” of a pathway towards the desired
goal. Furthermore, they claim that using models allows to learn about systems without conducting expensive
or time-consuming experiments, especially considering today’s computational advancements. MODEST does
not show a trans-system thinking aspect, instead covers the dynamic modelling for the speciﬁc sector of dis-
trict heating and energy sector. Nevertheless, it is apparent that besides energy aspects, also relevant economic
factors are considered.
As a result of conducted project and workshops by Broman et al. (2013) a ’resource kit’ was assembled which
contains an array of possible solutions and actions in the district heating sector. Among those solutions, the
tool includes a step-wise approach to use related methods and tools for example “context mapping, business
model canvas, value network analysis, life cycle assessment, scenarios, energy systems analysis, modelling,
simulation and sustainability indicators”. Further, the kit provides information on how to identify and get in
touch with stakeholders, organise workshops and supporting material. The resource kit further represents a
collection of identiﬁed leverage points which then allow for early smart actions, change of business models
and generation of upstream solutions. The resource kit was introduced as a methodological support element
and highlights the intention of the case study by Broman et al. (2013) towards sustainable development. Using
its elements, more sustainable action would be taken according to the authors. As described in the case study
the resource kit connects seamlessly to steps C and D of the proposed ABCD-methodology.
Besides the study presented by Broman et al. (2013) only one further case study was investigated that applies
the ABCD-methodology as an adaptation to what was presented in section 3.4.3. Arguably, some case studies
rely on a four-step method, which is not directly comparable to the ABCD-methodology (Schippl and Leisner,
2009; Giaoutzi et al., 2012; Musse et al., 2018). There is a host of available literature on the use of the FSSD and
the ABCD-methodology. However, little relevant case studies were found that applied backcasting outside a
business or local actor setting (Damsø et al., 2014) and documented their ﬁndings in English. The applica-
tion of the FSSD, as well as the ABCD-methodology, is carried out in diﬀerent forms and slightly diﬀerent
approaches by the diﬀerent Natural Step associations in many parts of the world. This ﬁnding was conﬁrmed
in a discussion with Prof. Dr. Holmberg (John Holmberg, Personal Communication, 2020).
5.3.3
Science and technology assessment: "Backcasting in Transport: Scenarios
for the Future of European Long-Distance Transport"
The study from Schippl and Leisner (2009) is a science and technology operations assessment using backcasting
from scenarios to discuss the European transport system with set scope to the year 2047. It analyses scenarios
for sustainable transport, increasing eﬃciency and reducing oil dependency. This case study is particularly
interesting as it has a Europe-wide scope. This case study relies on a four-step method which includes the
creation of images of the future, thereby it is categorised as backcasting from scenarios. However, this method
intends to explore pathway options and thus does not provide recommendations or pathway preference. The
purpose of the study was to spark discussion and inform politicians in the European Parliament (Jens Schippl,
Personal Communication, 2020).
46
==PAGE:60==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
The method applied in the case study by Schippl and Leisner (2009) can be summarised in four steps, similar
to other observed methodologies, as follows:
1. Identiﬁcation of problems and targets (goals)
2. Calculation of a baseline to illustrate the scale of change required to meet the targets
3. Designing images of the future
4. Analysis and assessment of technologies and policy measures
In the ﬁrst step of the method, the problem is deﬁned and the goals for the target year 2047 are set. In this case,
two quantitative and one qualitative target are determined. The two quantitative targets relate to emission
and oil reductions, whereas the qualitative target is related to accessibility. It is stated that since accessibility
is a qualitative target it can be conceptualised in a variety of ways. Description of the target generation is
not transparent in the case study, however, there is a reference to workshops and thus, it is presumed that
participatory backcasting or expert judgement was considered in this step. This step is comparable to Step A
of the ABCD-methodology.
The second step of the method is speciﬁcally designed to construct a business-as-usual scenario. The purpose
of the BAU scenario is to quantify the emission gap between the targets and the BAU case as it was observed
in Geurs and van Wee (2004), Giaoutzi et al. (2012) and Soria-Lara and Banister (2018). In addition, it high-
lights the scale of change which is required to meet the targets. This raises the question of what actions can
be taken to bridge this gap? The construction of a BAU scenario is considered a useful tool to identify the
scale of the problem. However, this process itself propagates a signiﬁcant level of uncertainty (Hyndman and
Athanasopoulos, 2018; John Robinson, Personal Communication, 2020). This is due to the scope of the study
since it is diﬃcult to gather detailed data on all modes of transportation on a European level. For instance,
international sea transport was excluded from the study due to lack of adequate data. This highlights two
important reﬂections: (1) uncertainty within long-term forecasting studies is largely dependant on the scope
of the study and (2) the utilisation of both qualitative and quantitative approaches in backcasting studies make
it a useful tool guide policy measures on a large scale.
In the third step three "images" or visions of the future are determined to guide the scenario generation. The
use of images of the future is still considered backcasting from scenarios, however, strictly focuses on the
end-point and considering the pathway separately. The images created can be titles as:
1. strong rich high-tech Europe
2. slow and reﬂective lifestyles
3. contrast image - economic pressure and expensive energy
47
==PAGE:61==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
These images of the future vary in economic growth, lifestyles, governance as well as transportation prioriti-
sation. Furthermore, these images must meet certain criteria. These criteria state that the images of the future
must:
1. Meet all targets
2. Be plausible but can be relatively extreme
3. Be clearly diﬀerent from each other
4. Cover a signiﬁcant range of possibilities and keep research manageable
5. Limit the number of images in order to maintain simplicity
The choice of a contrast image has not been observed in any other backcasting case study and as Schippl and
Leisner (2009) state is arguably not considered a normative scenario. However, the image was included to
provide a narrative to a negative image of the future and thereby exploring possible alternative futures. The
results of the backcasting application indicated that achieving each of the scenarios will signiﬁcantly reduce
the target ’accessibility’. Thus, both technical solutions and demand management solutions must be employed.
This ﬁnding indicates that it is not realistic to focus solely on one image of the future as it does not achieve
the objectives regarding accessibility as mentioned prior (see section 5.3.1).
The ﬁnal step of the method serves to form trajectories that lead towards the previously designed images
of the future, i.e. generating pathways. As observed in other case studies, information on generating these
pathways is not comprehensible, however, a few observations can be made. The identiﬁed actions or policy
measures aim to address the emissions gap by three diﬀerent strategies: decoupling transport growth from
economic growth, reducing the carbon intensity of diﬀerent transport modes and shifting to more eco-friendly
methods of transportation. These strategies of tackling the emissions gap were applied to each of the three
2047 images, to determine what policy tools could be utilised. The approach taken in step 4 of the method,
it can be seen that Schippl and Leisner (2009) consider not only the action that needs to be implemented to
achieve a future image but also the related policy options that are available. Following the description of the
method, there is no distinct separation of the ’what’, i.e. the actions and changes that need to take form and
the ’how’, i.e. how can these actions and changes be translated into reality through policy measures.
In conversation with Jens Schippl, it was gathered that this element of the method received insuﬃcient time
due to the time-consuming exercises of the previous steps, especially step 3 (Jens Schippl, Personal Communi-
cation, 2020). The generation of future images consumed a considerable amount of time due to the involvement
of diverse stakeholder groups, which encountered challenges in agreeing on the deﬁnition of future images
(similar to the statement by Broman et al. (2013)). Therefore, the length of the approach is an important factor
to consider when developing a methodology. The process must not be excessively resource and time-intensive,
otherwise, it may not be beneﬁcial to fund such a study.
48
==PAGE:62==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
5.3.4
System Dynamics Models: "Applying Backcasting and System Dynamics
Towards Sustainable Development"
The study by Musse et al. (2018) is novel in the sense that it combines backcasting from scenarios with system
dynamics in a dedicated methodological approach. The application of system dynamics was also observed in
the case study by Broman et al. (2013). However, Musse et al. (2018) provide a detailed insight on how to com-
bine system dynamics models in a participatory backcasting application, which is the reason for its detailed
presentation in the following. Further, the study provides a comprehensive and very detailed approach to-
wards engaging stakeholders in a participatory manner, which due to its extent is provided in detail in section
B.2 - Appendix. The study focuses on the housing planning for the city Florianopolis, Brazil with end-point
in 2030. The designated methodology for the case study incorporates participatory backcasting as well as a
separate step for the combination of backcasting and system dynamics modelling, which is the reason why
this case study is presented.
The guiding structure applied to the methodology in Musse et al. (2018) is a four-step framework. In all of
its steps, the framework is fundamentally relying on the exchange of information in stakeholder and expert
groups in the form of presentations, workshops and brainstorming activities in reoccurring patterns. The
details are taken from the original case study and entail:
1. Stakeholder’s strategic orientation to the problem
2. Building up scenarios for sustainable future
3. Backcasting - creating sustainable alternatives
4. Action plan monitoring
At this point, it is vital to mention that the terminology used by Musse et al. (2018) diﬀers from the deﬁnition
outlined in this thesis. Thus, for the following section, the terminology has been adapted such that it ﬁts with
deﬁned terminology in this thesis.
Contrary to other backcasting studies, Musse et al. (2018) start by gathering opinions and information about
stakeholder’s view on the problem. Why certain groups of stakeholders were involved is not clear from the
reviewed case study. However, it was mentioned that the "stakeholders are the actors playing roles and have
responsibility for the solution implementation" of the respective study. The stakeholders involved in the study
by Musse et al. (2018) include representatives with diverse backgrounds as community representatives, experts
or local leaders. The participants receive information from the administrators of the study to gain an initial
overview. Following that an analysis is conducted based on group discussion tools, as e.g. brainstorming,
interviews or practical learning are utilised to break down the problem in a structural way. This is used to
spark creative thoughts on how to solve the issue at hand. The approach taken is very problem-focused and
does not necessarily have to involve a vision. The focus here is solving an existing problem.
In the following step, participants of the case study are devised into groups, supervised by an assigned spe-
cialist. The use of a so-called "workshop toolkit" allows the specialists to adapt to group dynamics and the
49
==PAGE:63==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
problem in focus. This toolkit includes activities based on creating illustrations, brainstorming, comparative
analysis, practical learning and possibly Delphi techniques. This workshop toolkit was not presented in suﬃ-
cient detail, but in its basic idea can be understood as a similar concept as the resource kit produced by Broman
et al. (2013). During the process of group work, the participants collectively create normative images of the
future based on three diﬀerent perspectives:
1. pessimistic
2. intermediate
3. optimistic
To further the analysis of the future scenarios, the authors of the case study highlight that it is important to
elaborate on integrated analysis. This results in the identiﬁcation of the interconnections as well as trade-oﬀs
on a higher level, i.e. a systemic level. The case study by Musse et al. (2018) thereby actively includes an
element of system understanding and possible interconnections between sectors. Besides speciﬁc mentioning
in the work by Giaoutzi et al. (2012) it was not possible to identify if other case studies consider the aspect of
system trade-oﬀs and relationships for the analysis of the current state.
In step 3 participants are instructed to analyse the constructed images of the future and elements that would
need to be addressed to achieve such images. This is done by looking back in time in search of tangible
variables, ideas, technological leaps and tendencies, as well as realise technological, cultural, behavioural and
organisational change. Musse et al. (2018) provide in this step a comparatively detailed proposal in regards
to what levels of actions could be considered in a brainstorming exercise. No other case study provided this
level of insight to identify solution elements. Additionally, the participants are involved in creating a system
dynamics model using computer aided-tools, which in this case was the software Stella II. The embedded
assumptions on trends of key variables and parameters are made based on previous discussions and data gen-
eration, which are veriﬁed through stakeholder and expert groups. The computer programme was run for the
considered period of the project from 2010 to 2030 for each image of the future. Depending on the choice of
future image and related variable and parameter values, diﬀerent system behaviours could be observed. In a
participatory manner, the ﬁndings from the model supported the identiﬁcation of further systemic solutions.
It therefore also allows for better learning and understanding of the system for the participants and users of the
model. Musse et al. (2018) argue that the use of system dynamics models is beneﬁcial as it assists quantitative
decisions, meaning the modelling and simulation of deﬁned key variables and parameters. The authors further
argue that the combination of backcasting and system dynamics models is a core asset. Positive feedback was
gained from the participants as the use of system dynamic models was received well due to its user-friendly
interface. Further, system dynamics models contributed to the idea generation process and enabled discussion
based on quantitative simulations. Additionally, these models can be used to assess uncertainties and related
risks of projective scenarios through the evaluation of system behaviour. The authors of the report point out
that the approach taken contributed to social learning and networking amongst stakeholders, which is a key
aspect of using backcasting (Bagheri and Hjorth, 2007; Broman et al., 2013). They further argue that the use of
system dynamics and backcasting in the context of city planning allows for interactive teaching and learning
for stakeholders. This also allows for further use of more complex scientiﬁc applications as e.g. geographic
50
==PAGE:64==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
information systems (GIS).
Based on their ﬁndings in the previous steps, the participants create an action plan in the ﬁnal step of the
methodology. This action plan typically consists of a schedule, estimated costs and resources. To review the
implementation and monitor the execution of the plan, the authors advise utilising strategic planning control
and the monitoring of key events. Flexibility and adaptability should also be considered. When revisiting
the action plan as instructed by the schedule, new knowledge, generated data and gained experiences should
be considered. This subsequently leads to a reassessment of choices throughout the methodology including
the reﬁnement of the system dynamics model. Musse et al. (2018) thereby propose the idea of an action
plan expanded by an iterative evaluation mechanism. Similarly, the inclusion of a strategic action plan was
observed in the work by Sisto et al. (2018).
5.3.5
Policy Packages: "Scenario Analysis as a Foresight Tool in Agriculture"
In the case study by Giaoutzi et al. (2012) backcasting from scenarios is used as a scenario technique to study
future developments in the EU agricultural sector up to the target year of 2020. The case study aims at devel-
oping a new backcasting approach suitable for an EU wide study which can be used to inform policymakers.
Furthermore, the approach uses the element of ’policy packages’ originally developed in Banister (1998) which
has potentially fruitful learnings in regards to pathway construction. Giaoutzi et al. (2012) argue that the pro-
posed backcasting approach was useful and enabled focused discussions in the backcasting application, whilst
being able to stay within a set time frame. Furthermore, this approach gave ground to intuitive creativity and
by that creating policy pathways from multiple perspectives.
A four-step methodology is devised in the case study by Giaoutzi et al. (2012). It deﬁnes the goals, current
state and then leads on to pathway development/policy construction by the use of images of the future. This
method diﬀers from other methods explored by including subdivided steps and in-depth detail and structure
to the method. Giaoutzi et al. (2012) describe the proposed method in four steps, which they divide into an
’explorative element’ being the ﬁrst two steps and a ’backcasting element’ being steps three and four. All
steps besides step 3 entail several sub-steps which will not be explained in detail, however, a comprehensible
description can be found in section B.3 - Appendix. The method can be structured as follows:
1. Prioritisation and identiﬁcation of the problems
2. Structuring the baseline scenario
3. Designing the future images
4. Policy and pathway descriptions
Step 1 consists of three sub-steps which are described as ’regionalisation stage’, ’identiﬁcation/prioritisation
of problems, in both the internal (study system) and the external environment’, both leading up to ’selection
of objectives, policy targets and the respective indicators’. The regionalisation process serves to inform the
problem on a spatial scale and to set boundaries of the analysed system in the following. In this case study, the
51
==PAGE:65==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
scope is framed for the EU, thus information from EU agriculture case studies were analysed. This is then re-
ﬂected in the subsequent step where internal and external problem relationships for the agricultural sector are
described. It is important to deﬁne these elements to identify which of them are key and which are considered
drivers of the whole system. The drivers are important to identify to be aware of how policy can inﬂuence
them. In this case, the internal elements include agricultural production (demand, eﬃciencies), agricultural
social system (labour, migration patterns) and natural resource requirements (land use, water resources). The
external elements include market-related elements (economic growth, world agriculture markets) and non-
market related elements (climate change, rising sea-levels, depleting natural resources). Furthermore, it is
stated that the distinction between internal and external environment elements can be vague and thus a clear
decision must be taken to classify them. This sub-step of identifying internal and external problem variables
can be compared to what Musse et al. (2018) address in their methodology as system understanding. The prior
conducted work merges into the third sub-step which addresses the selection of objectives and policy targets.
These can be both qualitative and quantitative. Six objectives were deﬁned in the study, including objectives in
the environment, economic eﬃciency, regional development, social cohesion, food quality and energy. From
here the policy targets are constructed around the deﬁned objectives. To determine policy targets within each
objective, a mixture of top-down (expert and internal workshops) and bottom-up (reviews of literature and
sustainability targets) approaches were used.
The second step of the method by Giaoutzi et al. (2012) is used for constructing a baseline or BAU scenario
and can be divided into three sub-steps. As a ﬁrst sub-step, the present situation is studied including the
identiﬁcation of tendencies and projections. In this case study, the agricultural sector was further divided
into the sub-sectors: crops, livestock and forest to increase the level of detail. Many of the emerging patterns
were related to technological improvements and innovation. In the following sub-step, the goal is to identify
trends. In the case study by Giaoutzi et al. (2012) both European and global trends were analysed due to the
mutual inﬂuence on one another. The trends assessed include the following ﬁelds: environmental, market,
demographic and migration, etc. Consequently, it is proposed to generate a baseline scenario until the set
target year. This approach is common to the case studies by Geurs and van Wee (2004), Schippl and Leisner
(2009), Svenfelt et al. (2011) and Soria-Lara and Banister (2018). In the case study by Giaoutzi et al. (2012)
an integrated model framework was used to calculate the baseline scenario. The model framework consisted
of various modelling tools including a general equilibrium (GE) model and IMAGE. The framework further
integrates three spatial models ranging from national to regional and includes regions outside the EU. As an
outcome of the application of the said framework, it was claimed that set policy targets for 2020 will not be
achievable. Giaoutzi et al. (2012), therefore, conclude, that there is a need for political commitment to close
the projected gap.
The third step of the proposed method by Giaoutzi et al. (2012) entails the design of future images to meet
set targets as was observed in many other case studies (Schippl and Leisner, 2009; Svenfelt et al., 2011; Giurco
et al., 2011; Doyle and Davies, 2013; Damsø et al., 2014). This step is broken down into further sub-steps
following a similar structure provided in Banister (1998). This structure provides a comprehensible approach
to generating normative future images. As a ﬁrst element, the previously set objectives and targets from
step 1 are recalled to reignite the awareness about the long-term vision. Second, the ’contextual elements’
52
==PAGE:66==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
are considered in reference to recent global developments that can potentially be applied or utilised in the
future. This consideration is fed by the prior conducted work on system understanding and trends. Thirdly, a
consideration of ’strategic elements’ is suggested, which is important to identify what resources are available
at present that will help steer change. This includes the recognition of key drivers and leverage points in the
current system. The previously mentioned sub-steps are a repetition of encountered elements of the previous
two methodological steps. Following the images of the future can be constructed. The purpose of this step
is to describe various policy and social images in the future in which the targets have been achieved. Whilst
the elements, objectives and policy targets remain as the guiding visioning tool, varying combinations of
contextual and strategical elements give room for exploring diﬀerent images of the future. The images of the
future must also meet the following criteria:
1. The images must achieve the targets outlined in step 1.
2. The images must be realistic however can have comparably extreme tendencies.
3. Each image should be signiﬁcantly contrasting to one another.
4. There should be a large range of future images with the aim to explore many possibles.
5. Only a few manageable number of images must be considered to reduce complexity.
6. The images must be concerning the particular system in the study.
7. The images must take into consideration the contextual and strategic elements discussed prior.
This list of criteria can be directly compared to the work by Schippl and Leisner (2009) (see section 5.3.3). It can
be argued that Giaoutzi et al. (2012) adapted this list and added the elements 6 & 7 to include the previously
outlined methodological additions.
In the study by Giaoutzi et al. (2012), a total of 9 images are determined (see Table 5.3), which is remarkable as
amongst all other case studies no more than 3 scenarios could be observed. The strategic elements vary with
the level of technological innovation and decoupling from diﬀerent aspects of the agricultural system (e.g.
decoupling agricultural production from environmental impacts, GDP growth, etc.). The contextual elements
of the images vary in regards to top-down, bottom-up and combination approaches. It can be argued that
Giaoutzi et al. (2012) generate a better resolution of normative future images than other case studies due to
the number of total images. Nevertheless, this could mean that a considerable amount of time was needed
to conduct the construction of future images. To accommodate for this issue and to satisfy criteria number
ﬁve, only three images of the future were chosen based on their coherence, plausibility and performance.
Furthermore, upon selection of these images, additional development and reﬁnement of chosen future images
were conducted with expert groups. The three chosen futures were:
1. Image 1 (T1): High-tech Europe, global cooperation for sustainable agriculture
2. Image 2 (TD2): In search of balance, accord on sustainability
3. Image 3 (D3): Active regions and reﬂexive lifestyles
53

Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment

D2
Bottom-up
T3
TD3
D3
The ﬁnal step of the method presented in Giaoutzi et al. (2012) utilises a policy package and path approach to
generate elements and stepping stones of a pathway. This approach is fundamentally relying on the ability
to combine individual policy measures into policy packages, which then are aligned to design a whole policy
path towards the identiﬁed desired goal. The overall structure of this approach is depicted in Figure 5.3 and
originates from the contribution of Banister (1998). Similar to prior steps, step 4 is subdivided into four sub-
steps.
Figure 5.3: Depiction of policy package and policy path approach as discussed in Giaoutzi et al. (2012). Depic-
tion originally extracted from Banister (1998).
The sub-step of ’key elements and key states’ addresses areas that need a change to achieve the respective
future images. The key states refer to the gap between the BAU scenario and the future images and should be
assessed for each one of the identiﬁed key elements. This is then used to inform the suggested policy mea-
sures. The case study by Giaoutzi et al. (2012) identiﬁes key elements which include: bio-economy, regulated
agricultural factor markets and rural developments. However, the key states refer to the gap between the BAU
scenario and the future images and should be assessed for each one of the identiﬁed key elements. This is then
used to inform the suggested policy measures. In a second step, the available policy measures are chosen to
address the key elements prior. As depicted in Figure 5.3 after the identiﬁcation of available policy measures,
54
==PAGE:68==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
policy packages i.e. grouping of individual policy measures, are formed. The concept of creating policy pack-
ages beneﬁts from the resulting synergistic eﬀects of combining complementary policy measures. Combining
individual policies to form policy packages addresses set targets more eﬃciently than by considering single
policy measures or elements. The policy packages are constructed following two diﬀerent approaches, which
are deﬁned by the terms "deductive" and "intuitive". The deductive approach takes outset in the framework
of constructed images creating the outline of the pathway. In comparison, the intuitive approach returns to
identiﬁed policy measures and based on an intuitive creative process combines measures into packages. This
approach is ideally conducted in a participatory setting with relevant stakeholders and experts such as poli-
cymakers. In total 257 diﬀerent policy measures were identiﬁed by Giaoutzi et al. (2012) and categorised into
four categories: (1) lifestyle orientated policy measures, (2) market-oriented policy measures, (3) regulation-
oriented policy measures and (4) public infrastructure/services-oriented policy measures. To advice policy-
makers Giaoutzi et al. (2012) state that this approach provides policymakers with impacts on the targets for
each policy measure. However, no further information is provided on the impacts. Considering the number
of policy measures available this may have been a resource-heavy endeavour, however, if successful can be
incredibly informative. Subsequently, these policy measures were translated into 15 policy packages (see Table
5.4. The assembled policy packages are then put in place to design the actual pathway concerning time and
action towards the created vision in a subsequent step. A policy path includes several policy packages and
is created in an iterative procedure. Following this procedure, adequate detail can be provided regarding the
following questions: What needs to be done? Who is responsible for carrying out the action? Where should
the action take place? How long will the action take to show eﬀects and when should follow-up activities take
place? The outcome of this procedure is depicted in Table 5.4. Unfortunately, it was not clear from the case
study by Giaoutzi et al. (2012) in which order the individual policy packages are applied or what they entail
in detail. From Table 5.4 it can be observed that the pathway towards IMAGE I consists of the lowest number
of packages to achieve the objectives. IMAGE II & III both have additional packages and thereby require more
policy implementation. The link between policy packages and pathways is not explained in suﬃcient detail,
however, it could be deducted that IMAGE I with the lowest policy packages is the image with the highest
risk related to not achieving the goals. The more policy packages and thereby problem variables are addressed
the higher the likelihood to achieve the outset image. The approach suggested by Giaoutzi et al. (2012) is a
technique to address the issue of lock-ins which are particularly challenging for studies with a wide scope as
they provide a range of future images which can be achieved through the implementation of additional policy
packages (Banister, 1998; Giaoutzi et al., 2012).

5.4
Key Reﬂections
The following represents a summary of key reﬂections gathered from reviewing a total of 15 case studies and
additional supporting literature. The main limitation observed across all backcasting studies is the level of
detail in explanation and description of procedures and instructions. This fact may contribute to the perceived
limitations of backcasting in the following. Nevertheless, some reﬂections could be gathered regarding the use
of backcasting:
• The use of system dynamics models was observed in two case studies (Broman et al., 2013; Musse et al.,
2018). The work by Giaoutzi et al. (2012) applies a sophisticated framework of models to generate
projections. It could be observed that the use of models can contribute in multiple ways to the application
of backcasting. On one hand, models can be applied to understand the current system and subsequently
to serve as projective models, thereby supporting the generation of BAU scenarios. On the other hand,
models in backcasting can serve to identify leverage points and system relevant variables which then
fed into brainstorming of solutions and subsequently the generation of pathways. It was argued by
several authors that the use of models in a participatory manner contributed to the social learning and
creativity process (Broman et al., 2013; Musse et al., 2018). The use of models for assessing pathways
was not explicitly observed, however, is a relevant scientiﬁc ﬁeld of the modelling community as can be
seen from the IPCC report on ’Representative Concentration Pathways’ (Detlef van Vuuren, Personal
Communication, 2020).
56
==PAGE:70==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
• Another reﬂection was that besides the application of predictive scenario techniques such as the BAU
scenario no further elements of scenario analysis were encountered to enhance the backcasting process.
• A range of scenario scales was observed across reviewed case studies. The element of scales was only
once speciﬁcally addressed amongst reviewed case studies in the work by Giaoutzi et al. (2012). This
element lies without the context of this thesis. However, as further reading material the multi-scale
scenario approaches by Kok et al. (2006) and Stratigea and Giaoutzi (2012) are recommended. Both
cases show fruitful insight into translating and scaling of scenarios.
• The case study by Giurco et al. (2011) is the only case study amongst reviewed literature that employs
a risk assessment of generated pathways. The element of risks and linked uncertainty was assessed in
terms of technical, socio-political and economic risks for each cluster of scenario.
• Across reviewed backcasting studies it can be seen that the method designed for a backcasting study and
participants involved, depends on a number of factors. These factors being: scope; context; resources
and purpose. The factor of purpose is taken as a key factor behind the conduction of a backcasting
study.
• In total 11 case studies applied participatory elements to their backcasting methodology. The aspect of
participation is of high relevance and inﬂuences the methodological structure as well as the possible out-
comes of the application. This aspect in combination with backcasting has therefore been investigated
further. An elaborate discussion can be found in section 7.6.
• Lacking information in regards to conducting a backcasting study was predominantly present in terms
of ’how to generate a pathway’. Besides presented approaches by Musse et al. (2018) and Giaoutzi et al.
(2012) little tangible information was available in reviewed case studies. Further, it can be stated, that a
clear distinction between elements that have to change to achieve a desired outcome or vision and how
to translate these changes into reality, was missing.
• Closely related to the previous point is the link between future images and the formulation of pathways
to achieve these images. The only case study observed that considers providing more than 3 future
images is by Giaoutzi et al. (2012). However, Giaoutzi et al. (2012) also narrow down the number of
future images when it comes to generating pathways in their subsequent fourth step. Further, it was
seen that none of the case studies consider the option of providing multiple pathways for each image of
the future.
• By comparing reviewed case studies, it became apparent that there is no commonly agreed guidance on
how to structure future images. Besides the cases by Svenfelt et al. (2011), Doyle and Davies (2013) no
detailed information on the future images were provided and thus little to no conclusion was yielded
on the topic.
• Another reﬂection gathered from reviewing case studies is the aspect of time. Information about the
required time to prepare and conduct a backcasting thesis was scarce. This aspect was investigated in
discussion with experts as a result of this reﬂection.
It can be stated that the reviewed case studies provided a good general impression on backcasting and pro-
vided insightful concepts and approaches in applying backcasting. However, it must be stated that there are
57
==PAGE:71==
Chapter 5. Case Study Reviews: Backcasting in Environmental Management
DTU Environment
recognisable gaps and lack of knowledge regarding certain methodological steps and concepts. This ﬁnding
is taken forward in the context of this thesis and thereby supported further research. The reﬂections gathered
in this section are combined with further reﬂections from experts and the conducted teaching-based research
activity in Chapter 7, which ultimately leads to the design of the DTU backcasting methodology.
58
