==PAGE:79==
Chapter 7
Key Lessons Learnt
The leanings outlined in this section are the collection of gained knowledge and insights presented in the
previous Chapters 4, 5 & 6. The lessons learnt are discussed in each of the following sub-sections including
relevant supporting literature as well as comments from experts referenced throughout.
7.1
Encountered Backcasting Methods and Structure
The reviewed backcasting literature as well as case studies showed a variety of backcasting applications that
utilise variations of backcasting methodologies. It could be observed that all methodologies are linked in their
fundamental structure with the original work carried out by Prof. Dr. Robinson or Prof. Dr. Holmberg and
Prof. Dr. Robèrt. Across reviewed case studies variations of the elements: ’vision setting’; ’current state
analysis’; and ’identiﬁcation of solutions and/or pathways’ was identiﬁed. Nevertheless, it can be stated that
each structure showed variations and was designed for diﬀerent purposes and due to the scope, limitations
and resources of a project. In conversation with experts throughout the conduction of this thesis, it was
discussed which form and extents a backcasting application can take on and which intention is connected to
the respective elements of backcasting. In conclusion, we believe that backcasting is a fruitful long-term policy
support tool that can identify essential pathways towards the desired vision and future state. A backcasting
methodology needs to be ﬂexible and provide the essential methodological steps and tools relative to the
purpose of the application. Therefore, we propose the DTU backcasting methodology as described in the
following chapter.
7.2
Intentions and Context of Reviewed Case Studies
The reviewed case studies diverged in terms of the underlying intentions of the backcasting study as well as in
regards to the scale and context of the application. This highlights the usefulness and adaptability of the basic
concept of backcasting to diverse settings and the needs of the study administrators. As an example, the case
study by Schippl and Leisner (2009) illustrates that the backcast was primarily intended to provide grounds
for discussion amongst politicians about possible future developments and to create awareness about upcom-
ing challenges in the transport sector (Jens Schippl, Personal Communication, 2020). At the same time, this
approach provided the opportunity to explore possible futures and solution elements without direct prescrip-
tive nature. This intention of being policy-relevant is something that has occurred in other literature as well
as in personal communication with Jock Martin, Personal Communication (2020); Cathy Maguire, Personal
Communication (2020) and further reference in Tuominen et al. (2014); van Vuuren (2015). In comparison,
the study by Geurs and van Wee (2004) and Giaoutzi et al. (2012) apply the backcasting approach to directly
66
==PAGE:80==
Chapter 7. Key Lessons Learnt
DTU Environment
support the policy decision process by generating concrete pathways with identiﬁed policy options. Thereby,
it can be concluded that the structure of a backcasting methodology is dependent on the desired purpose of the
application. For example, the generation of policies or policy packages along a pathway can ﬁnd application
in the context of policy-relevant or even in a policy development context. However, generating policy path-
ways might be undesirable when approaching the application from a research perspective. Further, it could
be argued that the purpose of a backcasting study is inﬂuenced by the design of the backcasting application
in terms of participation. The conduction of expert backcasts in comparison to the involvement of diﬀerent
groups of stakeholders has implications on the resources necessary, quality of the outcome of the study and
should be reﬂected in the designated purpose for the application. These learnings have been taken forward
in the context of the DTU backcasting methodology and are reﬂected in the underlying ﬂexible conception of
the method (see section 8).
7.3
Sustainability Conditions
The ﬁrst step of all reviewed case studies involved the generation of a vision and its related objectives. In a
broader context, this can be called the deﬁnition of success of a backcasting application (Karl-Henrik Robèrt,
Personal Communication, 2020; Göran Broman, Personal Communication, 2020). The deﬁnition of success is
a term more frequently used in regards to backcasting from principles as no concrete targets or goals in quan-
titative terms have to be set. The FSSD, for example, works from boundary rules on sustainable behaviour to
steer a path towards the desired future (Holmberg and Robèrt, 2000; Ny, 2009; Broman and Robèrt, 2017; Musse
et al., 2018). These are called sustainability principles. This concept was utilised in three publications from
1998 and 2000 by Holmberg (1998); Holmberg and Robèrt (2000); Holmberg and Robèrt (2000) and has since
been elaborated upon. Broman and Robèrt (2017) further add criteria to the generation of the sustainability
principles stating that they must be: necessary but not more to prevent unnecessary restrictions; suﬃcient
to cover all levels of sustainability; general so that they can be relatable and understandable across disciplines
and scales; concrete so that they are clear and resilient to guide innovation; and ﬁnally non-overlapping
to not inﬂuence one another and enable the formulation of indicator frameworks. The element of being non-
overlapping is also important so that indicators can be used to eﬀectually monitor the principles and to not
give certain conditions higher weighting. The idea of using sustainability conditions to represent limitations
of society and industry and thereby to progress in a sustainable manner is of interest to this thesis. Sustain-
ability principles are used to map the safe area of operation. This consideration would provide a fundamental
long-term environmental sustainability character to the backcasting methodology and is therefore relevant for
achieving the 2050 European Goals as outlined in the European Green Deal (European Commission, 2019a).
7.4
Analysis of the Current State
Many of the backcasting studies reviewed as well as additionally reviewed literature apply unique terminol-
ogy and approaches for analysing the current state. These include the identiﬁcation of internal and external
variables, leverage points and exogenous variables (as seen in (Robinson, 1990; Geurs and van Wee, 2004)).
67
==PAGE:81==
Chapter 7. Key Lessons Learnt
DTU Environment
Though the terminology and form of exercise may change, the intention is still the same: understand the
system of interest and identify key drivers. The purpose of this is threefold: (1) The greater the system
understanding the more accurate modelling (BAU and scenario modelling) can be conducted; (2) the identi-
ﬁcation of drivers helps, on one hand regarding the generation of future images and on the other hand to
inform the solution/policy generation; (3) wherein the system is it best to act (Giaoutzi et al., 2012; Broman
et al., 2013; Burch et al., 2014; Musse et al., 2018). Said element (3) closely relates to the concept of leverage
points by Donella Meadows (Meadows, 2010). The identiﬁcation of leverage points is relevant for the concept
of system understanding which in itself is crucial for scenario analysis (Jock Martin, Personal Communication,
2020; Cathy Maguire, Personal Communication, 2020). Therefore, it was concluded that a major component
of a backcasting methodology is the analysis of the current state as this step shapes the subsequent steps of a
backcasting application.
7.5
Uncertainty of Using Business-As-Usual Scenarios in
Backcasting Studies
Many of the backcasting studies reviewed in Chapter 5 make use of a business-as-usual scenario (Geurs and
van Wee, 2004; Svenfelt et al., 2011; Giaoutzi et al., 2012; Broman et al., 2013; Musse et al., 2018). This is often
to identify the gap between the desirable future and the predicted future. This is a paradox in itself as much
of the justiﬁcation for using a backcasting methodology is that the future is highly unpredictable and thus,
it cannot rely on forecasting techniques (John Robinson, Personal Communication, 2020). It is argued that a
change of mindset is required to achieve a desirable future, which can be found in backcasting (Robinson, 1988;
Robèrt et al., 2002). Furthermore, many authors including Börjeson et al. (2006) have identiﬁed that long-term
forecasting is inherently uncertain and as such oﬀering scenario analysis and backcasting as alternatives would
yield a better understanding of the future. Hence, using predictive scenarios to inform the normative scenarios
in itself is a paradox. This juxtaposition was reciprocated through communications with Prof. Dr. Robinson
John Robinson, Personal Communication (2020). He suggested the idea of multiple incommensurable baseline
scenarios. This concept takes into consideration multiple baselines based upon a range of identiﬁed trends
without preconceiving the likelihood of occurrence. This element could be compared to exploratory scenario
analysis due to its acknowledgement that there are multiple possible outcomes, which are equally unlikely
(John Robinson, Personal Communication, 2020). This idea was adopted in relation to the DTU backcasting
methodology. Nevertheless, to remain the ﬂexibility of the proposed methodology the option of assembling
a BAU scenario is included. An argument for the application of BAU scenarios is the associated quality of
gap analysis which could spark awareness amongst public and politicians. In conversation with Prof. Dr.
van Vuuren, it was determined that BAU scenarios can provide additional value in a backcasting exercise,
even though an additional amount of time would be required in the preparation of such. He argued that the
gap analysis is capable of exemplifying the implications between ’acting now’ and ’acting later’ which is a
common theme in the realm of climate change debate. Prof. Dr. van Vuuren further stated that through this
narration of possible implication a better narrative can be constructed through which the human behaviour
can be addressed (Detlef van Vuuren, Personal Communication, 2020).
68
==PAGE:82==
Chapter 7. Key Lessons Learnt
DTU Environment
7.6
Participatory Elements
The inclusion of external contributions in a backcasting application was apparent in near to all the reviewed
case studies. Second-order or participatory backcasting, as portrayed by Robinson (2003), relies on the inclu-
sion of a multitude of stakeholders during the backcasting application. The inclusion of participants provides
insight on what is important to the participants and what values they as a person or in their function of a
business, knowledge institution, political or societal representative hold. Therefore, it is agreed with the elab-
oration by Wilson et al. (2006) that participatory backcasting provides the means to generate a broad decision
context and thereby allows for strategic decision making based upon the values of the participants.
The terminology related to stakeholders was used with slight variations in terms of reviewed backcasting case
studies and needed clariﬁcation in each discussion with experts. As a guideline in this thesis, the statement by
Kok et al. (2006) is followed. Kok et al. (2006) deﬁne four groups of stakeholders which include policymakers,
business representatives, experts, and citizens/public. It is acknowledged that this deﬁnition is broad and de-
pending on the context of the application this deﬁnition can vary. As an example, the term stakeholder could
also be deﬁned following the approach taken by the European Environment Agency:
"A stakeholder is an entity that can be aﬀected by the results of that in which they are said to be stakeholders.
This includes:
1. Those whose interests are aﬀected by environmental problems or whose decisions have environmental
eﬀects;
2. Those who have information, resources or expertise required for policy formulation and strategy im-
plementation; and/or
3. Those who control key mechanisms for policy and strategy formulation and implementation." (Cathy
Maguire, Personal Communication, 2020)
This example shows the complexity assigned to the term ’stakeholder’ and the groups outlined by Kok et al.
(2006) can fall within multiple of the deﬁnitions outlined by the EEA. However, to retain the element of ﬂex-
ibility from a participatory perspective for the DTU backcasting methodology and to allow for a cohesive
stakeholder terminology in this thesis, it was decided to follow the terminology by Kok et al. (2006). Each
of the mentioned stakeholder groups has beneﬁts and limitations of consulting in a participatory setting and
thus, thorough consideration should be made on when and which stakeholders should be consulted.
A number of authors and experts argue that participatory backcasting is a unique tool to support and in-
crease social-learning, create buy-in from participants and to provide narrative to future scenarios (Robinson
et al., 2011; Sisto et al., 2016; Musse et al., 2018; Cathy Maguire, Personal Communication, 2020). Interac-
tions between actors and negotiators is a learning process which renders information not just towards the
practitioners of the study, but also towards the stakeholders. From the practitioners perspective, qualitative
69
==PAGE:83==
Chapter 7. Key Lessons Learnt
DTU Environment
information is gained which can give valuable information on how to proceed with the pathway generation.
On top of this, speciﬁc knowledge on the values, attitudes and perspective of the stakeholders can be attained
(Quist and Vergragt, 2006; Quist, 2013; John Robinson, Personal Communication, 2020). In addition, interact-
ing with stakeholders can also have the beneﬁt of increasing their knowledge on the topic and in some cases
can inﬂuence their behaviour and values towards certain solutions, trades-oﬀs and values Robinson (2003);
Bagheri and Hjorth (2007); Broman et al. (2013); Musse et al. (2018). However, the participation of stakeholders
brings limitations as every stakeholder group and every individual stakeholder brings diﬀerent perspectives,
personal values and abilities. The contribution to a backcasting exercise is therefore mostly qualitative and
restricted to certain elements of the method. It was observed in many case studies that reﬂections were pre-
sented on the use of participants. In the cases of Bagheri and Hjorth (2007); Musse et al. (2018), it is argued
that the social-learning process has to be guided by expert supervision when modelling tools are applied. This
is due to the complexity of the models and limitations that are linked to individual participants. In the context
of social-learning and involving stakeholders, Sisto et al. (2016) argue that the quality of results of the study
depends on the stakeholders involved and whether or not the same stakeholders are involved along the whole
process (Patel et al., 2007; Kok et al., 2011; Quist and Vergragt, 2011). Thereby, it could be argued that conduct-
ing multiple workshops should involve the same participants throughout to increase the consistency of the
backcast. Lastly, Ronge (2017) highlight that including diﬀerent stakeholder groups can lead to contrary and
biased contributions to the study, referring to the work by Sandström et al. (2016). These are valuable points
to keep in mind when considering applying participatory elements in a backcasting study.
Considering the type of stakeholder may also vary depending on which stage of the methodology the work-
shops are held. Take the stakeholder group ’public’. Consulting the public gives beneﬁcial information on the
values and perception of the future. This can be useful in setting the objectives of the study and determining a
shared future (Robinson, 2003; John Robinson, Personal Communication, 2020; Kasper Kok, Personal Commu-
nication, 2020; Julio A. Soria-Lara, Personal Communication, 2020). Thereby, it can be guaranteed that a broad
part of society will feel represented in the study and as a result, more identiﬁable narratives towards the future
may be generated. Furthermore, the public can inform decision-makers on what trade-oﬀs the public would
be willing to accept. This, in turn, helps to make transparent decisions (John Robinson, Personal Communi-
cation, 2020). Although, the use of citizens in scenario generation can be counterproductive, often suggesting
good but unrealistic ideas (Julio A. Soria-Lara, Personal Communication, 2020; Kasper Kok, Personal Commu-
nication, 2020). It can be argued that the context and scope of a backcasting application majorly inﬂuence the
relevance of the public in the process of backcasting. Especially local or regional projects should include the
public as the context and scope of the project are directly relatable to citizens and residents due to the geo-
graphical proximity (Kasper Kok, Personal Communication, 2020). Moving on, the role of experts is usually in
formulating solutions and in providing input on the feasibility of a scenario or pathway (Robinson et al., 2011;
van Berkel and Verburg, 2012). They further contribute in terms of delivering factual knowledge on scientiﬁc
and technical topics as well as in terms of social, economic and political matters. Their broad understanding
and knowledge in various ﬁelds often allow for deeper insights that would otherwise be absent. However,
experts can have diverse opinions and can misconstrue the public’s preferences due to their powerful position
and authority related to their profession (Kasper Kok, Personal Communication, 2020). In reference to policy-
makers, their participation is vital when trying to be policy-relevant and ﬁtting within a political framework.
70
==PAGE:84==
Chapter 7. Key Lessons Learnt
DTU Environment
They give vital insight into what is realistic within the political system as well as allowing transparency be-
tween the administrators of the study and the politicians. However, it must be considered that as with the
other stakeholders there is a political bias or agenda bias (Ronge, 2017; Kasper Kok, Personal Communication,
2020). Often it can be important to have policymakers in the objective setting and implementation workshops
as they have an acumen for feasibility (John Holmberg, Personal Communication, 2020). Finally, business
representatives must be considered. It is vital to consult with the business representatives as they have a large
role to play in the economy and thus, can give judgement on how actions will aﬀect the market. Furthermore,
because of their position in the economy, they can have a large amount of inﬂuence on the implemented
action and thus, it is important to engage in a dialogue to further understand the value of the market place.
This, in return, is vital information to convey to policymakers. For these reasons it would be useful to consider
business representatives, particularly when setting the objectives, assessing impacts and generating pathways.
It can be argued that the involvement of participants by itself includes an element of preference. This is
linked to the element of social learning and the fact that involving participants in the backcasting process
generates an element of buy-in and self-identiﬁcation with the work of the backcast (Cathy Maguire, Per-
sonal Communication, 2020). This element of inherent preference of pathways or future images is, however,
linked to the individual contributions of involved participants and their position. As learned from discussions
with experts, participatory settings bare risks of biased information and inﬂuence from diﬀerent stakeholder
groups (Kasper Kok, Personal Communication, 2020; John Robinson, Personal Communication, 2020). The
perceived preference towards pathways or future images might vary between participants depending on how
well they can personally, or as representative of an organisation or community, identify with generated out-
comes. Therefore, the preference assessment should consider these potential diﬀerent perspectives and at best
include external viewpoints to evaluate the generated outcomes to increase the quality of the results.
7.7
Use of Models
Models were frequently referred to in reviewed case studies to support an element of forecasting (in the form
of a baseline or BAU scenario) or exploratory scenarios. The provided information on the application and
implications of applied models was scarce. Only two case studies provide a comprehensible understanding
of their application of models (Broman et al., 2013; Musse et al., 2018). The case studies applied the model
in two ways. It was either applied to allow for predictions that fed directly into the process of the backcast
or the model was designed in collaboration and close discussion with the participants. The latter approach
yielded the eﬀect of social-learning. Through the interaction and direct input by participants, knowledge and
understanding was enhanced on the topic of investigation. As an additional consideration, it can be stated
that the design of models with participants could increase the acceptance of outcomes as the participants were
personally involved in the process. Both case studies (Broman et al., 2013; Musse et al., 2018) apply local or
regional models which do not exceed a combination of two models. A more complex approach was seen in
Giaoutzi et al. (2012) which apply a modelling framework consisting of 7 models. These models are scaled
to an EU level to generate forecasts in the agricultural system. This shows that including models in an EU
context is a complicated endeavour, which comes with considerably higher time and resources requirements
71
==PAGE:85==
Chapter 7. Key Lessons Learnt
DTU Environment
in comparison to the local system or sector-speciﬁc models.
In discussion with experts, the following beneﬁts in regards to using models were identiﬁed. The use of
models can contribute to assessing an element of risk related to generated scenarios (Will Steﬀen, Personal
Communication, 2020). This element is beneﬁcial to support the narrative related to scenarios and pathways
as it highlights the element of acting now vs. acting later (i.e. cost of delaying action (EEA, 2019a)) in a
quantitative way. Therefore, models are a good tool for public discussion and transparency towards the public
(Detlef van Vuuren, Personal Communication, 2020). Integrated models can further support setting long-term
targets and agendas. Additionally, they bare the potential to inform about lowest cost pathways which give
insight on the performance of pathways (van Vuuren and Hof, 2018). In regards to the use of models, it has
to be acknowledged that a knowledge gap remains. No clear assessment of conducted scenarios or pathways
was visible in reviewed case studies. The investigation of models in the context of backcasting lies outside of
the scope and was therefore not deepened.
7.8
Scenario Themes, Future Images and Choice of the
Combination Scenario
It is observed from the literature that the choice of the scenario themes or future images in backcasting stud-
ies varies. This variation is in terms of how realistic and exploratory they are. This could be observed in a
range of case studies as well as in the work by students in the teaching-based research activity (see Chapters
4 & 5. Take the images of the future by Geurs and van Wee (2004): high technology and high management.
Achieving such futures may be possible but is not considered a realistic future. This is because a future dom-
inated by one singular trend or driver is not able to reﬂect the complexity of future societies (John Robinson,
Personal Communication, 2020; Julio A. Soria-Lara, Personal Communication, 2020; Robin Hickman, Personal
Communication, 2020). However, this approach has the beneﬁt of exploring possible futures and how they can
be achieved (exploratory scenarios) (John Holmberg, Personal Communication, 2020). The downside to this is
that often the ﬁnal recommendation is a combination, stating that a mixture of the scenarios should be utilised.
This outcome of a backcasting application was frequently observed in the work by the students. Alternatively,
realistic scenarios or images of the future can be constructed. Arguably the term realistic is depending on the
deﬁnition of and subjective perception of the term. Taking the approach by Giaoutzi et al. (2012), the ﬁnal
scenarios considered, include: ’In Search of Balance - Accord on Sustainability’ and ’Active Regions - Reﬂexive
Lifestyles’. These two images present an idea or concept and thereby encompass a multitude of societal layers.
The level of detail that can be concluded from such scenario descriptions is still not suﬃcient to understand the
image of the future but gives room for interpretation and visualisation. These scenario themes provide space
for diﬀerent elements of a future image such as values and principles; lifestyle types; state of the environment;
economic, social and cultural dimensions; etc. As seen in case studies by Svenfelt et al. (2011), Robinson et al.
(2011) and Doyle and Davies (2013) diﬀerent forms of future images can be generated by the use of diﬀerent
tools and forms of media. A detailed description or visualisation of a future can close the gap between the
participant of the study and the narration of the storylines. This cannot be said for dominant exploratory
72
==PAGE:86==
Chapter 7. Key Lessons Learnt
DTU Environment
scenarios. The downside to less exploratory scenarios is that it may limit the solution generation since the
scenario space may not be fully mapped. For this reason, it could be suggested to conduct a scenario analysis
with a normative iteration (Robinson, 1990; Geurs and van Wee, 2004). Furthermore, other backcasting studies
like to avoid the use of scenarios entirely opting for a backcasting from principles approach to prevent being
boxed in Robèrt et al. (2002); Broman and Robèrt (2017).
When it comes to backcasting from principles, it was seen in Broman et al. (2013) that it is argued for a princi-
pled deﬁnition of success, which as previously described, avoids the possible conﬂict between participants of
an application when needing to agree on normative scenarios. However, as experienced in the context of the
teaching-based learning activity, the participants generating solutions and pathways towards the 2050 Euro-
pean vision and goals struggled to work without a clear image of the future. Major diﬃculties were observed
in approaching the pathway generation. Therefore, most groups intuitively moved towards backcasting from
scenarios by choosing overarching scenario ideas that followed set objectives (see section 4). Thereby, it can
be argued that the application of backcasting from scenarios is a fruitful approach in cases where the purpose
of backcasting is focused on the pathway aspect of the application.
Further, in the context of creating scenarios, it was observed that a range of case studies designed an odd
number of scenarios (Geurs and van Wee, 2004; Schippl and Leisner, 2009; Giaoutzi et al., 2012; Musse et al.,
2018). Commonly three scenarios were observed in reviewed case studies. In conversation with Prof. Dr.
Robinson it was gathered, that the choice of an uneven number of scenarios can unconsciously lead to a biased
decision making by participants in terms of preference of a scenario by choosing the "middle scenario" (John
Robinson, Personal Communication, 2020). Therefore, the number of scenarios should be an even number and
not allow for a "middle scenario" to avoid undesired bias.
7.9
Deﬁnition of ’What’, ’Levels of What’ and ’How’
It is crucial to understand how to deﬁne solutions so that they can be used to structure a coherent pathway.
From a conceptual perspective, scenarios and pathways can be broken down into ’whats’ and ’hows’. This
distinction was not clearly portrayed in reviewed case studies as described in Chapter 5. From the conversa-
tions with Prof. Dr. Robinson it became apparent that to structure the pathway generation it is essential to
make this distinction and create space for a conceptual approach to pathway generation if deemed relevant
for the administrators of the study (John Robinson, Personal Communication, 2020). Therefore, in the con-
text of the DTU backcasting methodology, it was decided to diﬀerentiate between the ’what’, it’s associated
’level of detail’ and the ’how’. Understanding this diﬀerence is helpful throughout the methodology. Figure
7.1 exempliﬁes the use of the relevant terms and the relationship to targets. The ’what’ is deﬁned as an event
or change that has to take place to generate an outcome related to a speciﬁc target or indicator. The ’what’
can be expanded by additional levels to provide further detail as well as providing diﬀerent possibilities in
which change can happen. As an example, a reduction of CO2 emissions from diesel cars to address GHG
emissions in the transport sector can come to fruition through technical means (e.g. engine modiﬁcations) as
well as through behavioural shifts (e.g. shift from individual to public transportation modes). Looking at these
73
==PAGE:87==
Chapter 7. Key Lessons Learnt
DTU Environment
solution elements it becomes clear that there are possible further levels of ’what’. Having a deeper look at the
given example, the shift from individual to public transportation can be further divided e.g. encouraging use
of buses or trains. These are still considered ’whats’ as they do not state ’how’ one can encourage the use
of buses and/or trains. A ’how’ describes the diﬀerent available options to translate the identiﬁed ’what’ and
its levels into action. To exemplify how actions could be implemented, increase in public transport through
encouraging the use of buses or trains could be approached through diﬀerent policy options, i.e. policy mea-
sures, economic incentives (e.g. ticket price reduction), etc. The question of ’how’ the actions are translated
and implemented is speciﬁcally addressed after the pathway generation in the context of the DTU backcasting
methodology.
Figure 7.1: Depiction of relationship between targets, identiﬁed actions and their associated levels of ’what’
as well as ’how’.
7.10
Pathway Generation and Solutions
Upon reviewing 15 backcasting case studies, it became apparent that the level of detail in regards to the de-
scription of reviewed methods vary majorly. Especially, information regarding the creation of pathways based
on backcasting from principles nor towards future images was well represented in the reviewed backcasting
literature. As found from reviewed case studies the generation of pathways is closely related to the number of
future images, i.e. one pathway leading to the respective future image. We perceive this observation counter-
intuitive as there are a multitude of possible pathway trajectories to arriving a future image. We believe that
there are merits in creating multiple pathways towards generated future images to elaborate on possible path-
way options and to enhance the backcast. In conversation with Prof. Dr. Robinson it became apparent that
the trajectory of a pathway will inﬂuence the perception of its preferability and therefore will play a role in
terms of policy relevance (John Robinson, Personal Communication, 2020). It can be argued that the number
of pathways will heavily impact the time required for the backcasting application, which might have been the
74
==PAGE:88==
Chapter 7. Key Lessons Learnt
DTU Environment
reason for observed limitations in reviewed case studies. In the following, several beneﬁcial concepts will be
summarised in the context of pathway generation.
Worth mentioning as a positive approach is the policy package and policy pathway approach taken by Giaoutzi
et al. (2012) adapted from the Banister (1998) report. This approach provides an understandable procedure that
can be applied intuitively across context and scale. However, as policy pathways were established towards
desired future images no exact path in accordance with time and milestones was speciﬁed. Thereby, it could
be argued that the choice on when identiﬁed solutions and policies have to be implemented is assigned to the
political institutions and therefore do not form part of the backcasting approach. Geurs and van Wee (2004)
use the concept of time paths which consider the time aspect of implementation and policy choices within
their adapted backcasting method (see section 5.3.1). Another useful take-away from the Giaoutzi et al. (2012)
case study is how they structured their pathways. It was determined what policy packages are fundamental
for all pathways and what policy packages are speciﬁc to individual images of the future. This is beneﬁcial
as it shows the recipient of the study what are the most critical packages that are resilient across all images
and thereby which options are available to readjust the trajectory towards established objectives and vision.
The basic concept of the policy package approach was taken forward in the context of the DTU backcasting
methodology to support the pathway generation in the form of ’solution packages’. The underlying concept
can be applied in the action and solution placing on a pathway as well as in assigning implementation options
to the pathway. Thereby, the concept is aligned to the outlined distinction between the ’what’ and the ’how’
of a pathway (for further detail refer to section 7.9).
Another beneﬁcial tool for generating pathways is the resource kit presented in Broman et al. (2013). A similar
approach was observed in Doyle and Davies (2013) and further suggested by Group 6 of the teaching-based
research activity in the form of a ’solution bank’ (Binderup et al., 2020). On one hand, it summarises identiﬁed
and available solutions with or without assigned implementation measures and on the other hand, provides
a range of useful tools that can be applied to support the solution and pathway generation process. Unfortu-
nately, in the context of this thesis, no full understanding of the resource kit was achieved due to restrictions
in access. However, the underlying concept is regarded as beneﬁcial as it provides structure in approaching
and generating ideas and solutions for achieving future images. The idea of a resource kit can be said to
be similar to the approach taken by group 6 of the teaching-based research activity. The idea of generating
solutions and gathering them in an understandable format can also be found in the classic approach of the
ABCD-methodology. Step C identiﬁes solutions that contribute to achieving set vision and objectives before
prioritising and assembling an action plan with a detailed strategy (Broman and Robèrt, 2017). The concept of
a ’solution bank’ was taken forward for the methodological development of the DTU backcasting methodology.
An additional approach to generating pathways that was followed in the DTU backcasting methodology is
based upon logically structuring pathways. A more detailed explanation of that idea is described in section
8.5.1 of the thesis. This approach was supported by a discussion with Dr. Damsø who in his own work has ap-
plied a similar approach by analysing the existing system and thereby ﬁnding solutions that necessarily have
to be put in place from a logical point of view (Tue Noa Jacques Damsø, Personal Communication, 2020). The
opposite approach to pathway generation can also be taken by excluding solution elements that will not con-
75
==PAGE:89==
Chapter 7. Key Lessons Learnt
DTU Environment
tribute to the overall achievement of set objectives and targets. This will reduce the complexity and number of
solutions needed to form the basic skeleton (i.e. actions for set milestones) of a pathway, whilst in an iterative
approach, synergistic solutions can be added to support the pathway generation. Thereby, side-eﬀects of solu-
tion elements can be counteracted or attempted to solve for. As an example, the installation of wind turbines
will impact landowners by covering parts of their land. In return, this impact could be minimised by providing
countermeasures, such as an economic incentive. The iterative element of the pathway generation is useful
also in the context of the overall backcasting application. The purpose is to adapt and to involve developments
over time and thereby to reiterate the process of backcasting. This element is further explained in section 7.12.
In discussion with Prof. Dr. McGlade another concept for generating pathways was gathered, which can be
described as breaking down the investigated system into smaller and thereby more detailed sectors of a system
(Jacqueline McGlade, Personal Communication, 2020). As an example the energy sector can be broken down
into energy production, electricity generation, energy infrastructure and at the same time further dimensions,
such as e.g. societal (i.e. human behavioural levels), economical and institutional. Through a further literature
search, the concept of PESTLE (political, economic, socio-cultural, technological, legal and environmental)
analysis came into the spotlight. Considering the political, economic, social, technical, legal and environmen-
tal sectors to construct scenarios. This approach, as seen in Gray et al. (2020), can be used to break down
scenarios into smaller categories or sectors to determine what must happen in the latter for the scenario to
unfold. This silo or sectoral working approach ultimately should be combined to create a holistic picture of
the system in focus.
Lastly, the concept of ﬂexible platforms towards the future is considered a relevant approach concerning the
pathway generation. This approach is inherently embedded in the ABCD-methodology (see section 3.4.3) and
was mentioned in conversation with Prof. Dr. Holmberg, Prof. Dr. Robèrt and related literature (Broman and
Robèrt, 2017; John Holmberg, Personal Communication, 2020; Karl-Henrik Robèrt, Personal Communication,
2020). The basic concept relates to the choice of action towards the future. The intention is to generate the
most eﬀective and resilient short-term actions with long-term perspectives. The goal is to determine solutions
which provide a good foundation for the following solutions to be built on top. The solutions should also
make signiﬁcant progress towards the objectives of the study. The ﬂexible platforms approach is designed
to be iterated. Since the future is uncertain, the approach utilised by The Natural Step does not formulate
complete scenarios, and as such pathways towards the objectives. Instead, the backcasting approach is iterate
to determine what is the next level of short-term action which is sustainable and makes progress towards the
long-term vision. As such a pathway is generated iteratively over time. Thus the key element is determining
which elements implemented in the short term are ﬂexible to prevent lock-ins. The beneﬁt of generating
pathways over the time horizon is that as time goes by the uncertain of the future decreases and as such the
decision are more informed.
76
==PAGE:90==
Chapter 7. Key Lessons Learnt
DTU Environment
7.11
Assessment
Across reviewed case studies, there have been two majorly diﬀerent assessment types. On one hand, cre-
ated scenarios and pathways underwent an impact assessment regarding diﬀerent criteria as e.g. social,
environmental and economic (Geurs and van Wee, 2004). Soria-Lara and Banister (2018) apply participatory
multi-criteria analysis as a tool based on relative environmental, social and economic indicators to assess long-
term sustainable transport options. Another example can be found in Schippl and Leisner (2009), arguing that
policy actions explored will have rebound eﬀects and hence, it is concluded that a technological assessment
with stakeholder involvement should be conducted to classify the eﬀects.
On the other hand, assessments were applied to quantify how an implemented solution or policy contributed
towards set objectives. As an example from presented case studies, Giaoutzi et al. (2012) use indicators for this
purpose as they further assess the performance of policy scenarios at a later stage of the backcasting applica-
tion. This is part of an iterative procedure.
It can be argued that there is a third type of assessment. The preference of stakeholders and the public towards
designed scenarios and certain elements of a pathway should also ﬁnd consideration. Following from personal
communication with Prof. Dr. Robinson, it became apparent, that while the future image has to be normative
on basis of chosen objectives, at the same time it has to be accepted by the majority of communities that will
be aﬀected in the future image (John Robinson, Personal Communication, 2020). As mentioned prior, Prof. Dr.
Robinson argued that preference and desirability of a scenario is partly a function of the outcome and partly
a function of how the pathway unfolds (John Robinson, Personal Communication, 2020). Therefore, assessing
preferences from all representatives should be considered on both elements of a scenario, i.e. the future image
and its respective pathway(s). This can be related to the number of pathways which are created towards a
future image. Across all case studies, it was observed that for each created future image only one pathway
was considered. In that case, it is diﬃcult to detach the preference from participants and stakeholders towards
both elements of a scenario. That is why in relation to the DTU backcasting methodology it is possible to
approach each future image from a multitude of pathway directions. This gathers a broader resolution of how
to arrive at a future desired state and how participants perceive the path towards the respective image.
7.12
Evaluation and Iterative Procedures in Backcasting
Backcasting from scenarios as known by Robinson (1990) includes an iterative procedure after the completion
of a backcasting study. This is to assure that made implementation choices are still beneﬁcial to the achieve-
ment of the objectives set out at the beginning of the study. Multiple case studies have adopted this mechanism
in varying context and with diﬀerent intentions. Case studies relying on a backcasting from principles ap-
proach applied iterations to the learning process in cross-sectoral environments as well as to the learnings
from individual steps of the backcasting method. The case study by JRC (2008) apply an iterative procedure
in the generation of policy packages and point out that iteration is necessary as subsequent steps of the ap-
plied backcasting method mutually inﬂuence one another. Furthermore, they argue that the identiﬁcation of
77
==PAGE:91==
Chapter 7. Key Lessons Learnt
DTU Environment
policy pathways are iterative and beneﬁt from external inputs by experts. Other studies install a dedicated
iterative process to evaluate progress towards the objectives. Musse et al. (2018) install an evaluation process
to generate a possibility for feedback into the scenario implementation in the ﬁnal step of their methodology
based upon the use of indicators. Thereby, it encouraged adjustments in case deviations to occur from targeted
objectives. Ronge (2017) apply backcasting from principles based on the FSSD and make use of the ABCD-
methodology by including a dedicated action plan which includes an evaluation mechanism. Depending on
the need of the study this tool can be applied to generate feedback loops that allow for further learnings or
to assess progress towards set targets. In conversation with several experts, it was echoed that backcasting
should not be seen as a ’one-shot’ operation but rather as a tool that can be applied throughout the years
with an iterative character. This allows to adapt to occurring changes along a pathway and thereby allows
to readjust the trajectory towards achieving the vision (Göran Broman, Personal Communication, 2020; Karl-
Henrik Robèrt, Personal Communication, 2020; John Holmberg, Personal Communication, 2020; Kasper Kok,
Personal Communication, 2020; Jacqueline McGlade, Personal Communication, 2020). Prof. Dr. applied the
analogy of shooting for the moon. It will not be possible to shoot to the moon without adjustments along
the way. Therefore, it was deduced that the element of iteration needs to be included as part of the DTU
backcasting methodology.
The process of evaluation and iteration are closely linked. By evaluating the progress towards set vision in
comparison to the current pathway feedback can be generated that provides useful information for an iteration
of the application (Cathy Maguire, Personal Communication, 2020). Dr. Maguire argued that it is crucial to
design which elements need evaluation and that this element is again relative to the intended purpose of the
backcasting exercise. In the context of the EEA backcasting could be applied in the domain of policy-support
by engaging with stakeholders to generate pathways towards the set vision as described in the European Green
Deal (European Commission, 2019a). However, the choice of which elements of a pathway will be implemented
lay within the policy-development domain of the European Union and thereby without the responsibility of
the EEA (Dionigi and Rasmussen, 2019; European Parliament, 2019). This aspects needs to ﬁnd consideration
in the design of the DTU backcasting methodology and is addressed in sections 8 & 8.7.
7.13
Duration and Quality of Backcasting Studies
One element that is not reﬂected in the backcasting literature is the time required to conduct a backcasting
study. Multiple case studies like e.g. Broman et al. (2013) and Musse et al. (2018) document that the conduction
and iteration of the study took several years. Thereby, it can be concluded that the level of detail and contained
steps of a backcasting method determine the associated time for completion. Meaning that the more structural
elements and activities a backcasting contains the more time it might require to conduct an application. In
communication with Jens Schippl, it became apparent that conducting a participatory backcasting study may
result in time loss if spent on the wrong elements of the study and thereby reducing the quality of the outcome
(Jens Schippl, Personal Communication, 2020). In conversation with Prof. Dr. Robinson, it became apparent
that the budget assigned to a project will majorly inﬂuence the conduction of a backcasting exercise and
thereby can steer the extent of a backcasting application (John Robinson, Personal Communication, 2020).
78
==PAGE:92==
Chapter 7. Key Lessons Learnt
DTU Environment
During the teaching-based research activity, the students in groups of four managed to develop pathways
towards the 2050 goals in a three week period. Another consideration is the quality and detail of the work
that was generated. Given the short time frame and other work the students had to consider, the pathways
generated by some groups achieved a signiﬁcantly good quality. However, the quality was not to a suﬃcient
level to eﬀectively inform policy. That being said all the pathways generated collectively provides lots of
useful information. This is due to the fact that there were a total of 80 students. The quality of pathways
can be lower in a participatory setting as the sheer number of pathways helps to generate collective quality.
However, to inform policy, credibility also needs to be considered. The use of modelling or participation
workshops can be used to add credibility to the conclusions of a backcasting study. For example, models
can inﬂuence as they can prescribe quantiﬁed impacts which is valuable to provide to politicians (European
Commission, 2017). Participatory workshops also provide credibility by presenting the collective options
of multiple-stakeholders (Kasper Kok, Personal Communication, 2020). However, providing these tools adds
time pressures on a backcasting study. Since no information is available of the duration of backcasting studies,
the expert network was consulted to determine from experience how long it takes to conduct a backcasting
study. Further time demands for the use of participant workshops and models were also discussed. The experts
indicated that backcasting studies can range from a few weeks to 3 years. Further, they indicated that often the
time taken to conduct a backcasting study is setting up and organising, i.e. generating the models, organising
the workshops and managing the stakeholder groups. An addition learning was the two approaches towards
conducting a backcasting study. The ﬁrst is a quick and iterative backcasting study and the second is long and
thorough. The quick and iterative approach towards a backcasting study will require considerably less time to
conduct, however, requires regular iterations (Jaco Quist, Personal Communication, 2020; Vergragt and Quist,
2011). Due to the variation in approaches, considering the time requirements for participation or modelling
techniques can further gauge how much time is required in the backcasting study. In regards to participatory
workshops, the expert network indicated that participatory workshops can vary in time requirement from 1
month to 6 months depending on the extent and number of workshops. As a general statement, it can be said
that typically they take 2.5 months to organise and operationalise per workshop. In regards to modelling, the
experts indicated that this element can take 4 months in the best case to a few years in longer cases. This
depends on the complexity of the models.
7.14
Further Lessons Learnt
Further learnings gathered through the process of reviewing case studies and interviewing experts relate
to (1) observed time aspect of conducting a backcasting study; (1) the role of governments; (2) variation in
terminology, (3) economical analysis and (4) little to no consideration of reliability and adaptability.
1. In personal communication with Dr. Julio A. Soria-Lara it became apparent that the outcomes of his
work for sustainable transport in Andalusia, Spain were not taken forward in terms of implementation
as initially planned. This was because the regional authority had experienced a shift in leadership and
therefore the outcomes of the study were not considered (Julio A. Soria-Lara, Personal Communication,
2020; Soria-Lara and Banister, 2018). This indicates that governmental leadership has a great inﬂuence
on the application of a backcasting study as well as on the implementation of a pathway.
79
==PAGE:93==
Chapter 7. Key Lessons Learnt
DTU Environment
2. Various authors acknowledged the discrepancy in regards to deﬁned and used terminology throughout
scenario literature (van Vuuren, 2015; John Robinson, Personal Communication, 2020; Kasper Kok, Per-
sonal Communication, 2020; John Holmberg, Personal Communication, 2020). The main discrepancies
were observed for the use of the term ’scenario’, which in the context of this thesis is deﬁned as a se-
quence of events leading up to an end-point. It consists of a description of the end-point (e.g. in the form
of a scenario theme or future image) and a sequence of events leading up to it. Another key learning
regarding the terminology is related to the use of ’predictive’ and ’projective’. As the term ’predictive’
indicates, here a prediction of future events is done based upon the likelihood of the event (Börjeson
et al., 2006). The term ’projective’ in comparison relates to a projection of the future which is based on
assumptions rather than an element of likelihood. Thereby, projective scenarios are used for exploring
the possible scenario space (Cathy Maguire, Personal Communication, 2020). The misuse of terms could
lead to a misunderstanding of backcasting approaches and was therefore uniﬁed for this thesis.
3. Upon reaching out to experts and reﬂecting on conducted teaching-based research activity, it was deter-
mined that the use of cost-beneﬁt analysis in a backcasting study can be considered counter-intuitive.
Cost-beneﬁt analysis is often used to justify the beneﬁts of taking action from an economical perspec-
tive. In discussion with Prof. Dr. Bøye Olsen, he argued that it is not always cost beneﬁcial to justify
an action or set targets due to discounting. Thus, since the targets are already deﬁned it is more ﬁtting
to conduct a cost eﬃciency analysis (Søren Bøye Olsen, Personal Communication, 2020). Using cost
eﬃciency allows to determine the most economically eﬃcient solutions as well as pathways due to the
set long-term objectives which thereby does not adhere to the concept of economic discounting (Søren
Bøye Olsen, Personal Communication, 2020).
4. Throughout the review of case studies it was noticed that the elements of resilience and adaptability
were in part considered to be inherently covered by using a backcasting approach. This is as backcasting
does not rely on predicting upcoming events but tries to provide elements to accommodate for possible
events and thereby identify the most resilient steps towards the desired vision (Vergragt and Quist,
2011; Damsø et al., 2014). Resilience can further be assessed and enhanced through the application of
system dynamics models which provide insight on system behaviour and sensitivity (Broman et al.,
2013; Musse et al., 2018). Adaptability can be achieved by designing viable pathway options that work
towards an array of diﬀerent normative future images, thereby allowing to diverge and adapt from
current a pathway if needed (Broman et al., 2013; John Holmberg, Personal Communication, 2020).
Nevertheless, in the context of this thesis, both factors of resilience and adaptability are considered as
crucial elements in regards to ﬁnding solutions and forming pathways.
80
==PAGE:94==
Chapter 8
The DTU Backcasting Methodology
This chapter presents the proposed backcasting methodology, hereby coined the ’DTU backcasting method-
ology’ (DTU - Danmarks Tekniske Universitet). It is designed to be adaptable and thus, ﬁt with the needs
and resources available of whomever administrator the backcasting application. The methodology should be
applied according to the chosen purpose and involved participants as further explained in the following para-
graphs. The method has core and optional sub-steps. The core sub-steps oﬀer a streamlined path through
the method and contain the necessary elements required to conduct a valid backcasting study. The optional
sub-steps are recommended to conduct a robust methodology and to elevate the information gained from
backcasting. The DTU backcasting methodology is divided into seven structural elements which can be seen
in Figure 8.1, these correspond to the seven subheadings in this chapter. The fully expanded methodology
including the optional and mandatory steps displayed in Figure 8.2. The argumentation and instructions rel-
evant for each step are provided throughout. To further the understanding of the reader a number of steps
are illustrated in form of ’example boxes’. These illustrations are presented where possible and relevant in
regards to the scope of this thesis. At the end of the chapter, the role of stakeholders in regards to the pro-
posed methodology is described. It is relevant to mention that the DTU backcasting methodology is a result
of the ﬁndings and learnings presented in Chapter 7 and thereby follows the deﬁnition of certain terms and
concepts.
Application Choices of the DTU Backcasting Methodology
The application of the DTU backcasting methodology should come with two initial considerations on:
• Who is administering/conducting the study?
• For which purpose is the study intended?
The DTU backcasting methodology in its full expansion is designed to provide a multitude of options and
thereby support a complete policy development process. The last step of the methodology enables feedback
which then sparks iterations of the method by leading back to prior steps. The conduction of the complete
DTU methodology entices a range of purposes, on which the administrator of the study have to decide depend-
ing on their available resources, level of inﬂuence and participants that partake in the exercise. It is relevant
to mention that in regards to advancements in the ﬁeld of backcasting, the sub-step implementation options
might lay outside the intended purpose for consideration in an EU policy context. In the case of the European
Environment Agency, such a backcasting method as proposed might not ﬁnd application to its full extent. This
is due to the institutional setting which needs to ﬁnd consideration as certain steps of the methodology lay
within the typical policy development context. Speciﬁcally, the sub-step ’implementation options’ and struc-
tural element 7 ’evaluation and iteration’ lay within a policy development context. This consideration at an EU
81
==PAGE:95==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
level is necessary as policy development lays within the responsibility of the European Council, Parliament
and Commission as described by the ordinary legislative procedure (Dionigi and Rasmussen, 2019; European
Parliament, 2019). In this context, the purpose of conducting a backcasting exercise is not in identifying and
implementing policy measures, but rather to support the policy development process with useful information
(Cathy Maguire, Personal Communication, 2020). This information can be generated from diﬀerent steps of
the proposed DTU methodology, even if administrators of the study choose to exclude the identiﬁcation of
policy options and evaluation. The consideration of who will be involved as participants in the study and at
which step of the methodology, requires a separate reﬂection, which can be found in section 8.8.
To elaborate on the structure of the following sections: The main structural elements are presented as a
separate section (i.e. 8.1 Setting Objectives), whereas sub-steps of each structural element are designated with
numbered sub-sections (i.e. 8.1.1 Scope, Vision, Goals and Targets). Among the 7 structural elements, further
paragraphs for elaboration and deﬁnitions are inserted which are not numbered.
Figure 8.1: The DTU backcasting methodology designed to ﬂexibly support policy framework for local, re-
gional, national and international governance. The numbering and ﬂows indicate order and direction in which
the seven steps of the methodology should be conducted.
The individual structural elements and their sub-components can be seen in Figure 8.2 and are fully elaborated
in the following sections. This leads on to a discussion of the method as a whole, highlighting the diﬀerent
advantages and disadvantages of applying the methodology in a policy supportive context.
