8.1
Setting Objectives
Setting the objectives of the study are at the core of every encountered backcasting methodology and are
characterising the approach taken by the applicants of the method. As a ﬁrst step the two crucial elements
of ’Scope, Vision, Goals and Targets’ and ’Sustainability Conditions’ give guidance for the conduction of the
application. This is where the possible outcomes of the study are framed and where decisions are made about
what the future shall entail, i.e. what is the envisioned state in the future and what targets shall be achieved
until set target year. The set objectives will guide and steer the choices and actions along a pathway that is
necessary and have to be put in place to arrive at the envisioned end-point.
8.1.1
Scope, Vision, Goals and Targets
The initial task for conducting the ﬁrst structural element of the backcasting method lies in setting the Scope,
which determines the scale and limitations as well as the duration and timeline of the study. The Vision rep-
resents the overall mantra or guiding principle describing the desired state for chosen end-point in time. This
encompasses a description of what society, systems and relationships in the future shall look like and what is
aspired to become reality. The vision is agreed upon by the conductors of the study and can be generated in
diﬀerent ways, e.g. through participatory discussion and dialogues, scientiﬁc reasoning or adoption of existing
targets by organisations, governments or society. The vision is the overall guiding principle of the study and
therefore stands as a guiding statement at the beginning of the exercise. Setting Goals is closely linked to the
vision as it elaborates in more detail what has to be achieved to translate the described vision into reality. This
understanding is deepened by setting Targets which relate the goals to quantiﬁable achievements that have
to be reached as set out by the scope of the study. The outcome of the study is normative, meaning desirable
only if all of the outset elements: Vision, Goals and Targets are achieved and fulﬁlled.
84
==PAGE:98==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
Example box: Setting the scope, vision, goals and targets in the context of the EU energy system.
The overall vision from the EU green deal, echoed from the 7th Environment Programme, is: "In 2050,
we live well, within the planet’s ecological limits. Our prosperity and healthy environment stem from an
innovative, circular economy where nothing is wasted and where natural resources are managed sustain-
ably, and biodiversity is protected, valued and restored in ways that enhance our society’s resilience. Our
low-carbon growth has long been decoupled from resource use, setting the pace for a safe and sustainable
global society." European Commission (2013, 2019a)
The goals can be taken from ’A Clean Planet for All’ (European Commission, 2018). The EU aims to be
climate-neutral by 2050 – an economy with net-zero greenhouse gas emissions. This objective is at the
heart of the European Green Deal and in line with the EU’s commitment to global climate action under
the Paris Agreement.
And ﬁnally targets can be constructed from the supporting literature (European Commission, 2018):
• At least 40% cuts in greenhouse gas emissions (from 1990 levels) by 2030.
• At least 32% share for renewable energy by 2030.
• At least 32.5% improvement in energy eﬃciency by 2030.
• 0 (net) carbon emission by 2050.
8.1.2
Sustainability Conditions
The second task of the ﬁrst structural element is to deﬁne sustainability conditions. Deﬁning the sustain-
ability conditions is a vital step relevant for applications of the DTU backcasting methodology. This concept
is taken from the work on sustainability principles deﬁned by Holmberg and Robèrt (2000) and further de-
veloped by Broman and Robèrt (2017). The purpose of using sustainability conditions is to deﬁne the safe
operating space and as long as the conditions are met, it can be certain that steps are being made towards
sustainable development. To deﬁne the sustainability conditions, the practitioner of the study must consider
what elements of sustainability are going to be considered. This can include the three pillars of sustainability:
social, economic and environmental (Giddings et al., 2002). It is possible to deliberate from certain pillars of
sustainability, however, as a minimum the environmental and social dimensions of sustainability should be
considered. The deﬁnitions of the sustainability conditions do not necessarily have to be quantitative. How-
ever, they must be suitable for an indicator framework to be generated if considered relevant. This allows to
assess the impacts of chosen pathways or solutions in a subsequent step, to achieve the outlined objectives in a
system encompassing way as well as being able to evaluate their performance. This is an important step as the
deﬁnition of sustainability generated in this step must be robust for the duration of the study. Furthermore,
there are numerous deﬁnitions of sustainability in literature (Vos, 2007; Alhaddi, 2015). Thus, to simplify the
problem Broman and Robèrt (2017) states criteria should be used to generate the deﬁnitions of sustainability
85
==PAGE:99==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
across the backcasting studies. This criterion is discussed in section 7.3, however, to reiterate, the criteria for
sustainability conditions along with examples are stated as: Necessary, Suﬃcient, General, Concrete and
Non-Overlapping (Broman and Robèrt, 2017).
Example Box: Examples on sustainability conditions in the context of the EU energy system.
For this example, the principles of sustainability by Broman and Robèrt (2017) and The Natural Step
Canada (see sections 3.4.3) are applied as follows:
1. Action must not result in the systematic increase of concentrations of substances extracted from
the Earth’s crust (for example, heavy metals and fossil fuels)
2. Action must not result in the systematic increase of concentrations of substances produced by
society (for example, plastics, dioxins, PCBs and DDT)
3. Action must not result in the systematic physical degradation of nature and natural processes (for
example, overharvesting forests, destroying habitat and overﬁshing)
4. Action must not result in conditions that systematically undermine people’s capacity to meet their
basic human needs (for example, unsafe working conditions and not enough pay to live on)
Another approach is the doughnut economics model put forward by Raworth (2017). The doughnut
economics model deﬁnes a safe operating space where the thresholds of the nine planetary boundaries
(from the Stockholm Resilience Centre) are not exceeded, yet all 12 social foundations outlined by United
Nations SDG’s are achieved. The planetary boundaries include ecological boundaries such as climate
change, ocean acidiﬁcation, land conversion etc. The 12 social boundaries include standards on elements
such as access to safe drinking water, social equality, income and work, etc.
8.2
Understanding the Current State
Following ’setting objectives’, the second step of the method is conducted via two sub-steps ’System Under-
standing and Interactions’ and ’Current Trends and Leverage Points’. The purpose of this step in the context of
the DTU methodology is to document the conditions of the system at the time in which the study is being con-
ducted. This step is vital for all backcasting studies for many reasons. Firstly, this step provides information
on the level of action that is required by considering the gap between the current state and the objectives of
the study. Secondly, the information gathered in this section is required to understand the problem and iden-
tify critical variables (critical values that can change, depending on conditions), leverage points and drivers.
In plain text, these are places to intervene in the system to have eﬀective impacts. Finally, it is important to
understand the current state of the problem or system so it can be modelled. If models are not being used it is
still vital to understand the system interactions to avoid burden shifting. All this information can be fed into
the following steps of the methodology and inform the pathways towards the objectives of the study.
86
==PAGE:100==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
8.2.1
System Understanding and Interactions
The ﬁrst step is to describe the system or problem to a high degree of resolution. The description must be
detailed enough so that disregarded elements do not reveal themselves further down the line and reduce the
credibility of the pathways. The ﬁrst sub-step should be answering the following questions:
• What is the state of the system or problem?
• What is the gap between the current state and the ’objectives of the study’?
• What are the key drivers and variables having an inﬂuence on the system and/or problem?
• Which existing and ongoing events are contributing positively to achieving this goal and which are
deemed counterproductive?
• Where are the leverage points in the system?
To fully comprehend the current state, it is crucial to apply system understanding and identify crucial sys-
temic relationships and interconnections. The interconnections can be both direct and indirect and thus, can
be complex. One approach is to model the system or problem, this is addressed by integrated and system
dynamics models. This renders valuable information on dependencies and ﬂow of resources. Where possible
the current state of the system should be quantiﬁed, alternatively qualitative analysis can be used to describe
the system or problem and its interactions. Once the system has been outlined, the gap between the current
state and the objectives must be considered. Where possible this gap should be quantiﬁed, however, this is
often easier said than done. In many areas, quantitative targets are not set and in reality, there are often gaps
in the data for the current state. The gap between the current state and the objectives indicates the level
of action that is required and thus, informs the scale of change when identifying solutions and structuring
pathways. Next, it is essential to identify key drivers and variables which are having adverse impacts on the
achievement of the objectives. The driving forces are the main factors that cause something to happen. For
example, demand for land and timber are two of the key drivers behind deforestation. Drivers are important
to identify as policy and solutions may intervene to inﬂuence these driving forces. The identiﬁcation of key
variables which aﬀect the system or problem provides further understanding of how to potentially tackle the
situation. The next consideration is the current state of action which is being taken to address the system
or problem. To generate coherent solutions, the current action must be understood. This is to gain insight
into what has been done, what has worked and what has not. Finally, the concept of leverage points should
be considered. The concept of leverage points introduced by Meadows (2010) highlight where action should
be taken that has the leverage, i.e small action can result in large impacts and thus, progression towards the
objectives. There are some tools which can use to support the analysis in the step, these include, integrated
assessment models, DPSIR analysis as well as SWOT (Strengths, Weaknesses, Opportunities, Threats) analysis.
87
==PAGE:101==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
Example Box: System Understanding and Interactions in the context of the EU energy system. This
provides examples on how to address the fundamental questions outlined prior.
The energy sector was responsible for 26% of the 2017 greenhouse gas emissions in Europe. However,
emissions from the energy sector have seen a decline by 32% compared to 1990 levels (EEA, 2019b).
European ﬁnal energy consumption reached an astonishing total of 1,060 million tons of oil equivalents
in 2017, of which 31% was attributed to the transport sector, 27% to households, 25% to industry, 15%
to commercial and public services and the remaining 3% can be attributed to other sources. Of the
total ﬁnal energy consumption in 2017, 37% of this consumption was from oil, compared to 23% from
electricity (EEA, 2020a). In 2018 the overall share of energy from renewable sources reached 18%, of
which 37% can be attributed to hydropower, 34% to wind, 12% to solar, 8% to bio-fuels and 9% to other
renewable energy sources (eurostat, 2020).
Developments in the energy sector can have a knock-on eﬀect on other European systems such as
food and mobility. For example, achieving a high tech renewable energy economy has raw material
dependencies. Besides, certain energy sources such as biomass can have trade-oﬀs related to land use
and biodiversity loss. This will impact the availability of land for food production (EEA, 2019a). The
energy system has a large interaction with the transport sector and as such a shift to all-electric modes
of transportation will add to the ﬁnal energy consumption resulting in additional pressures on the
energy network. This interactions towards adjacent systems are important to acknowledge.
The EEA (2019a) states that Europe is largely on track to meet the 20% renewable energy mix in
2020. Thus, to meet the 2030 target of 32% renewable energy, an additional 12% of renewable energy
production is required. Furthermore, to meet net carbon neutrality by 2050, 1,060 million tons of oil
equivalents (from 2017 levels) must be decarbonised.
Key drivers of the energy consumption originate from the use of fuel in the transport sector; household
consumption in the form of electricity and heating; as well as industrial usage.
"EU greenhouse gas emissions have decreased by about 22% in the past 27 years due to the combined
result of policies and measures and economic factors. The carbon and energy intensity of the EU
economy is lower now than it was in 1990 because of improvements in energy eﬃciency and the
use of less carbon-intensive fuels, especially renewable energy sources. Transport remains one of the
biggest challenges ahead to decarbonising the economy" (EEA, 2019a). The Renewable Energy Directive
(2009/28/EC) has so far been eﬀective at target setting and aiding the energy transition.
Finally, in regards to leverage points (Meadows, 2010), there are a few paradigm shifts that could come
into play such as a switch to all-electric transportation or all nuclear power. Examples of further leverage
points include setting more targets, more advanced information ﬂows and changing the structure of the
energy system.
88
==PAGE:102==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
8.2.2
Current Trends and Drivers of Change
The second sub-step identiﬁes current trends in the system as well as drivers of change. The drivers of change
are trends which prompt a motivation to change. These can be global trends such as climate change or local
trends such as geopolitical developments. The drivers of change highlight the importance of why action must
be taken. Other trends that need to be considered are related to the drivers and variables that have an impact
on the system or problem. These trends can have both positive and negative eﬀects on the system and thus,
give vital information on how trends can aid and hinder future developments. The identiﬁcation of observed
dominant trends or so-called mega-trends that can further provide useful information about realistic future
developments. The knowledge generated in this step is a precursor to the scenario building step, where it
can be used to inform all the scenario types. Further, it allows assumptions to be made on how it will aﬀect
drivers and variables in the system, and as such, can be modelled or used as input for the scenario techniques.
The identiﬁcation of these trends can be achieved through trend analysis, investigation and literature research
about ongoing societal (including behavioural), environmental, technical, economical and systemic changes.
Example Box: Current trends and drivers of change in the context of the EU energy system.
Two of the six key drivers of change, that are identiﬁed by EEA (2019a), push for developments in the
European energy system. These are ’global climate change and environmental degradation’ as well as
’growing urbanisation and migrating global populations’. Greenhouse gas projections indicate the 2030
climate and energy targets are not going to be met. This is in turn related to rising energy consumption
trends. Hence, action must be taken to reduce emissions. The trend description in EEA (2019a) is based
on global mega-trends analysis by 7 diﬀerent EU member state reports. The reports took outset in the
methodological toolkit by EEA (2017).
Following the presentation in the EEA (2019a), further trends important to consider for future develop-
ments include:
∙Diverging global pollution trends
∙Urbanisation trends
∙Accelerating technological change
∙Continued economic growth
∙Intensiﬁed global competition for resources
∙Growing pressures on ecosystems
∙Increasingly severe consequences of climate change
∙Diversifying approaches to governance
89
==PAGE:103==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
8.3
Scenario Building
Step 3 of the methodology, ’scenario building’, consists of three sub-steps out of which two are optional. The
purpose of this step is to generate scenarios (end-points and the sequence of events, i.e. pathways leading up
to them) to gather deeper system understanding on possible futures and identify solutions and threats that can
help inform the ’solutions’ step of the study. There are beneﬁts of using the three scenario types: predictive,
normative and exploratory (Börjeson et al., 2006). The sub-step titled ’normative scenarios’ is at the core of
this structural element. The additional elements of ’predictive scenarios’ and ’exploratory scenarios’ can be
used to enhance this part of the method as they yield additional information that can be used to expand the
horizon of the study.
The minimum standard for generating these scenarios requires an extensive literature review carried out in-
ternally. However, involving experts and stakeholders is vital to increase transparency and legitimacy of the
scenarios and thus, it is recommended that this step should involve participation. Workshops or the use of
participatory tools should be utilised to engage with participants. A relevant concept to identify stakeholders
is stakeholder analysis, which can further be explored in Varvasovszky and Brugha (2000); Reed et al. (2009). If
participation is used at this stage then it is vital to convey the ﬁndings of the prior steps so the participants are
fully informed. Furthermore, research and modelling can be conducted to further aid the scenario discussion
and assess the desirability of the scenarios.
Since scenarios are being considered in this section it is important to refer back to the ’whats’ and ’hows’
discussed in section 7.1. When determining actions/elements within a scenario, as a minimum ’whats’ should
be included. i.e. what events must occur for a scenario to unfold (e.g. GHG emission reduction in the transport
sector to achieve a sustainable transport scenario). How these events unfold (e.g. via tax, voluntary programs,
etc.) is separate and should only be included if the implementation is to be considered in the latter step of
the DTU methodology (see section 8.5.2). This is particularly important for considering solutions that help
achieve the objectives of the study.
The next consideration is in regards to the level of detailed required for the pathways. It is important to con-
sider as it can vary depending on the purpose of the study as well as whether participation is conducted. In the
example in section 8.3.2, the level of detail in the scenario is low. This is because 64 pathways where received
from the students in the teaching base research activity and as such, the quantity of information gathered was
signiﬁcantly large. If participation isn’t being used, then more time and detail in the pathways is required to
gather the same quantity of information. Models can be used to increase the quality and detail in the scenarios.
Finally, the scale of scenarios (global to local) should be chosen in relation to the scope in ’setting objectives’.
Therefore, the scale of the scenarios should be decided upon by the administrator of the study based on the
context and purpose of the backcasting application. However, it has to be acknowledged that associated
uncertainties increase with the scale of scenarios as information and resolutions is often lost as the scale
increases (Robinson, 2003; Walker et al., 2003; Ericksen et al., 2009).
90
==PAGE:104==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
Cross-over between Scenario Types
It is important to mention the distinction and cross-overs between the predictive, exploratory and normative
scenarios. Predictive scenarios take into consideration the probability of occurrence. Normative scenarios do
not consider the probability of occurrence and instead only acknowledge scenarios in which the objectives of
the study have been achieved. The exploratory scenarios do not consider probability nor desirability, however,
focus on investigating what is possible in the future. Nevertheless, exploratory scenarios follow the ideology
that the future is inherently uncertain and thus, one must be prepared that anything can happen. As a con-
sequence, embedded in the scenario exploration both predictive and normative scenarios can be found. This
is illustrated in Figure 8.3. All scenarios whether normative, non-normative or predictive (baseline scenarios)
lie within the possible scenario space. It is important to mention Figure 8.3 further considers the concept of
realism in scenario generation. To structure eﬀective pathways scenarios must also be realistic. However,
in this step, the purpose is to generate normative solutions and thereby, is not concerned about generating
detailed and structured pathways. This concept is important for the latter step, ’pathway generation’ and is
discussed in section 8.5.1.
Figure 8.3: Adapted version of the futures cone by Hancock and Bezold (1994). Within the space lies possible
scenarios of the future. The closer to the centre of the cone the more realistic the scenarios. This is not to
be confused with probability, as forecasting the future is inherently uncertain. For this reason, a business-as-
usual scenario is not illustrated in the cone, instead, multiple baselines are considered. Within the possible
scenarios, there are preferred, realistic, normative and non-normative scenarios.
8.3.1
Predictive Scenarios [Optional]
Although this step is optional, it is presented ﬁrst as it can help inform both the normative and exploratory
sub-steps by increasing understanding of issues and drivers. The sub-step ’predictive scenarios’ focuses on
scenarios based upon predictions or forecasting methods. In this regard, two concepts are considered by the
91
==PAGE:105==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
DTU backcasting methodology: ’business-as-usual scenarios’ and ’multiple incommensurable baseline sce-
narios’. A typical ’business-as-usual scenario’ (or also called ’baseline scenario’) assumes that the current
situation can be extrapolated in a predictable fashion under considerations of existing long-term plans and
policies. Relying on forecast methods is by deﬁnition subject to high levels of uncertainty and in the context of
complex systems nearly impossible to predict (Robinson, 1990; Dreborg, 1996). The validity of such scenarios
is short-lived (Raskin et al., 2002) and negates the consideration of diﬀerent possible baselines – the so-called
multiple incommensurable baselines. A prime example of why conducting a BAU scenario is not a resilient
approach is the occurrence of wild cards. Wild-cards are low probability, high impact events (Barber et al.,
2006) and as such are often overlooked in BAU scenarios. This is generally acceptable for studies with a short
time span, however, over longer durations the chances of a wild-card occurring is higher and thus, could yield
the wrong conclusions. A good example is the occurrence of worldwide pandemic nCOVID-19 in 2019/2020.
Although there is a high likelihood of pandemics emerging in the future and pandemics are indicated as one
of the mega-trends outlined in EEA (2019a); the probability of occurrence within a short time frame was pre-
viously considered relatively low (Brown and Arminaite, 2016). Most conducted BAU scenarios would have
lost its validity with the occurrence of such events or sudden systemic changes (Brown and Arminaite, 2016).
However, due to the wide-spread use of these types of scenarios, it can also be utilised in the context of the DTU
methodology with consideration and acknowledgement of its associated high levels of uncertainty. This is an
approach that was observed in three case studies (Robinson et al., 2011; Broman et al., 2013; Musse et al., 2018),
which was discussed in chapter 7.7. The predictive scenarios can be beneﬁcial as they can inform the study
on the urgency and scale of action that is required to achieve the objectives of the study. Correspondingly,
it is recommended to take the perspective provided through ’multiple incommensurable baseline scenarios’.
To reduce the uncertainty of predictive forecasting, multiple baselines are inspired by current mega-trends
which, when extrapolated, could dominate diﬀerent futures. Thus, analysing a variety of trends in many sys-
tems, sectors and regions of society will yield a multitude of information compared to a BAU scenario. This is
useful to consider as it does not preconceive the likelihood of any trends, yet explores the feasibility space in a
trend-based manner. An example of using multiple incommensurable baseline scenarios could be forecasting
the best and worst-case scenarios (Önkal et al., 2013) or forecasting promising trends and mega-trends (Agami
et al., 2008). The current state and trends as well as underlying drivers which were analysed in a previous step
of the method, feed into this sub-step of ’scenario building’.
The outcome of the predictive scenarios should be able to answer a set of questions, which is listed below.
The information gathered from these questions should be compiled and well document by the administrator
of the study and subsequently fed into the ’solutions’ step.
• What do the predictive futures look like?
• What happens to the state of the issues and drivers based upon the predictions?
• What solution can be identiﬁed that will deal with the emerging issues and drivers?
92
==PAGE:106==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
8.3.2
Normative Scenarios
The structural element ’normative scenarios’ is set as mandatory for the DTU backcasting methodology. This
is as the subsequent elements of the methodology take results from the scenarios generated in this step. Con-
sidering normative scenarios allows solutions or pathways that proceed this step, to be in progression to
achieving the objectives. This enhances the solutions gathered in the following step of the methodology. Gen-
erating normative scenarios can be done by constructing images of the future that align with set objectives.
Meaning as a minimum, the vision, goals and targets should be fulﬁlled in these images. Furthermore, it is
important to consider the information gained from ’understanding the current state’ addressing key drivers,
variables as well as leverage points to bridge the gap between the current state and the objectives. A consid-
eration of the ’sustainability conditions’ is not mandatory at this point of the application, this consideration
comes later and is based on a high level of complexity and will aﬀord additional time and resources. The
normative scenarios must meet the following criteria:
1. The scenarios must achieve the targets outlined in ’setting objectives’.
2. The scenarios must be possible and can have comparably extreme tendencies (exploratory).
3. Feasibility does not need to be considered in these scenarios.
4. Each scenario should be signiﬁcantly contrasting to one another.
5. There should be a large range of scenarios with the aim to explore many possibilities. However, only a
few manageable numbers of scenarios must be considered to reduce complexity.
6. The scenarios must be concerning the particular system of the study.
The minimum requirement in regards to structuring scenarios in this sub-step is to identify solution elements
along the pathway to an image of the future. However, the administrators of the study can choose to conduct
comprehensible studies and analysis of full-breadth scenarios, which provide further consideration and struc-
ture to the pathways and thus, will yield better-informed solutions. This will come at a more time-intensive
cost. Another consideration is the scale of scenarios. Thus, by the end of the of sub-step the following ques-
tions should be addressed:
• What could possible normative futures look like?
• ’What’ key events must happen in the future to end up in the given scenarios?
This information is then compiled by the administrator of the study and is fed into the ’solutions’ step.
93
==PAGE:107==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
Example Box: Generation of normative scenarios in the context of the EU energy system until 2050.
First, it is important to consider scenario themes or images of the future, these provide a narrative
to form pathways. Some scenario themes that are commonly found in literature include: top-down,
bottom-up approaches, high technology and lifestyle changes.
These scenario themes appear
frequently in literature as they are signiﬁcantly contrasting from each other and as such cover a
large area within the normative space of the scenario cone (see Figure 8.3). The normative scenarios
generated by the students during the teaching-based research activity give a good example of normative
scenarios. Their scenarios were generated from literature reviews as well as group discussion. Take the
following example from group 18 (Mortensen et al., 2020): A high technology scenario - switching from
fossil fuel to renewable energy and limiting energy consumption. This example is rather detailed and
quantiﬁes the level of reductions that must take place on a ﬁve-year basis.
2020: Actions should be taken to change wood pellets and fossil fuel boilers to heat pumps. Old
buildings should be insulated to get better energy eﬃciency. Investment to bio-energy should also be
taken into consideration.
2025: 30% of wood pellets and fossil fuel boilers will be exchanged by heat pumps with the support of
government funding and limitation on the usage of fossil fuels. New technology and district heating
systems should be built in high heat density areas to reduce the pressure of electricity supply. With the
development of biotechnology, biomass gasiﬁcation facilities and bio-ethanol plants can be combined
with ethanol vehicles.
2030: 45% of wood pellets and fossil fuel boilers will be exchanged by heat pumps.
2035: 50% of wood pallets and fossil fuel boilers will be exchanged by heat pumps, and high-eﬃciency
heat pumps should account for a larger proportion of the market.
Biomass transaction should be
inspired in the EU as there is an imbalance in agricultural areas between diﬀerent countries.
2040: 75% of individual heating should come from electric equipment. District heating systems should
be well connected to make it possible to rely more on renewable energy, which is caused by the regional
imbalance of renewable energy.
New buildings must achieve energy-saving standards.
Increasing
funding for bio-energy to make sure the supply of bio-energy is stable.
2045: 90% of individual heating should come from electric equipment rather than boilers. Increase in
eﬃciency of renewable energy resulting in increased electricity and hence heat demand can be satisﬁed
by renewable energy and biomass.
2050: Aims at no wood pellets and fossil fuel boilers. All buildings will live up to the energy-saving stan-
dards. Continued investments into bio-energy production to increasing the output of zero to negative
carbon energy.

==PAGE:108==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
8.3.3
Exploratory Scenarios [Optional]
The purpose of this sub-step is to conduct scenario analysis to explore possible scenarios which could impact
the ability to achieve the objectives of the study. This sub-step utilises scenarios which are generated through
assumption based projections. This type of scenario is vital to design robust and adaptive solutions and path-
ways and therefore recommended for application in the DTU backcasting methodology. Further, the scenario
analysis is beneﬁcial as it maps out the scenario space and provides vital information on the potentially harm-
ful events that may undermine the pathways towards the objectives of the study. However, additional time
will be required to conduct this step, which should be carefully assessed by the administrators of the study. A
depiction of the idea of scenario space as well as the diﬀerent terminologies and concepts from this section can
be found in Figure 8.3. Exploring these scenarios can give insight into how to generate adaptive and resilient
pathways/solutions as well as increasing the understanding of how these scenarios may occur. Understand-
ing how these scenarios can occur further increases resilience as it is possible to identify early warning signs
(Alänge et al., 2007).
In order to generate productive exploratory scenarios it is important to identify the most signiﬁcant and
uncertain drivers and issues. These drives are still possible, however, would typically be disregarded due to
their uncertainty. These are then used to formulate the conditions for the exploratory scenarios. It is important
to provide signiﬁcant detail on how these scenarios may occur so that appropriate solutions and warning signs
can be considered. At the end of this step, a range of exploratory scenarios will have been mapped out and
the following questions answered:
• What signiﬁcant and uncertain issues and/or drivers could impact the ability to achieve the objectives
of the study?
• What events could cause these scenarios to occur?
• How can undesirable scenarios be avoided (deterring the threats)?
• If these scenarios occur how could the pathways adapt to make sure the objectives of the study are still
met (adaptive solutions)?
This information is then compiled by the administrator of the study and is fed into the ’solutions’ step.

Example Box: Exploratory scenarios. This step was not conduct in the case study and as such an
example from Gray et al. (2020) on coastal management is provided.

indicating their signiﬁcance and uncertainty. It was determined that the drivers with high signiﬁcance
and uncertainty were: ’attitude to climate science in public policy’ and ’economic vision’ (roll of the

selected by stakeholders. These scenarios were then further described in PESTLE formatting to generate
detailed images of the future which were then validated by participants. Subsequently, a backcasting
perspective was taken where the stakeholders had to identify incremental action that they saw necessary,
to ensure the objectives of the study were still met given the exploratory scenario came to fruition.
Finally, they were asked to identify the main factors that may cause such scenarios to occur.

8.4
Solutions
The next structural element of the methodology has rarely found its way into a backcasting application as
apparent from the conducted case study review. Out of the reviewed 15 case studies, only 2 cases considered
the explicit use of a solution step (Broman et al., 2013; Musse et al., 2018). A similar approach was applied by
one of the participant groups of the teaching-based research activity (see Chapter 4). In the majority of other
case studies the consideration of possible solutions is integrated within the scenario techniques and does not
form a separate step of the method. In the case of the DTU methodology, this approach was adopted due
to the beneﬁts of structuring and screening the results from the prior scenario building step. The solution
bank further helps when generating pathways in the next step and facilitates a plug and play approach. As
optional elements ’external solution generation techniques’ as well as an ’assessment of the solution bank’
can be considered at this step of the method.
8.4.1
External Solution Generation Techniques [Optional]
The previous exercises are relevant as they support the generation of a solution bank. Future techniques are
considered beneﬁcial to understand and explore potential solutions to feed into the solutions bank. Scenario
analysis and backcasting in particular are used as they are resilient by nature and provide a holistic approach
if implemented to their full extent. However, other methods can be used to generate solutions that have
their merit. Thus, to accommodate for additional tools and techniques, they can be included in this stage
of the methodology. The selection of these techniques must be decided by the administrator of the study to
include and can cross over a range of ideation concepts and activities e.g. brainstorming, SCAMPER (substitute,
combine, adapt, modify, put, eliminate, reverse), mind-mapping etc. At this point, it is suggested to actively
address identiﬁed problems, leverage points and drivers from ’understanding the current state’ that may have
not been addressed in the prior step. This will further utilise solution generation techniques to feed additional
solutions into the ’solution bank’.
8.4.2
Solution Bank
The ’solution bank’ can be documented in diﬀerent ways depending on the agenda and available resources
of the administrator of the study. The solution elements are gathered from deconstructing the scenarios con-
ducted in step 3, as well as any addition solution identiﬁed in the prior sub-step ’external solution generation
techniques’. It is the responsibility of the administrator of the study to organise the ’solution bank’ in a struc-
tured and coherent manner. In addition, the identiﬁed solutions should be screened to exclude repetitions
and manage the number of solutions to a sizeable quantity. This allows the identiﬁcation of predominate
solutions witch occur frequently and allows more detailed descriptions and assessments of the solutions (if
assessments are to be considered). However, consideration of promising and emerging solutions should be
considered and not ruled out in the initial screening. If implementation options were considered in the prior
step of the method, the associated measures to implement solutions can also be collected in the ’solution
bank’. This is considered beneﬁcial as it broadens the understanding for each solution element. However,
the ’implementation options’ for generated pathways will be speciﬁcally discussed in step 5 of the method-
97
==PAGE:111==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
ology. Therefore, documentation of implementation measures (’how’) is not a strict requirement as the main
purpose of the ’solution bank’ is to gather information about the necessary events that could take place in
the future to arrive at set vision. The solution bank elements as a minimum should be described with enough
detail that the individual solutions can be understood from a technical as well as an administrative perspective.
Example Box: Assembling a solution bank in the context of the EU energy system. In order to provide
a descriptive example on how to generate a solution bank, this step was performed and explained as
follows. This is not an exhaustive example of a solution bank.
The input solutions consisted of results from the use of normative scenario techniques using the
teaching-based research activity as well as reviewing the literature. In total 309 solutions were iden-
tiﬁed, which then had to be reﬁned due to repetitions and an aspect of feasibility. This was conducted
intuitively. The ﬁrst step was to categorise the solutions. 13 categories were chosen which can be ob-
served in Figure 4.1 from section 2.2. The next step was to identify prominent solutions that occurred
frequently and to translate these into more generic and detailed solutions, this resulted in 40 reﬁned so-
lutions. It is important to screen the solutions in this manner so that if an assessment is to be conducted,
it reduces the resource intensity of the process. Selected examples of the 40 solutions can be seen below:
• Carbon-oﬀsetting by companies: This is the process of investing in environmentally positive
projects as a way to reduce the negative impacts that are by-products of certain products/indus-
tries/etc. For example, this can be achieved by Funding reforestation, emerging green technology,
plastic clean-up programs, etc.
• Increased building eﬃciencies: This includes increasing the eﬃciencies of new builds as well as
the renovation of existing buildings to reduce the amount of energy and fuel required for heat and
power. This can be achieved via insulation, construction methods and materials, energy-producing
roofs, vertical and rooftop gardens, smaller living, shared living and working spaces, absorption
cooling technology, etc.
• Increase energy production via wind technology: Wind power generated through on-shore
or oﬀ-shore wind turbines. The modern oﬀshore wind turbine provides the capabilities of large
scale electricity production. Wind power generated through small-scale plants on houses or com-
munities oﬀers localised energy production and ownership.
• Power-to-X: Surplus electric power can be converted to other forms of energy for storage and
re-conversion (Buﬀo et al., 2019). Direct current electrolysis (eﬃciency 80–85% at best) can be
used to produce hydrogen which can, in turn, be converted to methane (CH4) via methanation.
Another possibility is converting the hydrogen, along with CO2 to methanol. Both these fuels can
be stored and used to produce electricity again, hours to months later.
• Climate funding: An EU lead climate fund that provide funding and investments for green grass-
root bottom-up initiatives.
98
==PAGE:112==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
8.4.3
Solution Assessment [Optional]
Following the previous step, an optional ’solution assessment’ can be carried out using diﬀerent categories and
supportive tools. This step is optional as a comprehensible assessment is carried out in step 6 after the gener-
ation of pathways. However, the assessment at this level is beneﬁcial as it informs the following ’generation
of pathways’ in step 5 of the methodology. The knowledge on possible solutions for the future contributes
and facilitates the generation of a pathway as relevant information on impacts, trade-oﬀs and possible im-
plications or uncertainties are made available through the assessment. The tools and techniques that can be
applied in this step vary and should incorporate experts from diﬀerent ﬁelds such as policy, social science,
economics, natural science and technology. Useful tools include, but are not limited to multi-criteria-analysis,
life-cycle-assessment and analytical models. The DTU backcasting methodology recommends two assessment
types in regards to the solution assessment, both of are covered in the EU Impact Assessment, outlined in the
’Better Regulation Toolbox’ (European Commission, 2017). The assessment suggested in the context of the
DTU methodology takes outset in the standards set by the European Commission and further explores pos-
sible techniques that were identiﬁed during the work of this thesis. A detailed explanation of assessment
procedures is outlined in Chapter 9 due to the extent of information. The assessment types considered in this
structural element are:
• Impact assessment: The identiﬁcation of the potential impacts relative to the sustainability conditions
and/or assigned indicators in regards to environmental, social and economic dimensions. This gives
signiﬁcant information regarding the trade-oﬀs of each solution relative to the sustainability conditions
and chosen criteria. The degree of analysis detail for each solution element is dependent on available
resources and choice of the administrators of the study. It is recommended to achieve a high degree of
detail and to cover impacts on the set environmental sustainability conditions as a minimum.
• Performance assessment: An assessment of how well the solutions perform in achieving the objec-
tives of the study. This should be assessed against the two categories: eﬀectiveness and eﬃciency. These
are referred to in the EU Impact Assessment (European Commission, 2017).
It is recommended to relate the criteria to set objectives of the study and thereby to address the possible im-
pact of solution elements on the sustainability conditions and their contribution towards the achievement
of the vision. Step 5 of the DTU backcasting methodology involves the generation of pathways towards the
set target year. This step is followed by an assessment element which is similar to the solution assessment
presented prior, expanded by the assessment of preference. The assessment of solutions as well as for the path-
way assessment is a crucial part of the method and therefore receives its own dedicated chapter (see Chapter 9).
99
==PAGE:113==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
Example box: Solution assessment in the context of the EU energy system.
A full assessment using LCA or modelling lies outside the scope of the thesis and is therefore not pre-
sented. Thus, multi-criteria analysis based upon literature was utilised to illustrate how analysis can be
conducted. Furthermore, solely an impact assessment was conducted and not a performance assessment.
The sustainability conditions were used as criteria for which the impacts would be assessed. Further-
more, since it was not possible to conduct a full workshop, qualitative scoring was utilised. The impact
was rated on a -3 to 3 scale. A score of 0 corresponded to no impact, 1 low impact, 2 for medium impacts
and 3 high impacts. The sign of the score indicated its direction i.e. positive or negative. Furthermore,
the criteria were weighted based on the importance of the sustainability condition. Taking the example
of the planetary boundaries in the doughnut economics model, biodiversity, novel entities and climate
change are already or nearly in exceedance, as such, they were ranked with an increased weighting.
8.5
Pathways Towards the Objectives
This structural element consists of two sub-steps, ’generation of pathways’ and ’implementation options’. The
ﬁrst sub-step, ’generation of pathways’, is a complex step and requires deeper consideration and problem-
solving techniques. For this reason, it is sub-structured covering key prior considerations and multiple ap-
proaches to aid the generation of pathways. The generation of pathways step focuses on the ’what’ elements
that are required to form a pathway. The second step, ’implementation options’ focuses on the ’how’ aspect
of solution implementation. This step is optional as it verges into the policy domain and as such, the inclusion
of this step is dependant on the purpose of the backcasting study (see section 8).
8.5.1
Generation of Pathways
The generation of pathways is at the core of the presented backcasting approach. The outcome generated
out of this sub-step should deliver holistic pathways that achieve the targets outlined in the objectives of the
study. For this reason, the sustainability conditions must be considered in this step. A few manageable number
of pathways should be presented, for practicality reasons, it is recommended that 4 is a reasonable number.
Besides the mentioned useful contributions on the topic of pathway generation in section 7, a conclusive
and elaborate perspective on generating pathways is missing in the literature. This leaves an open space for
experimentation which in regards to the DTU backcasting methodology, delivers a multitude of diﬀerent ways
to approaching pathway generation. This step of the methodology provides the most value to inform strategic
planning. However, there are two approaches to generating pathways in this section. The ﬁrst is quick and
iterative, this approach oﬀers a ’learning by doing stance’. Hence, the quick and iterative approach is often
beneﬁcial as it is more accessible for stakeholder engagement, and hence is beneﬁcial to inform policy where
often quick and continuous relaying of information is required. The second approach, long and thorough,
requires more detailed considerations while generating pathways. This approach is better suited if integrated
assessment models are to be utilised since more time is available to quantify impacts and performance of
100
==PAGE:114==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
solutions. In addition, the approaches to aid pathway generation described in this section can be more in-
depth and will allow for more informed decisions while generating pathways. This approach is better suited
for ’one-shot’ applications where the risk of failure is higher and as such needs detailed considerations.
Pathway Generation and Use of Scenarios
The pathway generation is inﬂuenced by the choice of backcasting approach. This ultimately comes down
to whether or not scenarios are considered in this step of the methodology. Thus, it is important to provide
information on how the use of scenarios will inﬂuence the pathway generation step and why it may be bene-
ﬁcial to include them. In the prior ’scenario building’ step, a range of diﬀerent scenarios were used to explore
the scenario space and gain valuable information to inform the solution bank step. Scenarios are beneﬁcial as
they provide structure and narrative which in the case of step 3 is beneﬁcial to gain understanding and insight.
However, in this step of the methodology scenarios are an optional tool to aid pathway generation. Hence,
since it is possible to exclude scenario narratives it is important to deﬁne criteria for the pathway generation
in this step. Thus, in step 5 of the methodology pathways must meet the following criteria:
1. The pathways must achieve the targets outlined in ’setting objectives’.
2. The pathways must adhere to the sustainability conditions, to ensure sustainability.
3. There should be a range of pathways with the aim to explore possibilities. However, only a few man-
ageable numbers of pathways must be considered to reduce complexity. An odd number of pathways
should be avoided to prevent the generation or selection of a ’combination pathway’. A total of 4 path-
ways could, therefore, be taken as guidance.
4. The pathways must be concerning the particular system of the study.
5. The pathways should be signiﬁcantly contrasting (yet still realistic) to provide as much information as
possible on how to plan for the future.
First, it is important to discuss whether scenarios want to be considered. Thus, a decision should be made
on whether backcasting from principles or backcasting from scenarios is to be used. Regardless of which ap-
proach is taken the pathways must adhere to the sustainability conditions. In the case of scenarios, preference
and progress towards the scenarios will change over time which could undermine a potential pathway. This
stresses the importance of using the sustainability conditions to guide the co-creation of scenarios. This re-
sults in a combination approach between backcasting from principles and scenarios, where the scenarios are
used to provide structure yet are still within the domain of the sustainability conditions (Karl-Henrik Robèrt,
Personal Communication, 2020). The beneﬁt of using scenarios is to provide narrative and engagement be-
tween people. This narrative can be used to structure pathways since it is easier to visualise a future that is
described by an image of the future or scenario theme; thereby, it is a powerful technique to engage stake-
holders. However, using scenarios may constrain the pathway generation by boxing the pathways according
to chosen images of the future or scenario theme. Studies that exclude the use of scenarios (backcasting from
principles) beneﬁt from the freedom to generate pathways where the only consideration required, is meeting
the sustainability conditions. If scenarios are considered then see section ’scenarios themes and future images
approach’ below.
101
==PAGE:115==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
Basic Structural Considerations Required to Generate Pathways
When approaching the topic of generating pathways, it can be stated that several variables need to be con-
sidered to structure a full breadth pathway. These variables relate to: the trajectory of the pathway which
involves the setting of targets and milestones along the path; the solutions which make up the pathway; the
ordering of these solutions; as well as the time required to implement these solutions and actions. The follow-
ing list shows a reﬂection for each of these variables, which will inﬂuence the characteristics and behaviour
of a pathway.
• Deﬁning trajectory, setting targets and milestones as displayed in Figure 8.5
– Linear target setting
– Ambitious target setting
– Reluctant target setting
– Variations of previous target setting approaches
• Use of solutions
– Individual solution elements
– Utilise assembled solution packages (categorised by scenario theme or by synergies)
• Order of action placement
– Create pathway by placing actions in a forward-looking manner by starting from the present mo-
ment
– Create pathway by placing actions in a backwards-looking manner from the chosen target year
– Subdivide pathway into pathway segments and place actions for individual segments
102
==PAGE:116==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
Figure 8.5: Depiction of concepts for target setting until 2050 based on (1) linear target setting, (2) ambitious
target setting, (3) reluctant target setting and (4) variation of previous target setting approaches.
As stated prior, the pathway generation lacks guidance in literature and as such, is often easier said than
done. The presented variables provide a better basic understanding of which conceptual choices in generating
pathways exist. Therefore, the presented variables are considered beneﬁcial in structuring pathways and can
help advance the pathway generation. However, through the review of case studies and consultation with the
expert network, many diﬀerent approaches can be identiﬁed which help inform the variables of a pathway
stated above. Predominately, they help determine which solutions should be included as well as the ordering
of the solutions. That being said, pathway generation is often a challenging task and although the below
approaches can help inform the generation, there is ultimately an element of problem-solving which should
result in the combination outlined approaches. A total of 5 approaches have been identiﬁed in the reviewed
literature and in discussion with experts. These approaches are presented in the following:
• Logical analysis
• Solution package approach
• Sectoral approach
• Scenario themes and future images approach
• Flexible pathways approach
103
==PAGE:117==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
8.5.1.1
Logical Analysis
The purpose of logical analysis is to identify what solutions should make up a pathway. It does not give infor-
mation on the ordering of the solutions or the trajectory of the pathways. This approach aims at identifying
which of the solutions in the solution bank are unavoidable if the objectives of the study are to be met. To
identify these solutions, there is a process of elimination. The criteria for elimination should be related to
the assessment of the solutions, i.e. addressing solutions which are the most eﬀective and have the lowest
impact. Thus, conducting the prior solution assessment sub-step would further aid this approach of pathway
generation. However, this can still be done intuitively or with participants. In more general terms the ﬁrst
step would be to rule out all solutions which would not signiﬁcantly bridge the gap between the current state
and the objectives of the study. Next, you must consider future trends to eliminate any solutions which do not
ﬁt with the growing trends. Finally, solutions which do not ﬁt with the sustainability conditions should be
excluded from the core elements of pathway generation. As a result, a number of solutions can be identiﬁed
that respect the chosen sustainability conditions in step 1 of the DTU backcasting methodology, which are
also able to achieve the targets for the respective system until set target year and are resilient to emerging
trends. These solutions then provide the core elements to form the pathways. In an iteration of the generated
pathways, previously excluded solutions can be reintroduced to account for possible negative impacts or iden-
tiﬁed trade-oﬀs along a pathway. This approach is useful when it comes to purely logically approaching the
set goals and targets for a system. For example, which solutions contribute to a reduction of GHG emissions
by 80% in the energy sector can be identiﬁed via this method. When applying this approach, consideration
must be given to the impacts of identiﬁed solutions on set sustainability conditions to conform with the overall
set objectives of the application. This approach is similar to what was gathered from the conversation with
Dr. Damsø (Tue Noa Jacques Damsø, Personal Communication, 2020) as described in Chapter 7. In his work
Damsø et al. (2014) a similar logical approach was taken to the energy system with the diﬀerentiating element
that instead of looking at the solutions that will achieve the goals the most likely, they ruled out solutions
that based on expert judgement seemed unrealistic to achieve set objectives. Thereby, a smaller number of
solutions will remain which then can be combined to generate pathways in an optimised manner.
8.5.1.2
Solution Package Approach
The solution package approach can aid the pathway generation by grouping key solutions together into pack-
ages and thus, help the solution selection aspect. As discussed in sections 5.3.5 and 7.10 the use of policy
packages can be eﬀective in grouping policy together to address the objectives of the study more eﬃciently.
This approach used in Banister (1998) and Giaoutzi et al. (2012) can be adapted to group solutions together
into ’solution packages’. Using solution packages can be beneﬁcial since solutions within the packages will
complement each other, thus ultimately becoming more eﬀective. In addition, it allows trade-oﬀs to be bal-
anced, allowing for certain inaccessible solutions that do not individually meet the criteria, to be included in
pathways. Grouping solutions into solution packages can be achieved in two approaches, as seen in Giaoutzi
et al. (2012). These are either intuitively or deductive. The intuitive approach groups solutions which comple-
ment one another to enhance synergistic eﬀects. As such it is beneﬁcial to have conducted the prior sub-step
’solution assessment’ as it will have identiﬁed the impacts and performance of the solutions. Another, argu-
mentation behind conducting the prior assessment is gaining deeper knowledge on the trade-oﬀs, on each of
104
==PAGE:118==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
the assessed solutions. However, intuition-based solution packages can be further aided by the judgement of
experts who can provide information on the feasibility and realism of proposed solutions working together.
The deductive approach groups packages which ﬁt with the scenarios themes or images of the future e.g.
technology, management, lifestyle, etc. This is since solutions that can be grouped under the same categories,
often complement one another.
Example box: Solution packages in the context of the EU energy system.
An example of intuitive solution package generation could be, increased energy production via wind
technology, an EU wide energy network and power-to-x technology. These solutions would work well
together in a solution package, as they complement each other. Since wind energy production cannot
be switched on (like a fossil fuel plant or nuclear power station), there is a need for more sophisticated
energy storage options as power-to-x. In addition, a larger connected EU wide network would help
handle ﬂuctuation in energy supply and demand. Combining all three of these solutions can help make
the energy system more resilient and as such does not require large buﬀers in the system, resulting in
more eﬃcient operation. Other renewable energy sources that require storage options could also be
included in this solution package.
Information campaigns, Education reforms and Eco-labelling could be formed into a solution package
based on the deductive solution package generation approach. These solutions require soft action and
aim at raising public awareness of energy consumption. Ultimately, the intention behind these solutions
is to change lifestyle and behaviour.
8.5.1.3
Sectoral Approach
Approaching the pathway generation from a sectoral perspective relies on silo thinking, meaning that in-
dividual components of a system will be tackled separately, instead of in a systemic way. This reduces the
complexity of designing pathways for large systems. This approach is designed to help determine what so-
lutions should make up a pathway and can advise the order in which the solutions should be structured.
The ﬁrst step would be to dissects the target system into smaller more manageable sections. The process of
breaking down the larger system is the root of the sectoral approach, however, how the system/problem is
sub-divided can vary from case to case. It can be deconstructed into sub-systems of the larger system; they
can be subdivided into social, economical, political and cultural dimensions; or it could even be sub-divided
into the individual targets and goals of the study. One approach used in Gray et al. (2020) was to subdivide the
study into PESTLE categories: political, economic, social, technical, legal and environmental. Nevertheless,
it is important to simplify the problem to determine how the solutions can solve simpliﬁed issues. After the
dissection of a system and its analysis and generation of individual sectoral pathways, the sectors will then
be reassembled to create a more holistic pathway. Here, the diﬃculty lies in accounting for sectoral trade-oﬀs
and complex relationships, similar to the approach of generating pathways for diﬀerent systems such as food,
energy and mobility.
105
==PAGE:119==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
8.5.1.4
Scenario Themes and Future Images Approach
Another approach often used in-explicitly in the reviewed case studies is the use of scenario themes and future
image narratives to determine the solution selection and their ordering. This approach utilises the consider-
ations from the prior steps of the methodology to make informed decisions on which solutions ﬁt within the
scenarios. From this point, the solutions can be ordered so that they form a coherent pathway towards the
objectives of the study. This approach is eﬀective at structuring the pathways, however, it is recommended
that this approach must be used into combination with the other approaches to increase the integrity of the
scenarios. Solely structure pathways based around scenario themes or future images may render ill-informed
pathways.
If scenarios are to be considered the next consideration is how to generate eﬀective and realistic scenarios
that inform action to the highest degree. The concept of realism in scenarios was brieﬂy discussed in sections
7.8 and 8.3 and is vital to consider in the pathway generation. A focus on one dominant scenario theme,
e.g. technology, management, etc., is not able to depict a realistic image of the future, therefore more holistic
images are needed (Raskin et al., 2002; Geurs and van Wee, 2004; Giaoutzi et al., 2012). Determining realistic
scenarios is a challenge as it is subjective. Thus, the attribution of realism in this regard is based upon the
judgement of experts and participants of the study. Further, it can provide a platform for public discussion
and political agendas. One approach to generating eﬀective scenarios is to provide combinations of dominant
scenario themes, preventing heavy dependency on certain elements, and then using them to structure the
pathways. These combined scenario themes can be used to describe detailed images of the future, providing
strong narratives which in turn are used to structure the pathways. Describing images of the future can
be more eﬀective if combined with the sectoral approach. This is the approach Gray et al. (2020) takes by
describing the images of the future using PESTLE analysis (see example in section 8.3.3). The drawback of
using future images is that they require detailed descriptions which can be time-consuming and often requires
stakeholder holder input. For this reason, the scenario themes approach is better suited for the quick and
iterative approach to pathway generation, whereas, future images are more appropriate for the long and
thorough approach.
106
==PAGE:120==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
Example box: Pathway generation, detailed and more realistic scenarios.
Taking the dominant development themes, technology, lifestyle and management and combing all three
with diﬀerent levels of focus. This approach was observed in the case study by (Giaoutzi et al., 2012).
The diﬀerent combinations can then be seen in Table 8.1, which result out of combinations between
varying levels of technology and lifestyle, mapped against three management approaches (i.e. top-down,
combined and bottom-up).

It is important to note that just because dominant scenario themes are taken in combination, it does not
mean that they are realistic. For example, in the case of the EU energy system, many of the targets are
related to improvements in energy eﬃciency and scaling up of renewable energy sources. As such, there
is a signiﬁcant dependency on technological solutions, hence, scenarios L1-3 are most likely unrealistic.
8.5.1.5
Flexible Pathways Approach
This approach aims at ordering solutions along a pathway. It takes inspiration from The Natural Step’s priori-
tisation of solutions in step D of the ABCD-methodology (Broman and Robèrt, 2017; TheNaturalStep-Canada,
2020a; John Holmberg, Personal Communication, 2020). In the ABCD-methodology paths are constructed by
determining which solutions are the most ﬂexible to prevent any solutions that may result in lock-ins. This
is designed to generate the most eﬀective and resilient short-term actions with long-term perspectives, how-
ever, pathways are not considered in the ABCD-methodology. Applying this concept to a pathway approach
requires some additional considerations. The goal is to formulate pathways with ﬂexible solutions at the fore-
front, the choice of these solutions should be made in a way that suﬃcient options remain available in case of
unforeseen events occur that disturb the unfolding of a pathway. Hence, making sure the short-term action
of the pathways is ﬂexible to allow pathway ’jumping’. Thus, when discussing ﬂexibility the primary focus is
in regards to lock-ins. It is importation to consider what solutions must be, or must not be, implemented now
to ensure the future has the most possibilities. Thus, having these ﬂexible pathway allows for later adaptation
or reconsideration in the iteration of the backcasting (see section 8.7). To understand this concept, consider
the analogy of a spaceship aiming towards the moon with no pilot. It is highly unlikely that the spaceship will
land safely on the moon. Now consider a spaceship aiming towards the moon with a pilot to correct the tra-
jectory, the pilot can make an adjustment to ensure the spaceship lands safely. In this analogy, the moon refers
to the objective of the study, and the spaceship refers to the current state and pilot represents ﬂexibility. To
107
==PAGE:121==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
allow for corrections, ﬂexibility must be built into the pathway, otherwise, it is like aiming for the moon with
a spaceship that has no pilot. This concept of ﬂexible pathways deviates from The Natural Step’s approach,
as full pathways towards 2050 are considered. However, it is still beneﬁcial to consider the ﬂexibility of the
pathways so that solutions that could potentially cause lock-ins are not considered in the early stages of the
pathway. As such, when advancing in time and the uncertainty towards achieving the objectives decreases,
more informed decision can be made. Thereby, it should be possible to determine which options further down
a pathway remain open and in which direction a pathway could be steered in case it is needed.
The concept of ﬂexible pathways is closely related to resilience and adaptability. The use of exploratory
scenarios in step 3 of the DTU backcasting methodology, helps provide information on how to prevent or
adapt to potentially signiﬁcant and uncertain scenarios. Further, it helps to understand what is possible in
the future and increases the level of system understanding and adaptability. Thus, if the ﬂexible pathways
approach is to be utilised, it is suggested to make sure the sub-step ’exploratory scenarios’ is conducted as a
precursor.
8.5.1.6
Combining Approaches and Considerations on Pathway Trajectory
A combination of presented approaches and methods is considered beneﬁcial in forming structured and ef-
fective pathways. Combining the approaches described above can help determine the selecting and ordering
of solutions, however, this can have a direct eﬀect on the trajectory of the pathways. As such further consid-
eration is required regarding the trajectory of pathways. Considering all the above approaches, the element
of pathway trajectory will have a large inﬂuence on how the pathways are constructed. Take for example
the logical analysis. If progressive milestones are set then this will delimit the number of solutions that will
be able to achieve the set milestones. Furthermore, the same can be said for other approaches. In compari-
son, the trajectory could be deﬁned in a more dynamic approach which puts focus on the solutions to dictate
the trajectory of the pathways. Finally, preference needs to be considered. As stated prior (see section 7.11),
preference towards pathways is partly a function of the endpoint and partly a function of how the pathway
unfolds. This further indicates the importance of preference towards the solutions in a pathway, as well as,
the trajectory of the pathways. As such, when combining the approaches it is possible to present pathways
and scenarios with similar or identical solution elements and varying trajectory. However, to reiterate path-
way criteria 5 (’pathways should be signiﬁcantly contrasting (yet still realistic) [...]’, see section 8.5.1), at the
end of this process it is important to be able to present a variety of contracting (yet still realistic) pathway
options towards achieving the objectives of the study. Thus, this consideration must be had while generating
pathways or scenarios. Combining all the variables of a pathway (solutions, ordering of solutions and trajec-
tories) is a challenging task. Applying combinations of the approaches above as well as consideration on the
trajectory involves a large element of problem-solving. Further, the outcome of this step largely depends on
the context and purpose of the study. The addition of expert and policy-making participants can help facilitate
this process. Their knowledge on specialised topics and judgement on feasibility can help put the pieces of the
puzzle together. To better illustrate the combination of approaches, an example of how this can be conducted
is presented in the example box below.
108
==PAGE:122==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
Example box: Pathway generation in the context of the EU energy system. The example box aims
to illustrate on a simpliﬁed level how certain approaches can be applied and combined. As illustrated
above there are many diﬀerent ways to approach pathway generation. This is purely to provide an ex-
ample and how to apply some of the approaches. As such only pathways towards 2030 will be considered.
In this example, a quick and iterative approach to pathway generation is going to be conducted.
For this reason, scenario themes are going to be used to help structure the pathways. Two scenario
themes are going to be exempliﬁed to provide contrast amongst the pathways. The two scenario themes
chosen are "Combined management approaches to achieve the 2050 objectives in the energy sector, with a
heavy focus on technology-based solutions and small consideration of lifestyle changes"; and "bottom-up
management approaches to achieve the 2050 objectives in the energy sector, with equal focus on technology
and lifestyle based solutions". Both scenarios are related to scenarios ’T2’ and ’TL3’ from prior example
box under ’scenario themes and future images approach’ in section 8.5.1. For the chosen scenarios,
there has to be emphasised consideration of technology-based solutions as the targets/context of the
study have large dependencies on technology. For example, it is not possible to achieve the target, 32%
renewable energy mix, without increasing renewable technology.
An intuitive solution package approach is going to be utilised in this study and as such a few packages
are exempliﬁed below. A multi-criteria analysis solution impact assessment was used to help inform the
groupings of solutions.
• SP1: Increased wind energy, Power-to-X, EU wide Energy Network.
• SP2: Eco-labeling, carbon-oﬀsetting by companies, carbon capture and storage technology.
• SP3: Information campaigns, education reforms.
• SP4: Energy generation from biomass, carbon capture and storage technology change in diet.
• SP5: Set more stringent and progressive targets, reduction of oil/gas imports, market regulation
on energy-eﬃcient applications.
• Etc...
Next, the logical analysis approach is used to aid the selection of solution packages. Ideally, this ap-
proach would use an element of participation from experts and policymakers to help inform the elimi-
nation process. This is since the elimination of solutions packages requires better judgement as overall
performance and impacts can change as a result of the groupings. Nevertheless, both solution packages
and solution elements were considered. Following, the criteria of the logical analysis must be deﬁned.
The criteria was related to the performance of the solutions and solution packages, the impacts (i.e. does
it breach any of the sustainability conditions) and whether they are ﬁtting with potential future trends.
Hence, it is important to state that this example box utilises the principles of sustainability to deﬁne the
sustainability conditions; and all exempliﬁcations from the example boxes before this step. Based on this
criteria solutions and solutions packages were eliminated some of which are exempliﬁed below:
109
==PAGE:123==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
Example box (Continued):
• SP6: Nuclear power and Green Bonds. This package was eliminated as the impacts associated with
nuclear energy breach the second sustainability principle (a systematic increase of concentrations
of substances produced by society) of the sustainability conditions.
• Solution element: Fossil fuel ban. This solution although eﬀective at cutting greenhouse gas
levels, will breach sustainability principle 4 (the ability for people to meet basic human needs).
This is since currently, fossil fuel energy is responsible for 82% of the overall share of energy in
Europe (data from 2017). The circumstances may change in the future, however, this is uncertain
and would be based upon assumptions.
• Solution element: Marine algae (for biomass). This solution adds to the growing pressure on
ecosystems and based oﬀof the current state of the technology is unlikely to contribute large
progress towards the objectives of the study.
Since certain solutions from solution packages were ruled out, the solution package approach was
iterated to regroup ﬂoating solutions.
At this point, the ﬂexible pathways approach is eﬀective to determine how to order the solutions and
solution packages. No detailed consideration on lock-ins has been considered, however, it is possible to
intuitively rule out solutions that can cause heavy lock-ins or implement certain solutions to prevent
lock-ins. For example, if energy eﬃciency is not tackled in the early stages of pathway generation then
there is a resulting lock-in by having to meet higher ﬁnal energy demands.
The next consideration is the trajectory and milestone setting. In this example, both scenarios will
be formulated with a linear trajectory to simplify the example. Milestone will be set every 5 years from
2025 until 2050:
2020 20% improvements in energy eﬃciency, 20% renewable energy share and 20% reduction of GHG
emissions (from 1990 levels).
2025 26.3% improvements in energy eﬃciency, 26% renewable energy share, 30% reduction of GHG
emissions (from 1990 levels) and decarbonise a total of 178 million tons of oil equivalents from
2020 levels.
2030 32.5% improvements in energy eﬃciency, 32% renewable energy mix, 40% reduction of GHG emis-
sions (from 1990 levels) and decarbonise a total of 353 million tons of oil equivalents from 2020
levels.
Now that the solutions and trajectories are determined the scenario narrative approach can be used to
structure the two pathways.
