The pathway indicated in Table 8.3 is broken into ﬁve-year phases. Since the trajectory is linear the
progression is constant, as observed in column 2. In Phase 1, wind energy and biomass are considered
as they provide a foundation on how to proceed in the next ﬁve years. Wind energy is currently one of
the most promising renewable energy sources, with the oﬀshore wind taking a leading roll in energy
generation Zappa et al. (2019). However, energy production from wind energy has large ﬂuctuations
due to weather conditions and thus, sophisticated storage solutions are required, hence the need for
power-to-x facilities. In addition, the mass mobilisation of biomass plants partnered with carbon capture
and storage (CCS) technology is required to reduce the concentrations of carbon in the atmosphere.
Hence, contributing largely towards the targets. CCS can also be used in many fossil fuel plants. There
are risks and uncertainties associated with the use of CCS, as currently there are technological barriers
and hence, it is not widely operational (EEA, 2019a). However, the risk of not having CCS available
as a mitigation option out-ways the risk of development, hence, its inclusion in the pathway (Dutta,
2017). By considering the other solution element biomass, there is a trade-oﬀin regards to land use.
Since agriculture along with forestry is causing large pressures on land-use change EEA (2019a), dietary
habits should be addressed to reduce the added pressures caused by biomass crops. In Phase 2, given
the increased shift towards renewables, reductions of oil and gas imports should be made. Furthermore,
setting more stringent and progressive targets can help in the following years to stay on track towards
the overall vision. Together with better market regulation, the milestones should be achieved.
The pathway indicated in Table 8.2 takes a linear trajectory and as such, the progression towards the
milestones are the same in the two pathways. The second pathway is more focused on bottom-up so-
lutions as well as lifestyle and behavioural changes. In Phase 1 there is a heavy focus on waste man-
agement, encouraging the public and industries to increase the recycling eﬀorts, as well as incinerating
non-recyclable waste. This is to reduce the amount of virgin material being consumed.

==PAGE:125==

DTU Environment
Example box (Continued):
Furthermore, modern waste to incineration plants operate at high eﬃciency and with gas treatment can
emit low levels of pollution. In the case of Copenhill in Denmark, total net energy eﬃciency can reach
over 107% (Hulgaard and Sondergaard, 2018). Additional industry-focused solutions relate to carbon-
oﬀsetting and eco-labelling. This aims to put more responsibility on industry to inform the market on
their environmental impacts and make eﬀorts to reduce them. Finally, an EU wide information network
should be generated. The information network intends to support a range of bottom-up climate and
energy solutions which can feedback into a centralised system. This system will gain insight on which
solution is working and under which conditions and as such, can inform investments through an EU
wide climate fund. The climate fund is aimed to support the development of a large number of localised
solutions. Phase 2 of the pathway builds upon Phase 1 and is focused around personalised ownership
of energy sources and peer-to-peer energy trading. This is facilitated via the use of high-tech energy
monitoring and trading system utilising blockchain technology.

Now that the generalised structure of the two pathways is outlined, an iteration of the pathways should
be conducted to increase the detail in the pathway elements (this is related to the ’level of what’ concept
discussed in section 7.9). This iteration is conducted since the quick and iterative approach has been
utilised, if a long and thorough approach was conducted, a high level of detail would be considered from
the beginning. The present level of detail in the pathways is understandable to individuals who do not
have expertise or knowledge in energy transitions. However, for an expert, more detail is needed. For
example, what deeper solutions in TL2 are required when increasing wind energy? This could be as
speciﬁc as the type of oﬀ-shore wind turbines and how many turbines are required. This iteration was
delimited from the scope of this thesis, as it involves expertise on a deeper level (which can be achieved
in a participatory setting) or an in-depth literature backing.
112
==PAGE:126==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
8.5.2
Implementation Options [Optional]
The identiﬁcation of implementation options for identiﬁed solution elements can be conducted in this sub-
step of the methodology. The sub-step is optional and largely dependant on the purpose of the backcasting
study. If the study is designed to inform policymakers on the trade-oﬀs and implications of pathway elements
and choice, then implementation options do not necessarily need to be considered as they fall outside of the
boundaries of policy relevance. However, providing information on a range of implementation options and
their implications on elements such as environmental impacts can still be beneﬁcial for policymakers. If the
purpose of the backcasting study is to deﬁne strategic action for the future then implementation needs to be
considered as generated pathways should ﬁnd their way into a policy-making context (Robinson, 1990). The
consideration of implementation options in a backcasting study adds considerable levels of complexity as ad-
ditional factors need to ﬁnd consideration, e.g. behavioural and institutional responses (Robinson, 1990). The
consideration of implementation options, therefore, may inﬂuence the structure of pathways. Noteworthy in
this context is the elements of implementation time and institutional barriers. Implementation options can be
associated with a time that is needed for an implementation to pass through governmental and institutional
bodies due to their structure and barriers in policy development processes. As Geurs and van Wee (2004)
state in their case study, consideration of time in regards to overcoming institutional barriers and political
opposition may require an idea similar to ’time paths’ (Julio A. Soria-Lara, Personal Communication, 2020).
The consideration of implementation options and related time aspect is a challenging task and generally lies
within the policy development domain. Therefore, in the DTU backcasting methodology suﬃcient expertise
on the topic of policy and related time for implementation needs to be guaranteed.
When it comes to identifying available implementation options, it can be stated they can range in terms of
eﬀectiveness and eﬃciency and additionally might have considerable economic, environmental and social im-
pacts (Robinson, 1990). Furthermore, the implementation will vary considerably across diﬀerent applications.
On an EU level, there is a range of legal acts that are available to the Commission, ranging from EU regulations
to directives, economic incentives and other acts (Borchardt, 2010). If the implementation is being considered
by individual member states, the local situation and tools available may be diﬀerent. In addition to the tools
at hand, the social situations may vary depending on the context of the implementation. Referring back to
the EU, social impacts are essential, such that they adhere to the founding principles of the European Union,
described in Article 2 of the treaty of Lisbon (Borchardt, 2010). Thus, consideration must be made on the
intrusiveness of the pathway implementation, whilst at the same time achieving the objectives of the study.
The example box below provides a list of identiﬁed implementation options relevant for prior identiﬁed so-
lution packages. Similar to described levels of ’what’ there is an associated level of ’how’ to implement the
identiﬁed solutions. Due to the time-consuming nature of deeper levels of ’how’ and its relevance for a policy
development context, the example provides a generic example of implementation options.
Due to outlined implications resulting out of considering implementation options, the assessment should be
considered for the complete pathways. Thereby, the assessment would be expanded from assessing the impacts
and trade-oﬀs of solution elements to their related policy options.
113
==PAGE:127==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
Example box:
An illustration of an example of implementation options can be found in the work by Giaoutzi et al.
(2012). Their approach to forming policy packages was taken to inform step 5 by forming solution
packages. However, in case of implementation is considered as part of the backcasting application,
policy packages could be preferable.
Taking the provided solutions packages, SP2 and SP8 from the previous example box, one can identify
relevant policy options to implement or encourage these packages. The identiﬁcation can be done in
terms of research, participatory methods and deconstructing of analysing applied implementation op-
tions (e.g. past policy implementation). The list presented in Table 8.4 is by no means exhaustive and
with the right experts in the policy domain and stakeholders involved, a better resolution of available
options and more detailed levels of ’how’ to implement these solutions can be identiﬁed.

8.6
Assessment
This structural element is a mandatory part for the methodology as the created pathways and determined
actions will have an impact on the system in focus. This sub-step is applied to the pathways constructed
prior, either purely based on consideration of ’what’ actions must be taken or based on the combination
of ’what’ and ’how’, i.e. the implementation options. Therefore, the assessment step will give a varying
degree of information resulting from the assessment based upon the selection and application of optional
114
==PAGE:128==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
backcasting elements as provided in the DTU backcasting methodology. If pathways are generated without
addressing the implementation options the assessment will provide limited insight as it will not be covering
related implementation options. Nevertheless, an assessment of pathways will provide insights on trade-oﬀs
between solution elements and allow for a comparison of diﬀerent pathways and scenarios. The assessment
step draws on similarities from the prior structure element, ’solutions assessment’. Three assessments must
be considered at this stage and with an increased level of detail compared to the prior ’solution assessment’.
The assessment categories suggested are:
• Sustainability Assessment
• Performance Assessment
• Preference Assessment
In comparison to the previous ’solution assessment’, the preference assessment is an additional aspect re-
garding the assessment of a pathway. This should include the acceptance and perceived preferability of dif-
ferent stakeholder groups. This would provide valuable information in a political context on how well the
generated pathway could be received by diﬀerent stakeholders. As stated in ’solutions assessment’, the as-
sessment of solutions and pathways is a crucial part of the method and therefore receives its own dedicated
chapter (see chapter 9).
8.7
Evaluation and Iteration
As a ﬁnal element of the DTU backcasting methodology, the possibility for ’evaluation’ and ’iteration’ is
provided. The step of iterating the backcasting application was observed in a range of case studies, especially
in the context of the ABCD-methodology by The Natural Step (Broman and Robèrt, 2017; Göran Broman,
Personal Communication, 2020). This was further suggested by a group of students of the teaching-based
research activity as presented in section 7.12. To provide useful information for a subsequent iteration, an
evaluation needs to take place in combination. Here it is important to consider: What information and data
are relevant to inform an iteration of the backcasting application?. It is suggested that evaluation takes place
close to each planned iteration of the backcasting exercise. The iteration of a backcasting exercise could be
set depending on the resources of the administrators and the availability of participants. A further inﬂuential
factor is the design of pathways and setting of milestones. In terms of the DTU methodology, it is beneﬁcial
to align the iteration of a backcasting exercise and as such, the evaluation with set milestones as this will
enable direct comparison to previously designed pathways. The comparison will be based upon the recent
policy decisions and developments up until the time of evaluation. Therefore, it is relevant to start at the
implementation and action level as these inﬂuence the current state of the investigated system. It should be
asked:
• Which implementations have been carried out since the end of the backcasting exercise?
• Which implementations have shown to be successful? How eﬀective have these or previous implemen-
tations been?
115
==PAGE:129==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
• Which implementations did not show success? What can be concluded in such cases?
• How much time has past since the implementation of a pathway element in respect to its eﬀects regard-
ing set targets?
The evaluation should provide an improved understanding of the current state and thereby, inform the sub-
sequent steps of the backcasting iteration. The evaluation can be based upon the targets and milestones or
additionally chosen indicators. Further, it can be considered to assess against the sustainability conditions as
set in step 1 of the study. In the best case, the evaluation is done continuously until the next iteration date.
The better the resolution of evaluation results the better informed the iteration of a backcasting exercise will
be. This is limited by the availability of resources and complexity related to the evaluation of indicators.
As concluded in Chapter 7, iteration is relevant as the decisions for achieving the long-term vision need to be
constantly adapted to occurring events and changes in behaviour and trends. Thereby, the iteration serves the
purpose of feeding back information into a subsequent backcasting application, which in return, yields a better
understanding of the transforming system and can provide better results for policy-support. When iterating
a backcasting study it is possible to adapt previously carried out work and reevaluate what has changed. The
reevaluation shall be guided by the decision of the administrators and participants of the study. However, to
provide guidance some questions could be considered:
• Step 1: Have the objectives of the backcast changed? Has the scope of the backcasting application
changed?
• Step 2: Has the current state of the system improved or deteriorated? Has the gap increased or decreased
towards achieving the vision? Have elements of other systems been aﬀected due to the changes in the
investigated system? What is the current state of the previously identiﬁed trends and drivers? Have
new trends emerged? Are the main causes for the current trajectory still the same? Are made system
assumptions still valid or do they need reconsideration?
• Step 3: Has the perception of generated scenarios from scenario analysis or normative scenarios changed?
What are the relevant developments in regards to (normative) scenario analysis? Are previously gen-
erated outcomes still applicable at the time of the backcasting iteration? If exploratory scenarios were
conducted, have any of the identiﬁed early warning signs been observed? If so, what does this ﬁnding
imply for the scenarios and the iteration of backcasting?
• Step 4: Have promising solutions emerged? Are relevant solutions still the same as at previous back-
casting exercise? Which solutions proved to be successful/beneﬁcial to set targets? Have preferences
changed to the use of certain solutions? Which solutions are less relevant for an iteration in the context
of the iteration?
• Step 5: How do previously generated pathways compare to the current state of the system at the time
of iteration? Considering the gained knowledge up until the time of iteration, are generated pathways
still valid to achieve the objectives of the study in step 1? In the case of utilising future images: Have the
normative images changed? Do the images of the future need adaptation due to changes in preference
116
==PAGE:130==
Chapter 8. The DTU Backcasting Methodology
DTU Environment
or recent developments? What are the implications of that, regarding the prior constructed pathways?
As an example: Are corrections needed for subsequent phases of the pathways, do new pathways need
generating, etc.?
Besides the presented implications of the ﬁnal step in the DTU methodology, it is deemed beneﬁcial to set in
place a quality control mechanism between the conclusion of a backcasting application and the subsequent
iterations. By documenting the procedures and learnings in regards to the conduction of the method, related
workshops and applied tools, adaptations can be made before the start of an iteration. This could potentially
reduce the required time and resources as well as yield better quality outcomes. This element supports the
inherent ﬂexibility of the DTU methodology to the needs and assigned purpose of the application.
8.8
Participatory Considerations
As a ﬁnal consideration, the element of participation needs mentioning in the context of the DTU backcast-
ing methodology. The terminology related to the term ’stakeholder’ may vary depending on the context of
application, context or background of the administrators. Therefore, no deﬁnite stakeholder terminology is
prescribed in the DTU backcasting methodology to remain its ﬂexibility. In the following the basic stakeholder
deﬁnition by Kok et al. (2006) is adopted to allow for a comprehensible description.
In total 11 case studies applied an element of participation. The observations made and learnings gathered
from discussion with experts has found a thorough discussion in section 7.6. Backcasting when applied in
participation can act as a platform for discussion between stakeholders and the policy domain. This element
seamlessly connects to the exchange of information, identiﬁcation of values, perspectives and discovery of
opportunities for a common future. By involving diﬀerent stakeholders a broader picture of the future can be
painted, which ultimately results in relevant information in a policy context. Therefore, it is possible to use
participatory engagement throughout the DTU backcasting methodology in a ﬂexible manner. This provides
the possibility to enhance the DTU methodology as seen ﬁt by the administrators. However, the process of
conducting workshops with a multitude of stakeholders is time and resource-intensive. If participation is de-
cided upon, it should be thought-out where best to involve them in the workshops depending on, the assigned
purpose, step of the method and type of stakeholder group. A useful tool for the identiﬁcation of relevant
stakeholders is stakeholder analysis (Varvasovszky and Brugha, 2000; Reed et al., 2009). In the context of
the DTU methodology, it has to be considered that an application is yet missing to support made conclusion
in practical terms. This includes the recommendations on which stakeholders to include at what stage of a
backcasting application. However, due to the discussion held with experts and observations from literature, a
guideline can be presented. The guideline includes two elements. The ﬁrst element is an overview of stake-
holders (see Table 8.5) and their consideration for each step of the DTU methodology. The second element
includes a recommendation in the form of a heat map (see Figure 8.6) to indicate the level and importance of
participation for each step of the methodology.

==PAGE:133==
Chapter 9
Assessment
To inform policy on an EU or member state level, it is important to be policy-relevant (Tansey et al., 2002).
Thus, in order to provide vital information on identiﬁed solutions and pathways, several considerations should
be included. Thereby, in the context of the DTU backcasting methodology, three assessment categories are pro-
posed including: ’Sustainability Assessment’, ’Performance Assessment’ and ’Preference Assessment’. Each
of the proposed categories is discussed in the following sections after introducing relevant approaches to the
EU Impact Assessment. Since the scope of this thesis is Europe speciﬁc, the EU Impact Assessment was taken
as inspiration for assessing solution elements and scenarios. The EU Impact Assessment is applied for Com-
mission initiatives that potentially have signiﬁcant economic, social or environmental impacts. Legislative
proposals, non-legislative initiatives that involve future policies (for example white papers or action plans) as
well as implementing and delegated acts have to undergo an impact assessment according to EU regulation
(European Commission, 2019b). In section 9.5 of this chapter, a list of tools and concepts that can support the
process of assessment are outlined to provide an overview.
9.1
Approach Utilised in the European Commission’s
Better Regulation Toolbox
Within the European Union, several impact assessments are required for diﬀerent purposes. The most noto-
rious impact assessment is conducted by the European Commission and is outlined in the publication "Better
Regulation Toolbox" by the European Commission (2017). The Commission is required to conduct an impact
assessment for initiatives that have potentially large economic, social and/or environmental impacts. The
approach taken in the better regulation toolbox is a mix of both quantitative and qualitative, stating where
possible impacts should be quantiﬁed. This approach to impact assessment in the EU would normally take a
year to conduct, however, this includes the formal reporting, criteria checks and other requirements necessary
in the policy domain. This level of detail is not required within the backcasting domain and as such, a stream-
lined approach taking only the relevant impact assessment techniques should be considered. The following
sub-sections indicate some of the useful techniques and concepts which are relevant for the DTU backcasting
methodology.
9.1.1
Impact chains
Each solution or policy option is aimed to address a problem by causing direct and/or indirect behaviourally
changes to the drivers of the problem. Thus, the ﬁrst step is to consider the chain of impacts that can occur
from implementing a solution option. This can be achieved by questioning: "What are the direct and indirect
120
==PAGE:134==
Chapter 9. Assessment
DTU Environment
behaviour changes, why are they required and whom does it aﬀect?". To be coherent, six potentially aﬀected
groups are categorised, the following groups identiﬁed by the European Commission (2017) are:
• Citizens – Whenever changes are widespread and do not aﬀect any particular subgroup.
• Consumers – Whenever users of a particular product/service are aﬀected.
• Workers – Whenever employees in general or in a speciﬁc industry are aﬀected.
• Enterprises – Businesses in general or in a speciﬁc sector. It is also useful to distinguish enterprises
according to their size (micro, small, medium and large) whenever they are subject to diﬀerent regulatory
requirements (i.e. exemptions and special regimes) or are likely to be aﬀected diﬀerently by the same
policy measure.
• Public authorities – Distinguished between EU, national and sub-national levels as appropriate.
• Third countries – Whenever they are directly aﬀected. Relevant subgroups of countries (main trading
partners, developing countries, etc.) and within countries, entities (i.e. businesses, citizens, etc.) should
be used.
9.1.2
Screening, Selecting and Quantifying Signiﬁcant Impacts
When conducting an impact assessment there typically is a plethora of impacts which can be overwhelming.
These impacts vary in terms of severity and a number of them will be on an insigniﬁcant level and as such
do not necessarily require a comprehensive analysis. To determine the considerable impacts, ﬁrst, all impacts
must be screened. The ﬁrst step is to identify all the positive and negative impacts of a selected solution or
pathway option. These include direct, indirect, intentional, non-intentional, short-term and long-term im-
pacts. To be holistic economic, social and environmental dimensions of the impacts must be considered. To
provide guidance a table indicating all the economic social and environmental impact categories relevant to
consider is presented. This can be seen in Table 6 in section E - Appendix.
Once all the impacts have been considered, it is important to single out which impacts are signiﬁcant. This is
achieved using the principle of proportionate analysis which takes into consideration the criteria presented
below. This process must take stakeholders’ and experts’ views into the examination. Thus, once conducted,
all signiﬁcant positive and negative impacts should have been identiﬁed.
• The relevance of the impact within the intervention logic. This takes into consideration the im-
pacts associated with key elements that contribute to the achievement of the objectives.
• The absolute magnitude of the expected impacts. I.e. impacts which have a larger eﬀect in com-
parison to the other impacts.
• The relative size of expected impacts for speciﬁc stakeholders. This takes impacts into consider-
ation that may have a small absolute impact, however potentially large impacts to certain stakeholders.
121
==PAGE:135==
Chapter 9. Assessment
DTU Environment
• The importance of impacts for the Commission’s horizontal objectives and policies. This is to
consider important impacts which may conﬂict with other important political objectives.
Once the signiﬁcant impacts have been identiﬁed, it is important to assess each of them quantitatively where
possible. The European Commission does not recommend a procedure to assess signiﬁcant impacts, however,
provides certain tools which can be used including life cycle assessments, use of analytical models, multi-
criteria analysis and cost-beneﬁt analysis (European Commission, 2017). The quantiﬁcation and assessment
of identiﬁed signiﬁcant impacts are further explained in the following assessment categories of the DTU Back-
asting Methodology.
9.2
Sustainability Assessment
The terminology sustainability assessment is used to assess the impacts of the solutions and pathways. Sus-
tainability assessment is a more recent development which has emerged from the ﬁeld of environmental impact
assessment and strategic environmental assessment (SEA) (Bond et al., 2012). It aims at informing decision-
makers on sustainability trades-oﬀs and directing developments towards sustainability. In this methodology,
the intention is to assess the impacts relative to the sustainability conditions. Further, these conditions can
incorporate the three dimensions of sustainability, hence, the terminology sustainability assessment (instead
of impact assessment, environmental assessment or SEA) was deemed appropriate. This assessment category
takes outset in the EU Impact Assessment and sets the target to acquire relevant knowledge about possible im-
pacts, trade-oﬀs, ﬁrst and second-order consequences of identiﬁed solutions, pathways or scenarios. There are
a variety of tools used to assess sustainability observed in the literature. In the context of the DTU backcasting
methodology, they encompass multi-criteria analysis, life cycle analysis and the use of integrated assessment
and system dynamic models. The presentation of the state of the art in this ﬁeld is out of the scope of this
thesis, however, some key tools have been identiﬁed which are brieﬂy outlined in section 9.5.
9.3
Assessing Performance
The term ’performance’ is used in the context of the DTU backcasting methodology about the objectives of the
study. In particular, performance is measured in the context of eﬃciency and eﬀectiveness. This is inspired by
criteria applied in the European Better Regulation Toolbox (European Commission, 2017). In the case of the
performance assessment proposed in this methodology, eﬀectiveness is hereby deﬁned as the progress a so-
lution makes towards achieving the objectives of the study. In comparison, eﬃciency is deﬁned as the option
that provides the best outcome compared to the necessary input (e.g. monetary investments). Determining
eﬃciency is useful to measure performance in both the solution assessment as well as the pathway assess-
ment. However, eﬀectiveness is only relevant for assessing performance in the solution assessment step or for
segments of a pathway, since each pathway as a whole should inherently be normative and as such, should
achieve the objectives of the study, by deﬁnition. The range of applicable tools to assess performance that can
be considered in the context of the DTU methodology includes multi-criteria analysis, cost-eﬀectiveness anal-
ysis and integrated assessment models. These tools are not exhaustive, however, provide a good basic toolset
122
==PAGE:136==
Chapter 9. Assessment
DTU Environment
to assess the performance of solutions and scenarios. It is important to state that assumptions about future de-
velopments or states of a solution or scenario should be excluded from the assessment of performance, unless
solid scientiﬁc evidence can be provided to support such assumptions. This is to generate a common ground
for comparison based on current knowledge.
9.4
Assessing Preference
As gathered from previous learnings the inclusion of a preference assessment is a vital component to form
a holistic assessment. The outcome of the assessment can be used to inform decision-makers about the per-
ceived preferability towards individual solutions in pathways, scenarios or future images by stakeholders.
As outlined in the learnings, the preferability can be related to pathways and future images individually as
multiple pathways can be detached if wanted. This is due to the possibility of generating multiple pathways
towards each future image which can be reﬂected separately in terms of preferability as a result. As is the case
for other parts of the DTU backcasting methodology, the preference assessment is dependent on the purpose,
choices of methodological components and desired outcomes of the backcasting application.
The element of preference can be assessed in qualitative and quantitative terms, depending on the choice
of tools. A qualitative assessment is capable of providing detailed information about the perceived prefer-
ence towards pathways and future images in text form. However, this can be challenging as the complexity
and amount of information can be extensive. Therefore, quantitative approaches could be applied. Such
approaches include the ranking of preferred solutions or pathways or the assignment of numeric values to
portray a participant’s preference. Although the quantitative approach facilitates the documentation of pref-
erence, it does not allow elaborate information to be documented in a comprehensible way. Another approach
that could be taken regarding quantitative assessment is the translation of qualitative information into nu-
meric values in economic terms. Concepts such as willingness-to-pay or willingness-to-accept are frequently
applied within an economical domain. These approaches bring their own merits and limitations, and despite
their relevance in an economic context, are not considered in more detail in the context of this thesis. Assess-
ing preference can be conducted by the use of multiple tools and concepts, such as multi-criteria analysis or
mapping, surveying or simulation games. All of which are outlined in section 9.5.
9.5
Overview of Available Tools and Techniques
The list of tools provided is not exhaustive as the assessment step in regards to backcasting was not the
focus of this thesis and, as observed in the literature, is lacking concrete supportive information. As such,
the tools outlined in the following describe basic considerations of understanding what each tool entails. If
tools are to be utilised further expertise is required to conduct such analysis. It is worth mentioning that the
assessment in terms of the DTU backcasting methodology suﬃcient tools should be considered to cover all
areas of assessment that are deemed relevant to the study. Further, consideration should be made on the most
competent tools given the scope and boundaries of the study. Every outlined tool has its merits and diﬀerent
123
==PAGE:137==
Chapter 9. Assessment
DTU Environment
application possibilities, which in combination can provide a medley of relevant information.
9.5.1
Multi-Criteria Analysis
Multi-criteria analysis is a powerful tool which can be used in support of decision making by comparing multi-
ple options. The term multi-criteria is often used in support of cost-beneﬁt analysis, an approach that was not
further touched upon in this list. MCA encompasses diﬀerent types, forms and procedures of analysis (Dodg-
son et al., 2009) and is therefore generalised in the following to provide a basic overview of key procedural
elements. The use of multi-criteria analysis tools involves criteria, scores and weightings to each of the criteria.
In principle, the choice of criteria can be made freely or set before the conduction of the analysis. Typically,
multi-criteria analysis is performed using participants to form decision-making teams. These groups are then
used to form a judgement on a range of options based on the use of indicators (Drechsler, 2010). Alternatively,
this tool can be applied in an isolated expert setting. The assignment of a score can be done both in qualitative
and quantitative terms. However, the beneﬁt of applying a quantitative approach based on assigning numeric
values as scores is that it facilitates the comparison between a series of options (Soria-Lara and Banister, 2018).
Indicators to describe the sustainability impacts of the backcasting application can be used as criteria for multi-
criteria analysis. An example of such an approach was observed in Soria-Lara and Banister (2018), where
sustainability conditions were deﬁned in terms of environmental, social and economic dimensions. There-
fore, indicators for each category were deﬁned and applied in the analysis. Environmental impacts need to be
based on multi-disciplinary research and can be highly complex. Therefore, expertise from legal, economic,
ecological and ethical domains should be represented (Drechsler, 2010). In the context of the DTU backcasting
methodology and speciﬁcally sustainability assessment, MCA must be applied about the sustainability con-
ditions set out in step 1. Thereby, the number of criteria would be aligned with set sustainability conditions
or choice of indicators. This allows to directly reﬂect the impacts of pathways and scenarios caused by the
chosen sustainability conditions of the backcasting application.
When considering to apply MCA in regards to performance assessment it can be said that the approach is
very similar. Here, the criteria should be related to indicators, which were chosen as the base for comparison
in terms of eﬃciency and eﬀectiveness. Diﬃculties may arise from elements and factors which are complex
and therefore not easily measurable in a dedicated quantitative form. As mentioned, one way of determining
eﬃciency and eﬀectiveness is to translate the input into monetary value. This is advantageous as it provides a
standard in which comparisons can be made. This would further allow for application in an economic context.
When applying multi-criteria analysis, some limitations need to be acknowledged. The use of quantitative
assessment schemes provides the possibility to rank and compare diﬀerent assessed pathways or solution el-
ements. However, a single numeric value is unlikely to provide suﬃcient detail to inform a decision. Due to
the in-built assumptions and numerical generalisation, the level of detail is reduced which could have been
included in a qualitative assessment form. An additional consideration in this regard is the element of partic-
ipation. It has to be guaranteed that suﬃcient expertise is available in the assessment process. Participation
of stakeholders, such as business or public representatives might be able to reﬂect their preference in terms of
124
==PAGE:138==
Chapter 9. Assessment
DTU Environment
ranking. However, these stakeholders should not be included when assessing environmental factual impacts
due to their potential bias and misinformed stance (Kasper Kok, Personal Communication, 2020). Considering
both quantitative and qualitative information in MCA can be considered beneﬁcial, which is accommodated
for in multi-criteria mapping.
9.5.2
Multi-Criteria Mapping
The tool of multi-criteria mapping (MCM) combines the elements of quantitative multi-criteria analysis as out-
lined prior and an additional qualitative element (Stirling and Mayer, 2000). The software tool was developed
by the STEPS Centre - the University of Sussex, pioneered by Andy Sterling. It is aimed to aid decision-making
by exploring contrasting perspectives on complex issues. Therefore, it systematically ’maps’ out the practical
implications of alternative options, knowledge and values. Similar to the multi-criteria analysis approach seen
in Soria-Lara and Banister (2018), the software tool applies criteria, scores and weighting procedures to map
the preferences of participants. The additional beneﬁt gained from using the multi-criteria mapping tool is the
qualitative element behind the reasoning for the choice of criteria and assignment of scores (Dodgson et al.,
2009).
In application, the MCM tool can be applied with predeﬁned criteria or allowing the participant to choose
several criteria and factors. The latter is done to open a reﬂection space without constraints on the assess-
ment of preferences, however, will increase diﬃculties for comparison of results between participants. Either
of the outlined approaches is considered suitable in the DTU backcasting methodology. During the evalua-
tion of preference, participants are asked to elaborate on their decisions in a qualitative manner as well as on
further details regarding their decisions (such as personal-judgement or expressed uncertainty). Thereby, the
tool generates an overview that portrays relevant uncertainties and their practical implications. This element
provides transparency in the documentation of results. In the following step, participants are asked to assign
weights to each chosen criteria according to traditional MCA procedures. The software analyses the contribu-
tions by participants and processes both, quantitative and qualitative data to generate a ’map’ of perspectives
including the reasoning behind each perspective (Stirling, 2006). For a more detailed representation of the
procedures behind the multi-criteria mapping software, refer to the documentation of University of Sussex.1
9.5.3
Life Cycle Assessment
Life Cycle Assessment (LCA), is a decision support tool which can be used to assess and compare impacts
on environmental sustainability (Rosenbaum et al., 2018). This approach is detailed and renders quantitative
impacts which are beneﬁcial to help inform the generation or assessment of pathways. Due to the complexity
and time requirements of this assessment tool, it is more appropriate for the solution assessment. It is bene-
ﬁcial to conduct a generic assessment of an individual solution as they are more simplistic in comparison to
complex pathways. Nevertheless, it is still possible to conduct an LCA to assess pathways, given a common
functional unit can be identiﬁed between the pathways (Giurco et al., 2011). A functional unit as deﬁned in
Rosenbaum et al. (2018) is "a quantitative description of the function or service for which the assessment is
1Weblink to MCM - University of Sussex: http://www.sussex.ac.uk/mcm/
125
==PAGE:139==
Chapter 9. Assessment
DTU Environment
performed, and the basis of determining the reference ﬂow of product that scales the data collection in the
next LCA phase, the inventory analysis." In plain text, this scales the analysis such that a fair comparison
can be made. However, due to the complexity of elements constituting a pathway, conducting an LCA on
pathway options may take considerable time. The purpose of LCA can vary, however, in the context of the
DTU backcasting methodology, its use is to explore the relative lifetime impacts of solutions and/or pathways
to compare the diﬀerent options. LCA is a complex approach which has further requirements in regards to
meeting ISO standards and as such, a step-wise procedure could not be presented in this section. If LCA is to
be included concerning the DTU methodology, further reference to the handbook developed by Rosenbaum
et al. (2018) and internationally standardised procedures (ISO14040 and ISO14044:2006) is recommended. Nev-
ertheless, there are still numerous relevant considerations, which are discussed below.
LCA aims at assessing the environmental impacts of a product or system over its total lifetime (Guinée et al.,
2011). This spans across raw material extraction, manufacturing, usage and end-of-life. In LCA, environmental
impacts are categorised into impact categories. Impact categories eﬀectively group the emissions of a prod-
uct or system and attribute them to one impact category, based on their ability to contribute to the problem
area (Stranddorf et al., 2004). Examples of impact categories include climate change, ozone layer depletion,
acidiﬁcation and human toxicity. Further, the type and quantity of these categories vary depending on the
database and method of assessment chosen in LCA (Guinée and Heijungs, 2017). The impact categories in
LCA studies are comparable to the ’sustainability conditions’ in the DTU backcasting methodology. Take the
examples above, climate change and acidiﬁcation are similar to the planetary boundaries which are included
in the doughnut economics model exempliﬁed in section 8.1.2. However, the impact categories used in LCA
may not necessarily align with the chosen sustainability conditions. Thus, it is important to use the results
of the LCA to inform the impacts in regards to the sustainability conditions. Additionally, the sustainability
condition may include both elements of social and economic sustainability. However, recent developments in
the ﬁeld are moving towards social life cycle assessments (SLCA) and life cycle costing (LCC) (Guinée et al.,
2011; Benoît Norris et al., 2013; Guinée and Heijungs, 2017; Rosenbaum et al., 2018). Together LCA, LCC and
SLCA can be combined to form a more comprehensive Life Cycle Sustainability Assessment (LCSA) which
can be used to assess all three pillars of sustainability. LCC, LCSA and SLCA are all established approaches,
however, the latter two are still juvenile compared to the established LCA framework. Thus, an increase in
uncertainty of the assessment can occur. Nevertheless, LCSA is still a relevant assessment tool and can be
beneﬁcial depending on whether the social and economic dimensions of sustainability are deﬁned in step 2 of
the backcasting methodology.
The quality of an LCA is largely dependant on the representativeness and availability of the data and as
such, assumptions have to be made to ﬁll in gaps in the data sets Rosenbaum et al. (2018). A combination of
backcasting and LCA has been observed in the case study by Giurco et al. (2011). They discuss the concept of a
streamlined LCA as a less comprehensive and data-intensive process, stating that it is commonly used in other
scenario assessment applications. This streamlined approach is appealing in scenario studies as frequently
representative data is not available. However, in the case study by Giurco et al. (2011) a streamlined LCA is
not conducted as it was not possible to deﬁne a common functional unit across all of the scenarios. Thus,
they took a life cycle approach to the assessment but instead relied on other assessment concepts including
126
==PAGE:140==
Chapter 9. Assessment
DTU Environment
sustainability assessment and strategic environmental assessment (SEA).
9.5.4
Use of Modelling Tools
The application of models can be of use in terms of assessing sustainability as well as performance. A number
of analytical models are available to assess diﬀerent impacts for a range of diﬀerent societal sectors. These
models replicate the interactions between the planetary physical dimensions and interaction with the human
system (Rotmans and Asselt, 2006; van Vuuren and Hof, 2018). Two types of models were identiﬁed in re-
viewed case studies and discussion with experts, being system dynamics and integrated assessment models as
stated in Chapter 7. The application of models as seen from reviewed case studies was exclusively done in an
ex-ante fashion, meaning before or during the backcasting sessions. An application after the completion of a
backcasting study, aimed at assessing complete pathways or scenarios, was not directly observed. Therefore,
it is disputable in which way analytical models can be applied in a backcasting application. The investigation
and elaboration on modelling tools in the context of the DTU backcasting methodology lies outside the scope
of the thesis. To provide a basic understanding of their application, reference is made in the following to the
details provided in the Better Regulation Toolbox.
The purpose of analytical models in regards to the DTU methodology is to provide relevant information for
subsequent use in the methodology or to inform decision-makers (van Vuuren, 2015). The type and quality
of delivered information for use diﬀers depending on, the purpose of the application, level of quality and
applied model. As observed in reviewed case studies, models were applied to generate information based on
assumptions and decisions made during the backcasting application. By using models such as system dynamics
models or integrated assessment models, it is possible to test stated assumptions of the backcasting application
in regards to future trends and developments (Detlef van Vuuren, Personal Communication, 2020). Further,
models can be used in such a way that they analyse generated pathways to provide lowest cost pathways,
which can be useful in terms of the performance assessment. An additional application of models lies in
regards to the generation and optimisation of pathways in an iterative approach, as it allows to identify deeper
levels of ’what’. As an example, the exact quantity of wind turbines that would be needed to achieve a certain
increase in renewable energy share and their related cost. As further listed in the Better Regulation Toolbox,
models such as general or partial equilibrium models and econometric models can provide insight on shocks
and behaviour of economic systems due to made choices in a future scenario (European Commission, 2017).
Another example can be integrated assessment models which speciﬁcally serve to support the policy domain.
Such models provide insight into the implications of policy decisions on multiple areas (Wang et al., 2017; van
Vuuren and Hof, 2018). This is achieved through integrating diﬀerent disciplines and interweaving models into
a framework which is capable of providing information on system behaviour and trade-oﬀs between diﬀerent
sectors of a system. These disciplines range from macroeconomics to engineering science, environmental
science, earth system science and so-called ’operational science’ (van Vuuren and Hof, 2018) encompassing
amongst others land use, agricultural, transport, and energy models. The use of integrated models allows
consideration of what-if questions related to policy action. Such an approach is often seen in the climate
policy domain, where policy decision can be modelled against planetary models depicting the change in the
system relevant variables such as temperature, CO2 levels, etc.
127
==PAGE:141==
Chapter 9. Assessment
DTU Environment
• Econometric models
• Partial equilibrium models
• Micro-simulation models
• Input-output models
• Integrated (assessment) modelling approaches
When considering to apply modelling tools in regards to assessing sustainability or performance in the DTU
backcasting methodology, it is recommended to provide detailed documentation on the procedures and un-
derlying information. This should include elements such as Quality assurance, sensitivity and uncertainty
analysis and transparency as elaborated upon in the Better Regulation Toolbox - Tool #62 (European Commis-
sion, 2017).
When assessing impacts and trade-oﬀs through the use of models, some limitations need to be considered.
The development and use of models are time and resource-intensive and need adequately trained profes-
sionals with a background in science and modelling depending on the use of modelling software (European
Commission, 2017). The increase in complexity of a model, for example adding components and sectors to
a model, brings additional implications. The addition of complexity may increase the quality of output, in
return, however, will increase the number of variables and parameters used in the model framework. This
increases the resources needed for development and the diﬃculty of interpreting the model results. Further,
the level of complexity is related to the scale of the model which can vary from local to global conﬁgurations.
Depending on the available data sets for respective scales, limitations may occur regarding the scalability
of models (Kasper Kok, Personal Communication, 2020). Integrated models have achieved a high degree of
reliability in depicting relationships between diﬀerent disciplines, especially in terms of technical domains.
However, the social dimension of a system is not depictable to a comprehensive degree in such models (Detlef
van Vuuren, Personal Communication, 2020). A further limitation can be linked to the interpretation of re-
sults. Generating numeric values from models may seem desirable when it comes to comparing pathways or
scenarios. However, the degree of detail related to this information is being lost when generalising and there-
fore, false conﬁdence in models can lead to wrong conclusions (Kasper Kok, Personal Communication, 2020).
As addressed in the SOER, integrated assessment models can misguide if used in isolation (EEA, 2019a). This
is due to the important fact that these models neglect the fundamental characteristics of transitions. As EEA
(2019a) outlines, these elements are related to the role of shocks, resistance in systems, radical innovation,
actors and institutions as well as social practices and behavioural shifts.
9.5.5
Surveying
One way of assessing preference is by conducting surveys. Generally, speaking surveys can be structured
and organised in a multitude of ways. However, for the sake of simplicity, the two main tools of question-
naires and interviews are considered in the following. A questionnaire entails a set of standardised questions
which follow a ﬁxed scheme to collect individual data about one or more speciﬁc topics (Trobia, 2008). Typ-
ical forms of questionnaires include market research; political polling; evaluations; opinion polls; and social
science research (O’Leary, 2017). In comparison, an interview is typically a face-to-face conversation between
128
==PAGE:142==
Chapter 9. Assessment
DTU Environment
a researcher and a participant involving a transfer of information to the interviewer (Creswell, 2009). The
intention and thereby structure of the chosen survey tool can inﬂuence the outcome of the preference assess-
ment. As an example, participants or external evaluators could be speciﬁcally asked to rate certain elements
of a pathway, which provides deeper insight into which solutions and elements of a pathway were preferred
compared to elements of other pathways. As observed in the review of case studies, participatory backcasting
studies applied a range of surveying tools, i.e. questionnaires or interviews (Sisto et al., 2016, 2018; Musse
et al., 2018). These tools were either applied in a workshop setting with personal interactions or through on-
line media (such as e-mail surveys).
The method of surveying can consume a considerable amount of time due to the need for the preparation of
the questionnaire or interview and the response by the interviewees. The success of a questionnaire might
further be related to the achieved response rate and therefore, does not guarantee a holistic preference as-
sessment. Similar limitations can be seen for conducting interviews. While this approach could potentially
achieve better coverage of perspectives, the time required per participant might exceed the available time for
conducting the preference assessment.
These presented tools can also be used in the form of online or software-based media such as public engage-
ment software. An example of which is MetroQuest.2 In times of modernisation and expansion of computer
devices, this allows to quickly and easily spread surveys across a range of participants. This element can be
taken even further with the inclusion of simulation and gaming apps as elaborated in the following.
9.5.6
Simulation and Gaming Apps
The use of simulation and gaming apps can be considered to assess the preferences of participants towards the
results of a backcasting application. This approach was seen in the Georgia basin futures project which was
documented in the work by Robinson et al. (2011). The software applied was GB-QUEST, a simulation/gam-
ing software designed amongst others by Prof. Dr. Robinson (Robinson, 2003; Carmichael et al., 2004). The
computer-based tool works interactively with participants who inﬂuence the input of the simulation. The
participants are asked to explore possible future scenarios in terms of social, environmental and economic
impacts of their choices. Robinson et al. (2011) state that in the context of collective simulation games, partic-
ipants would express their preferences about future outcomes through scenario choices and discussion with
other participants in the scenario generation process. Therefore, the use of simulation and gaming apps seems
most beneﬁcial in the context of exploring diﬀerent future images in a participatory setting. Through the use
of GB-QUEST, participants receive detailed information regarding their choices about "uncertainties involved,
unavoidable trade-oﬀs, higher-order consequences, the role of world views and values, and the inﬂuence of
global forces on local issues" (Robinson et al., 2011). Another example of model-based simulation software is
the work by the British Government, the UK pathway generator, which can be visited here.3
A similar approach as seen in both examples above could be considered in terms of assessing preference of
2Weblink to MetroQuest: https://metroquest.com
3Weblink to UK Pathway Generator: https://www.gov.uk/guidance/2050-pathways-analysis
129
==PAGE:143==
Chapter 9. Assessment
DTU Environment
workshop participants as they can be applied in multiple ways. The approach further facilitates the interpre-
tation of results and creates a strong narrative between the future and participants (John Robinson, Personal
Communication, 2020). However, this concept needs further development and groundwork in the context of
the DTU backcasting methodology as some limitations can be observed. First, the trade-oﬀs and impact as-
sessments related to the choices of the participants have to be assessed before the design of the app. Second,
the complexity of scenarios and pathways might be impossible to replicate in such tools and therefore, would
inﬂuence the quality of preference statements obtained. Third, the processes of developing such applications
are labour intensive and expensive to run which could ﬁnd constraints in terms of the project budget.
