==PAGE:144==
Chapter 10
Discussion and Recommendations
This section of the document reﬂects upon the ﬁndings of this thesis, key considerations concerning the limits
of this work, as well as the reﬂection on delimitations and further research topics. The discussion is presented
in two separate parts, which is related to the individual structural elements of the DTU backcasting method-
ology in section 10.1 and to backcasting in a practical and research context in section 10.2. These reﬂections
are necessary to gain a better understanding of the proposed methodology, its beneﬁts and limitations as well
as to understand its relevance in a European policy context. Further, this section elaborates on related topics
and components to backcasting that need additional discussion.
As outlined in the introduction of this thesis, the work carried out aimed at providing a comprehensible
review of the state of the art, identiﬁcation of methodological gaps in existing backcasting methodologies
and associated to that the relevance of backcasting in a European policy context. For the conduction of the
thesis, several delimitations were considered to accommodate for the limited amount of time available. These
delimitations are further discussed are related to:
• theoretical background of sustainable development and sustainability conditions
• ethics in backcasting
• exploration of modelling
• related topics in future and foresight studies
• (to an extent) assessment in relation to backcasting (economic assessment, risk analysis, uncertainty
analysis)
• conduction of a full-breadth case study
10.1
Structural Elements of DTU Backcasting
Methodology
The following section discusses the presented DTU backcasting methodology and selected elements of its
structure. In particular, the elements of sustainability conditions, pathway generation, assessment, evaluation
and iteration are discussed. The ﬁndings regarding each element are brieﬂy presented and reﬂected upon to
provide discussion and to outline topics that could be further explored in future research.
131
==PAGE:145==
Chapter 10. Discussion and Recommendations
DTU Environment
10.1.1
Sustainability Conditions
The choice of sustainability conditions is an important consideration in the DTU backcasting methodology.
The deﬁnition of sustainability conditions is part of a larger topic, which is delimited from the scope of this
thesis, hence why, the work on sustainability principles originating from backcasting from principles was
adopted. Nevertheless, this element needs discussion as it generated several relevant reﬂections during the
development of the DTU methodology.
Sustainability conditions serve to deﬁne the safe operating space of the study and thus, can inﬂuence many
structural elements of the methodology. In particular, the choice of sustainability conditions aﬀects the as-
sessment of the solutions and pathways as well on the generation of pathways. Thus, it is important to be
holistic when deﬁning sustainability conditions. They must be able to eﬀectively assess solutions so vital
information in regards to, as a minimum environmental and social, trade-oﬀs can be determined. If this is
not the case the sustainability character of the study might be ﬂawed and render outcomes of the study, in
fact, unsustainable and might, therefore, lead to an undesired future. The drawback with more detailed and
holistic conditions is that more work is required to assess them and in many cases if they are too complex, it
is not possible (Tue Noa Jacques Damsø, Personal Communication, 2020). Using fewer conditions can render
more accurate assessments and also, simpliﬁes the assessment procedures. This concept could be beneﬁcial
when including participants in a quantitative or qualitative assessment as seen in the case study by Soria-Lara
and Banister (2018). Experts were consulted to determine what impacts were vital to consider and as such,
provided essential information relevant to the assessment of the study. However, it could be argued that as-
sessing environmental or social impacts by including participants in the form of expert judgement might not
necessarily lead to constructive assessments due to individual bias and possible uninformed stances.
The ﬁve criteria for sustainability conditions taken from Broman and Robèrt (2017) were adopted in the context
of the DTU backcasting methodology. In the context of backcasting from principles, a total of 8 sustainability
conditions are determined which provide a systemic and holistic approach to sustainability. As the discussion
of sustainability conditions was not an integral part of this thesis, only the ﬁve criteria for sustainability condi-
tions were taken forward and require further consideration. The adoption of the sustainability criteria leaves
space for determination of sustainability conditions as intended in the DTU methodology. This is beneﬁcial as
this supports the element of ﬂexibility and oﬀers the administrators of the study the option to include what is
deemed relevant for their sustainability concept. As an example, it could be considered to apply the Sustain-
able Development Goals, Doughnut Economics or after all the sustainability principles as outlined by Broman
and Robèrt (2017). It can be argued that this ﬂexibility can cause a bending of the term sustainability, which
is not the intention of the sustainability conditions. However, it might be necessary to conﬂict with certain
elements of holistic sustainability to accommodate social sustainability or economic sustainability concepts,
which can be applied in terms of the DTU methodology if desired.
Another reﬂection gathered on the use of sustainability conditions are concerning the application of prior
mentioned sustainability frameworks and concepts. To provide an example based on the Broman and Robèrt
(2017) criteria, the use of ’doughnut economics’ as sustainability conditions has potential limitations. Although
132
==PAGE:146==
Chapter 10. Discussion and Recommendations
DTU Environment
the conditions from the doughnut economics model are suﬃcient, in parts they are unnecessary, overlapping
and not concrete, nor general. There are 21 individual conditions in the doughnut economics model which
could be simpliﬁed. Many of the planetary boundaries, such as novel entities and functional diversity, are still
unclear and thus, not concrete. There are certain overlaps in the conditions such as climate change and ocean
acidiﬁcation due to the mutual sensibility to the concentration of CO2 in the atmosphere (Steﬀen et al., 2015;
Will Steﬀen, Personal Communication, 2020). Another consideration of the sustainability conditions is the
scope and context of the study. The use of doughnut economics or planetary boundaries is not applicable for
small scale studies due to the global deﬁnition of planetary boundaries, e.g. deﬁnition of freshwater boundaries
which are not directly related to geographical and political boundaries (Will Steﬀen, Personal Communication,
2020). The scalability of such approaches to smaller regions is therefore problematic and could breach the
condition of being a ’general’ sustainability condition. Thus, consideration must be made so that the conditions
are speciﬁc to the scope of the study or designing conditions which are both applicable on local levels as well as
on global levels to suit the criteria ’general’. A deeper reﬂection of how diﬀerent sustainability concepts relate
to the deﬁned criteria was not conducted in the context of this thesis and should ﬁnd further consideration.
The fundamental and extensive work by the Natural Step and in that regard especially by Prof. Dr. Robèrt and
Prof. Dr. Broman is recognised, however, was not be represented to their full extent due to the delimitation of
the thesis.
10.1.2
Pathway Generation
The structural element ’Pathways Towards the Objectives’ receives a lot of attention in the DTU backcasting
methodology. This sections of the methodology are linked with the highest uncertainty as very little support-
ive literature, nor mentioning across reviewed case studies, was found. Thus, the ﬁndings of the proposed
structural element in regards to pathway generation should be discussed.
The structure for generating pathways presented in the DTU backcasting methodology targets the elements
of pathway trajectory by setting milestone and target setting, the choice of solutions and their order of action.
These levels of a pathway were further enhanced by providing ﬁve approaches that can support the generation
of pathways in an internal or participatory workshop. These approaches were partially identiﬁed through lit-
erature and partially in discussion with experts. Contrary to a structured procedure in generating pathways,
Assoc. Prof. Dr. Hickman was of the opinion that this step should entirely rely on the creative contribution of
participants. He argues that following structured procedures would limit the ability to think ’outside the box’
and to come up with beneﬁcial solutions (Robin Hickman, Personal Communication, 2020). However, Assoc.
Prof. Dr. Hickman also emphasised that there might be diﬀerences in the reaction of participants to this ap-
proach depending on the participants and their background. He argued that typically engineers and scientists
of technical or nature-related topics tend to cling to guidelines and instructions, whereas participants with
a less technical background (as e.g. social sciences or artists) normally welcome this freedom in their work
(Robin Hickman, Personal Communication, 2020). This consideration in regards to generating pathways is
acknowledged and should ﬁnd consideration on the side of the administrators before the start of a work ses-
sion. It could be argued that the underlying purpose of the backcast will heavily inﬂuence the type of pathway
generation. If the purpose is to spark discussion between a number of diverse stakeholders then it might not
133
==PAGE:147==
Chapter 10. Discussion and Recommendations
DTU Environment
be beneﬁcial to structure a pathway including presented approaches. In such settings, it would be of higher
beneﬁt to leaving more room for creativity which in return sparks discussion. The learnings and information
that can be gathered from discussion alone can be more valuable than a vague pathway construct based on
uninformed assumptions.
Respecting the chosen delimitations it can be stated that the approaches suggested in regards to pathway
generation need further discussion and research. In practical terms, it was not possible to determine what
possible role the application of diﬀerent types of models could have when generating pathways. Some appli-
cations were seen in reviewed case studies and aspects of using models were discussed with experts. However,
as no practical application of the methodology was conducted conclusions about possible implications by us-
ing models are uncertain. A similar statement could be made about the elements of risk and uncertainty
analysis which could form part of the assessment. The incorporation of these concepts could beneﬁt the path-
way generation and in terms of providing relevant information on risk and uncertainty in a policy context.
These topics are recommended to be further explored with pathway generation. Other topics that could ﬁnd
further research to gain insight into the generation of pathways are the topics of transformation and transition
science and the related topic of development pathways.
10.1.3
Assessment
The assessment as it is described in the DTU backcasting methodology represents a simpliﬁed approach to
assessing sustainability, performance and preference across systems. As observed in reviewed case studies,
the assessment of proposed pathways is not considered at all or only to a low degree. The exception to this is
the preference assessment. The tools and techniques outlined in the preference assessment are found in much
of the backcasting literature and were indicated through discussion with experts in the ﬁeld. This comes back
to the concept of desirability, typically in backcasting from scenarios. As stated by Prof. Dr. Robinson, de-
sirability is a function of the endpoint, and how the scenario unfolds. When discussing desirable futures in a
democratic society, it tends to refer to collective desirable futures and as such, there needs to be transparency
and consultation with all stakeholders. This is why the participatory backcasting ﬁeld and second-order back-
casting emerged. Hence, most of the tools indicated in the preference assessment have been applied in a prior
backcasting context, although they have not been tested in the form of this methodology.
The remaining assessment steps, i.e. sustainability assessment and performance assessment have seldom
found their place in backcasting studies. For this reason, the assessment step is largely theoretical and needs
further research. The tools and techniques indicted in the sustainability and performance assessment section
(9), have seldom found their way into backcasting studies and as such are not tried and tested. Although the
authors of this thesis have some expertise in some of the assessment techniques including LCA, the remaining
tools are based upon supporting literature such as the EU Better Regulation Toolbox European Commission
(2017). Thus, it is not possible to discuss how these tools perform, nor provide reﬂections or recommendations
on which tools should be used. These reﬂections would provide, credibility to the methodology, allow for more
concrete instructions and hence, streamline an eﬀective methodology. This presents a limitation of the DTU
backcasting methodology.
134
==PAGE:148==
Chapter 10. Discussion and Recommendations
DTU Environment
An additional element that could be included in the assessment, is risk assessment. Determining the risk of
a pathway or solution element is particularly important to inform politicians on the uncertainty associated
with a described options. This topic has potential fruitful beneﬁts by enabling backcasting to become more
informative and hence, increasing policy relevance. However, it could undervalue backcasting studies by
highlighting risks which are inherent to large scale transformative change. This is related to the concept of
post-normal science. The climate issue on an EU level can be argued to be operating in a post-normal science
setting. This is since the action must be taken now to reduce the risk of climate and ecological breakdown.
Yet, at the same time, the uncertainty around how to act is high Sardar and Sweeney (2016). Backcasting aims
to help tackle this issue by being an eﬀective integrated assessment tool, however, the fear in highlighting
the underlying risks of future planning could increase the gap between science and policy action, in a time
where urgent action is required. Nevertheless, risk analysis should still be explored as it can help identify the
lower-risk options, which if a post-normal reality is recognised, is beneﬁcial for politicians.
10.1.4
Evaluation and Iteration
The structural element ’evaluation and iteration’, provides the option to assess the progress of existing and im-
plemented policy choices in comparison to proposed pathways. Thereby, it is possible to assess what progress
to set objectives was made until the time of iteration. The iteration of a backcasting study provides a powerful
element of the methodology and expands the approach from a ’one-shot’ methodology to a strategic future
planning concept that can be applied over a longer time-span and thereby, accompany the transition process.
This again depends on the purpose of the backcasting application. The evaluation of progress and iteration
step requires further discussion as they have many implications on the whole of the backcasting methodology.
The evaluation process provides necessary information for the next iteration of the backcasting application,
which might inﬂuence the procedure in multiple ways. This element of the DTU backcasting methodology
can be applied ﬂexibly and moulded depending on the purpose chosen by the administrators of the study. The
administrators might consider continuing with previously generated pathways or abandon previous work to
generate entirely new pathways due to the occurring changes in the system or underlying long-term objec-
tives. Continuing with previously generated pathways could save time due to made improvements in the
iteration. Further, it provides discussion at the point of iteration about what has improved, which options or
pathways are no longer achievable and what implications this has for the future. This approach is in alignment
with creating adaptable and ﬂexible pathways, which take inspiration from the ﬂexible platforms approach
included in the ABCD-methodology. In such a case, it is important to state that the reconsideration and adap-
tation of the future strategies may seem simple in theory, however, in practice it is a highly complex topic. It
has to consider several variables such as: how has the availability of promising solutions changed over time;
has there been a shift in political preference; have the objectives of the study changed, etc. These considera-
tions are not exhaustive and should be tested in a practical application of the methodology.
Further discussion is needed in regards to the context of the backcasting application and its implications on
the evaluation process. Looking at a European context, it is rather unlikely that proposed pathways ﬁnd direct
135
==PAGE:149==
Chapter 10. Discussion and Recommendations
DTU Environment
implementation due to the complex nature of policy development in the European Union. The evaluation of
progress towards set objectives (such as milestones) is still valuable as it can show in which areas improve-
ments are generated through implemented solutions and policies. This, in return, can inform the iteration of a
backcast. When applying the DTU backcasting methodology in a business context, the outcome of a backcast
might be more likely to ﬁnd implementation in business policy. Thereby, the element of evaluation can di-
rectly be linked to identiﬁed policies and a direct comparison between designed pathways and made progress
is feasible.
It can be added that the methodology provided at this point does not elaborate on the tasks and exercises that
should be conducted in detail when approaching an iteration. The information provided in the context of the
DTU methodology is by no means exhaustive as there is a broad range of factors that need to be considered and
which conclusions should be drawn from them. Thereby, it is suggested that further research and reﬁnement of
this methodological step should be undertaken. A possibly fruitful approach to generating adaptive pathways
could be the tool of ’fault tree analysis’ in combination with a Six Sigma-like management system (Carter,
2010).
10.1.5
Uncertainty, Resilience, Adaptability, Flexibility and Lock-ins
The concepts of uncertainty, resilience, adaptability, ﬂexibility and lock-ins are important to consider in strate-
gic planning. First, it is important to discuss uncertainty. If the future was certain, strategic planning would
be easy, however, this is not the case. As discussed prior, this uncertain coupled with the urgency of action to
ﬁght climate change has resulted in a post-normal science-policy interaction. Thus, the topic of uncertainty
is vital to consider to increase the policy relevance of the DTU backcasting methodology. Uncertainty is a
function of the duration of time into the future, the further into the future, the larger the uncertainty. This
is since the possible scenarios space is larger (hence the cone shape of the futures cone, see Figure 8.3) and
predictions are less reliable, as they are based upon more assumptions. Thus, when considering long-term
planning, it is vital to acknowledge that this uncertainty is unavoidable. Hence, it is important to consider
resilience (sometimes referred to as robustness). The intention behind resilience is to determine pathways
towards the future which are hardy to uncertain events. Two approaches to resilience are related to ﬂexibility
and adaptability. Adaptability is related to being dynamic and changing, quick enough, to deal with uncer-
tainties that may arise in the future. Being adaptable is vital in the foresight and futures studies ﬁeld. This is
the intention behind scenario analysis, by considering signiﬁcant and uncertain scenarios which could come
into reality, eﬀective solutions to navigate these issue can be generated. This is how the Shell corporation
managed to adapt to the 1973 oil crisis Wack (1985). However, to adapt to uncertain and signiﬁcant issues, the
current trajectory must be ﬂexible. This means that it must be possible to change the trajectory or direction
of a pathway so that it can adapt. Hence, there should not be any lock-ins. Lock-ins can cause pathways to
become rigid and if a signiﬁcant issue is to occur it can undermine the entire pathway.
In the context of the DTU backcasting methodology, the intention behind the application of the methodology
is to inform resilient pathways which can deal with the uncertainty of the future. When considering long-term
pathways, resilience is diﬃcult to design as often the later elements of the pathways have prior dependencies
136
==PAGE:150==
Chapter 10. Discussion and Recommendations
DTU Environment
on the earlier elements succeeding. This complication is the reasoning behind the Natural Step’s ﬂexible
platforms approach. However, in the context of the DTU methodology, resilience can be achieved by utilising
the ﬂexible pathways approach as well as considering adaptation solutions. However, to eﬀectively consider
the ﬂexible pathways approach in the pathway generation step, there needs to be a detailed consideration of
lock-ins. Lock-ins are hard to determine as they are a function of future developments. There needs to be some
level of deeper analysis in regards to the solutions to determine how lock-in situations could occur. Besides, the
process of developing adaptation solutions requires scenario analysis in the exploratory scenario building step.
The exploratory scenario analysis helps by rationalising uncertain scenarios, this is particularly important to
determine early warning signs. Often, when navigating uncertain futures it is diﬃcult to be certain on the
impacts of events which can undermine the pathways. As such, understanding early warning signs can give
enough time for adaptation solutions to be implemented. However, to implement adaptation as well as to
identify if early warning signs are occurring, an iteration of the backcasting methodology must be performed
and scenario analysis further repeated. This is in reference to step 7 of the DTU methodology. Considering
resilience is vital to forming eﬀective pathways and from a theoretical perspective, we recommend that this
consideration should be highly ranked in the DTU methodology and other foresight approaches. The elements
in the DTU methodology aim to tackle these considerations around uncertainty, however, they take time to
include and thus, a decisions need to be made on what elements of the DTU methodology are to be included
or excluded due to time requirements.
10.1.6
Time Requirements and Duration of the Backcasting
Relevant aspects to discuss regarding the application of the DTU backcasting methodology are related to the
elements of time, resources and duration of a backcasting application. All of these elements are related to each
other and mutually inﬂuence the application in a backcasting study. In respect to time, it can be said that the
application of backcasting requires a considerable amount of time. As no practical example or workshop was
carried out in the context of this thesis, no exact time requirements can be provided. However, this aspect is
crucial when considering to apply the methodology. As gathered from experts, a backcasting application can
range massively between a few months to some years depending on the level of participation, use of certain
methodological concepts and ultimately the preparation needed for conducting the backcast itself (i.e. in form
of workshops, working documents, models). Further, the element of iteration in the backcasting methodology
requires a considerable amount of additional time and ﬁnancial resources by design. This is further dependent
on the approach taken by the administrators in regards to the iteration. The level of detail that is generated
in each of the individual steps of the DTU methodology is directly related to the time required to conduct
the application. Thereby, it could be argued that in case a low level of detail is documented, similar to a
streamlined approach of the DTU methodology, less time will be required overall. In comparison to that, a
detailed backcasting study will take considerably more time and as such requires a larger iteration. It can be
argued that the element of iteration can decrease the time needed for the conduction of the backcast due to
improvements of the workshops, previously conducted work and established models (if chosen to be applied).
As echoed by the students in the teaching-based research activity, reviewed case studies and experts, the ele-
ment of iteration is a core asset of backcasting. As backcasting should not be applied as a one-shot approach,
it provides guidance in achieving the desired vision. Thereby, if chosen to exclude the iteration, this method-
137
==PAGE:151==
Chapter 10. Discussion and Recommendations
DTU Environment
ology would not be capable of supporting long-term policy processes as it does not accommodate for changes
in objectives, the current state and other drivers of change relevant to the system/transition. In regards to the
inclusion of participants, it can be stated that additional time needs to be set aside for administrative tasks and
the management of workshops. The inclusion of stakeholders will further require the scheduling of dates and
arrangement of workshop facilities. In this regard, it could be argued that ﬁnancial limitations also relate to
the choice of assessment tools as the application of certain tools (e.g. models or LCA) require trained experts
to conduct the exercise and analyse the results.
Lastly, the aspect of component selection of the DTU backcasting methodology can ﬁnd a discussion about
time requirements. The methodology as such is designed to provide ﬂexibility from a theoretical perspective.
The application of which in practical terms has implications on the subsequent steps of the methodology
as well as the required time. Every additional step conducted in the methodology requires additional time,
which in respect to the purpose of the backcast and its available project budget might cause conﬂicts and
limitations. Therefore, it is necessary to consider what the intention of applying backcasting in each case is;
what resources are available and therefore, which components of the proposed methodology are necessary.
As stated prior, no practical application of the DTU methodology could be conducted to further investigate
the outlined reﬂections in regards to time requirements. This aspect might reduce the beneﬁts of the proposed
methodology if applied without deeper consideration and reﬂection.
10.1.7
Participatory Elements
The element of participation in regards to the application in the DTU backcasting methodology ﬁnds discus-
sion in the following. As commonly applied in other backcasting methodologies, the element of participation
is an option in the proposed methodology in this thesis. Alternatively, the methodology could be applied in an
internal expert setting. This is dependent on the available resources and the chosen purpose of the backcasting
application. The full potential of backcasting is unleashed when including multiple views and stakeholders
and therefore, it is recommended to apply backcasting in a participatory context. Associated to this recom-
mendation is the need for discussion of some of the presented ﬁndings in Chapters 7 and 8.
The term ’stakeholder’ can be used in numerous diﬀerent ways and depends on the context of the application.
As an example, the deﬁnition of stakeholders at the European Environment Agency was provided in chapter
7. This highlights the diﬀerent approaches to participatory processes and the inclusion of stakeholders. The
diﬀerences and implications which result out of the deﬁnition of stakeholders are considered a delimitation in
the context of this thesis. However, it is necessary to state that the deﬁnition of the four stakeholder groups
as taken from Kok et al. (2006) may oversimplify the relations between diﬀerent stakeholders. This leads to
a missing level of resolution as more relevant information could be gathered about stakeholders, e.g. their
power relationships in workshops and their possible contribution in a backcasting exercise. An additional
important consideration is related to the quality and character of information that each stakeholder type could
contribute. Especially, interesting is the contribution of stakeholders from a ﬁeld of disciplines to contribute
to backcasting in an interdisciplinary setting. It can be argued that this element is extremely relevant for
conducting a backcasting study to inform in a policy context. This is due to the complex relationships between
138
==PAGE:152==
Chapter 10. Discussion and Recommendations
DTU Environment
cause and eﬀect and the implication of made policy choices on society and existing systems. The above-stated
considerations can be considered as further topics to investigate and thereby, to close the gap between the
applied stakeholder terminology in this thesis and a more complex and detail deﬁnition of participants in a
backcasting study.
10.2
DTU Backcasting Methodology in a Practical and
Research Context
The following section provides a discussion of the DTU backcasting methodology as a whole, in a larger
political and research context. In particular how the methodology ﬁts in an EU policy context, how it compares
to similar backcasting approaches and the beneﬁts it has to oﬀer. Throughout the following section, further
points for exploration are highlighted.
10.2.1
Backcasting in an EU Policy Context
As outlined in the introduction of this thesis, regarding the current environmental situation and the global
threat of climate change, it becomes apparent that there is a need for change. This change has to be aligned
with the vision and goal setting for the future. Speciﬁcally for Europe, the ambition to achieve a sustainable
society with net-zero emission by 2050 is an immense challenge. To tackle this challenge there is a need for the
right concepts and tools to support the political discourse about the desirable pathways towards a sustainable
future.
The European Green Deal mentions the need to involve the public and stakeholders in the process of transition-
ing the societal and economical dimensions of society and to protect the state of the environment (European
Commission, 2019a). It is in their interest to spare ecological systems from breakdown and thereby to protect
future generations from their subsequent detrimental consequences. Participatory processes in backcasting
can provide support in an EU policy context as the complexity of system transition requires the participation
of every societal actor. Backcasting in these terms can serve as a discussion platform to concretely deter-
mine what we, as members of the European Union, want in the future and further, how we need to get there.
Thereby, backcasting can provide insights on diﬀerent perspectives, opinions and values of stakeholders. It
further can enable discussion about relevant topics that will come as a consequence of envisioned futures.
This is especially interesting looking at the EU recognition of negative impacts of climate change and the
endeavour of green transition, as stated in the ’just transition’ element of the European Green Deal. The in-
formation about possible trade-oﬀs, negative impacts and unavoidable consequences is extremely relevant in
this context and represents an element that backcasting is capable of supporting. Further, backcasting can
spark the process of ﬁnding solutions and innovations, which are required to ﬁll the gap between the current
system conditions and the vision for 2050. Despite all the ambitions seen at a European policy level, it is
also apparent that change towards a sustainable form of economy and society is making slow progress. The
delay of inevitable action increases the risks and costs for future generations and could cause consequences of
unforeseeable magnitude (respecting tipping points, the collapse of ecosystems, etc. EEA (2019a)). Turning so-
139
==PAGE:153==
Chapter 10. Discussion and Recommendations
DTU Environment
ciety and existing production-consumption systems from their current state into an environmentally balanced
European community is a challenge that grows, as more time is wasted in delaying this action. Backcasting
in this context can be a powerful tool to support the ideation and discussion needed to make this transition
possible. It further serves as a social learning platform to inform stakeholders about the consequences of in-
action and thereby motivates to participate in the process of moving forward towards a desirable future.
The DTU backcasting methodology, as well as other methods in the ﬁeld of backcasting, are vital approaches to
facilitate the above-mentioned applications. In the context of policy-support at a European level, it becomes
apparent that such tools can ﬁnd application with generating, analysing, contextualising and distributing
information. Looking at the example of the European Environment Agency which is responsible for providing
sound and independent information on the environment, it can be said that involving stakeholders is crucial.
The complexity of systems and their interconnections in this day and age cannot be assessed without the
involvement of stakeholders. Using backcasting and involving politicians as a tool to translate visions into
political long-term goals has been acknowledged by the European Environment Agency (EEA, 2019a). An
additional element that is incorporated in the DTU methodology is the element of assessment. Assessing
whether identiﬁed solutions, pathways and scenarios are considerate of all their associated impacts on the
environment and related topics, such as social or economic dimensions, is crucial when providing information
in a policy-support context. This element is included in the DTU methodology and despite its potential for
further research, could prove relevant for an application in a policy-related context. The scale of change
needed in a European context requires diverse tools to serve a multitude of purposes. Besides, these tools
need to handle issues related to uncertainty and build resilience into strategic planning. By eﬀectively studying
pathways towards the objectives and the scenario space, better communication of information can be conveyed
between science and policy, which is particularly important to tack the prevalent environmental issues in this
post-normal setting. This is what the DTU methodology tries to accommodate for as it can be applied in the
context of research, discussion and future planning. With this underlying ﬂexible character, the proposed
methodological concept is ideal for use across diﬀerent contexts supporting a transition with a long-term
perspective.
10.2.2
Comparison to the ABCD-Methodology
The DTU backcasting methodology incorporates many of the considerations which are fundamental to the
ABCD-methodology and the larger FSSD. Granted, certain elements of both methodologies are fundamental
to all backcasting studies such as deﬁning the objectives and the current state. However, some larger similar-
ities can be observed. Both approaches aim to develop a methodology which is iterated over the time horizon
of the study and which is capable of guiding long-term sustainable development. As such, the DTU method-
ology took inspiration from the work of sustainability principles developed by The Natural Step (Holmberg,
1998; Holmberg and Robèrt, 2000). However, to increase the ﬂexibility of the DTU methodology, this term was
broadened to sustainability conditions so that alternative boundaries of sustainability can be included. The
intention is still the same, to provide a deﬁnition of sustainability which can consistently guide development
over the duration of time until the objectives are met. This element found discussion in section 10.1.1. The
solution step of the DTU methodology can be compared to step C of the ABCD-methodology. In step C of
140
==PAGE:154==
Chapter 10. Discussion and Recommendations
DTU Environment
the ABCD-methodology creative solutions, which work in progress to achieving the objectives, are generated.
These solutions are then prioritised in the next step using the ﬂexible platforms approach (see section 7.10).
The DTU methodology diﬀerentiates from The Natural Step’s approach to generating solutions by incorpo-
rating scenario building. The step is more closely related to the work pioneered by Prof. Dr. John Robinson.
The scenarios identiﬁed in the scenario building step are deconstructed to inform the solution bank. Further,
the approach to generating action towards the future is diﬀerent. In the ABCD-methodology pathways are
not considered, instead, a path (see terminology in section ) is constructed by considering ﬂexible solutions
(ﬂexible platform approach) which are selected to inform short-term action with long-term vision. In the
DTU methodology, full pathways toward the objectives of the study are considered. These pathways can be
generated in numerous ways, however, a ’ﬂexible pathways’ approach can be used to allow adaptability and
pathway jumping. This ﬂexible pathways approach is inspired by the work from The Natural Step as well as
the work conducted in Giaoutzi et al. (2012).
This diﬀerence in the generation of pathway and paths is the key diﬀerence between the two methodologies
since it is the only element the DTU backcasting methodology does not accommodate for. However, the
reasoning for this is twofold. First, the context of the DTU methodology is concerning EU politics. As a result,
the purpose is predominately (but not in all cases) to inform policymakers on the potential options towards
achieving the objectives. Thus, considering a range of pathways portrays how the long-term vision can vary
and further results in forced reﬂection as to numerous potential options which may not have been conceived.
In a political context, this can be supported by public and stakeholder preferences. The second reason is related
to the potential scale of backcasting studies. If the backcasting is to be conducted in a European context, the
scale of change required is unprecedented. As such, large scale change takes considerable time and is subject to
higher levels of uncertainty. Therefore, it could be argued that full pathways must be considered to understand
the bigger picture and to identify areas where there are gaps in the data, hence are uncertain. Also, exploring
multiple pathways can help deal with this uncertainty by rationalising scenarios in uncertain areas. Thus, in
a European political context, the DTU methodology can provide additional beneﬁts.
10.2.3
Beneﬁts and Limitations of DTU Backcasting Methodology
The DTU backcasting methodology aims to provide an all-inclusive methodology that can facilitate a range of
tools, techniques and approaches towards achieving long-term visions. As seen in the review of backcasting
case studies, there is a multitude of methodologies which are tailored to ﬁt with the context, scope and purpose
of the individual studies. Thus, if backcasting is to be used in a European policy context there needs to be a
framework that allows for adaptability of the methodologies to ﬁt with these deliberations. The contributions
of the proposed methodology in this thesis can be discussed in two regards, its contribution to the academic
area and research, and the practical side of the methodology.
From an academic perspective, the DTU backcasting methodology draws upon knowledge from a range of
authors in the ﬁeld, each with diﬀerent approaches, and brings together a medley of considerations under one
methodology. As a result, the DTU methodology includes a range of concepts that enable a thorough back-
casting exercise. The backcasting approach conferred combines key attributes of, backcasting from principles,
141
==PAGE:155==
Chapter 10. Discussion and Recommendations
DTU Environment
scenarios and participatory backcasting. In doing so, it aims to utilise the beneﬁts of each methodological con-
cept. By applying additional elements of the methodology for example predictive or exploratory scenarios,
additional relevant concepts can be introduced to support the ﬁndings of the backcasting exercise. As outlined
prior, such concepts include the ability to introduce scenario exploration to increase the awareness and pre-
paredness in case of undesired events occurring, which in return supports the generation of ﬂexible solutions
and pathways. Including the identiﬁcation of policy, options allow to gain insights on political matters of de-
signed pathways and thereby generate overarching pathways which come with a diﬀerent set of implications
and information. This ﬂexibility of the methodology is the core attribute and is capable of adapting to diﬀerent
purposes, be it for research purposes or an application in a political context. In particular, this methodology
brings together learnings to provide objective descriptions on how to generate detailed pathways, an element
that is seldom described in the literature. The DTU methodology includes many approaches that can help
understand the structure of pathways and at the same time facilitates the generation of pathways depending
on the intended application. The formulation of pathways is supported by step 4 of the methodology, which
is a placeholder for identiﬁed solutions and their related information. This information can be enhanced by
assessing the identiﬁed solutions. The mentioned assessment element is an additional beneﬁt of the DTU
methodology, which despite its reference in identiﬁed case studies, did not ﬁnd detailed consideration be-
fore. In the proposed methodology two dedicated assessment applications are suggested and can be applied
depending on the choice of the administrators. The assessment aspires to generate relevant information in
regards to the impacts, performance and preferability of solutions and pathways. Consideration of each of
these elements was not observed in such a manner before.
From a practical perspective, the DTU backcasting methodology provides a high level of detail and consider-
ations on each structural element of the methodology. Such detail was commonly observed to be missing in
identiﬁed case studies. Further, the proposed methodology can be applied by the practitioner depending on
their intended purpose and respective resources. This will allow the backcasting methodology to be as eﬃcient
as needed, whilst providing the desired level of detail. This ﬂexibility of the methodology further comes into
play when considering a constant iteration of the procedure. If additional elements, such as exploratory sce-
narios in step 3 of the methodology, are considered relevant at the time of iteration, the concept can easily be
added to the procedure of the application. The same can be said about the inverse, where elements which were
included in a previous application are deemed irrelevant for the next iteration. To draw an analogy to Donella
Meadows’ work on leverage points, one could state that designing frameworks that are ﬂexible and adaptive
with the ability to evolve, is a powerful leverage point (Meadows, 2010). Hence, this method tries to achieve a
high degree of ﬂexibility, whilst at the same time, balancing the need to provide objective instructions on how
to conduct a backcasting study. Further research and development of the proposed methodology are consid-
ered vital to tailor backcasting for speciﬁc policy purposes in a political context. As such this methodology
and thesis as a whole presents all the necessary considerations that have been identiﬁed through extensive
literature search and expert consultation.
Naturally, the methodology proposed in this thesis is subject to discussions outside of the theoretical domain
and should ﬁnd application in a real case to prove the validity of made statements. When discussing the
presented work it is necessary to highlight some limitations that were observed in regards to the proposed
142
==PAGE:156==
Chapter 10. Discussion and Recommendations
DTU Environment
methodology. These limitations were partially already outlined in the previous sections can be related to two
factors encountered during this thesis. They relate to the chosen delimitations and the level of complexity of
the resulting DTU backcasting methodology. These limitations are summarised in the following.
The ﬁrst element that causes limitations in terms of the DTU backcasting methodology, is related to the chosen
scope and delimitations of the thesis. Several delimitations in this context inﬂuence the outcome regarding
the proposed methodology and therefore, opens up space for discussion for which further concepts could be
applied and tested. Relevant to mention here is the assessment component of the DTU methodology. The de-
limitation regarding the assessment of solutions and pathways was taken to cover the basic tools and concepts
relevant to the proposed methodology. In return, more in-depth investigation is missing on how diﬀerent tools,
such as modelling, could be utilised to assess pathways. Further, the topic of assessment can be expanded to
accommodate other relevant assessment criteria, touching upon economic or social impact assessment. An-
other crucial delimitation was taken in regards to conducting a conclusive case study. This had implications on
the available information on the required time to conduct the DTU methodology and the use of stakeholders
in regards to the individual steps of the methodology. These elements relate to the practical side of applying
the proposed methodology and could only be supported by providing estimates and guidance as gathered from
reviewed case studies and discussion with experts. To close this gap it is recommended to conduct further re-
search on concepts and relevant topics as outlined in previous sections and to conduct a case study to further
the reﬂections regarding the DTU methodology.
The second element of limitation and the reason why a full application of the DTU backcasting methodology
could not be applied in this thesis is due to complexity and requirements in regards to time and resources. It
is possible to conduct the DTU methodology without any of the supporting tools in regards to participation,
modelling and detailed assessment. However, due to the large uncertainty of strategic future planning, without
the validity of these tools, the concluding pathways have no credibility and may render misinformed outcomes.
Without credible backing, the conclusions will not inﬂuence informing policy. To facilitate these tools to
support the study, signiﬁcant time and expertise must be designated as discussed in section 10.1.6. Thus, there
is a trade-oﬀbetween, the credibility of the study and time requirements. Further, it must be stated that if
a thorough backcasting study is conducted, there will be increased credibility behind the recommendations.
However, the outcome of the study can lose relevance due to the urgency of the information required in a
post-normal science setting.
143
==PAGE:157==
Chapter 11
Conclusion
This master thesis set out to explore how backcasting can be developed as a methodological tool to be used to
achieve the European Union 2050 goals of sustainability. The intention was to address the following research
questions:
• What is the state of art in the context of backcasting applications for strategic planning?
• What are relevant concepts and identiﬁed gaps regarding the topic of backcasting for long-term policy
support and what conclusions can be drawn from the ﬁndings?
In doing so, a large network of experts in backcasting and related ﬁelds was established; an in-depth back-
casting case study review was conducted and a teaching based research activity with a class of students was
administered. The prior work resulted in an array of learnings which were crucial to this thesis.
The state of the art in the context of backcasting for strategic planning was documented by reviewing the
literature and a total of 15 case studies. Until today, backcasting has seen several diﬀerent concepts and ap-
proaches. Grouped into the respective categories of backcasting from scenarios, backcasting from principles
and participatory backcasting, the methodology has found application across a range of purposes, ﬁelds and
contexts. Identiﬁed purposes vary from sparking discussion on identiﬁcation of short-term solutions, identi-
ﬁcation of research gaps, to supporting policy development in local, national or international contexts. Case
studies apply backcasting in diﬀerent ﬁelds and contexts, such as energy, transport, mobility and others. Com-
mon to almost all methodologies from reviewed case studies are the steps: ’visioning’, ’analysis of the current
state’ and ’generation of solutions’. A remarkable ﬁnding is a shift towards participatory backcasting which
emphasises the inclusion of diﬀerent stakeholders to expand involved perspectives and thereby, give ground
for a collective design of a desired future and the achievement of such. The literature covered in this respect
is not complete and should ﬁnd further investigation for future work. Backcasting ﬁnds frequent application
to guide sustainable future planning. Two concepts were identiﬁed in literature which either targets the as-
sessment of backcasting outcomes and/or applies a set of principles to guide sustainable development. On
one hand, an environmental, social and economic impact assessment was identiﬁed which lacked a detailed
explanation in the literature. On the other hand, the application of the ABCD-methodology showed con-
sideration of sustainability principles which were fundamental for the subsequent procedures of short-term
decision making. An element that is not well represented in literature is the element of pathway generation in
backcasting. Two basic concepts were identiﬁed. First, the generation of a singular path through a gradual and
iterative procedure over the time period of the study, where short-term action is conducted with long-term
visioning. The second concept incurred the development of multiple complete pathways from the current
state to the objectives of the study. Lastly, it can be stated that backcasting encompasses a range of diﬀerent
144
==PAGE:158==
Chapter 11. Conclusion
DTU Environment
methodologies which are designed to address speciﬁc needs and which hold their merits and limitations. Re-
markable is the approach taken through the Framework for Strategic Sustainable Development which includes
the ABCD backcasting methodology. Nevertheless, it does not accommodate for the generation of pathways
in their methodology, which leads to the conclusion that there is no one-size-ﬁts-all methodology.
It was found that there is a lack of a suﬃcient backcasting methodology which is designed to be integrated
into an EU political context, to address pressing issues around sustainability. Two studies were identiﬁed
which apply backcasting on an EU level to address sustainability issues. However, their use was limited to a
single shot operation and no further applications resulted out of their use. Thus, to provide a methodology
that can ﬁt in an EU political context, a methodology would have to be ﬂexible to ﬁnd relevance and use with
varying applications and purposes. It was found that, if a methodology was to be generated, greater detail
on how to generate pathways in a backcasting study is required. Amongst the literature, vague explanations
were presented which did not provide suﬃcient information on how to reproduce pathways. Additionally,
there needs to be an element of assessment which can be used to support the decision-making process and
further the relevance of a backcasting methodology. Assessment approaches were observed in a few of the
backcasting case studies, however, they exempliﬁed that more work should be done in this area.
It was concluded from these gaps that since there is no suﬃcient methodology which could be used in an
EU political context, that a methodology must be developed to meet these requirements. As a result of these
ﬁndings, the DTU backcasting methodology is presented at the core of this thesis. For elaboration, the method-
ology is in parts exempliﬁed in the form of a case study, tackling the apparent challenges in the EU energy
system. The DTU backcasting methodology is a seven-step process which consists of: (1) setting objectives,
(2) understanding the current state, (3) scenario building, (4) solutions, (5) pathways towards the objectives, (6)
assessment and (7) evaluation and iteration. Each of these steps consists of numerous sub-steps which guide
the application of the methodology and allow the administrator to ﬂexibly apply the method for the chosen
purpose. In the context of this thesis, it is recommended to apply the DTU backcasting methodology to its
full extent, making use of scenario building techniques, assessment of solutions and constant iteration of the
application. Further, it is key to consider the involvement of stakeholders as they represent the key to a just
green transition.
The developed methodology contributes to the ﬁeld of backcasting as well as advancing knowledge of fore-
sight and future techniques into the realm of EU policy. From an academic perspective, this work furthers
the ﬁeld by facilitating a methodology which draws upon a range of concepts and considerations from case
studies and experts in the ﬁeld of backcasting. The methodology was structured to accommodate for a medley
of identiﬁed techniques in a ﬂexible manner which, when used in combination, further the value of the incor-
porated backcasting techniques in an EU political context. In particular, the DTU backcasting methodology
provides structured considerations on how to formulate pathways towards the objectives. Further, it aims to
facilitate assessment steps to help form pathways and in a practical setting, further provide information to the
policymakers.
145
==PAGE:159==
Chapter 11. Conclusion
DTU Environment
From a practical perspective, the methodology presented is structured and informative, yet allows for adapt-
ability so that it is applicable for a range of purposes. This aims to build a foundation from which a multi-
purpose backcasting methodology can evolve and as such making the application of backcasting more accessi-
ble for the wider political use. The beneﬁt of generating a backcasting methodology for use in a policy setting
is plentiful. In particular, it allows for more informed decision making with reﬂections on what possible fu-
ture pathways are available. It helps to address topics of uncertainty and formulate better interaction between
science and policy, a valuable tool for the current circumstances in a post-normal science setting. Backcasting
is a concept further provides a platform in which dialogue between stakeholders and decision-makers can be
held, increasing transparency and input which is vital for a just and sustainable transition.
That being said there are limitations with the DTU backcasting methodology, that are important to acknowl-
edge to further the progress of a multi-purpose methodology. Two limitations were identiﬁed which encom-
pass a number of topics. These limitations are related to elements of the research which were delimited from
the scope of this thesis and the limitations due to the complexity of DTU backcasting methodology as a whole.
As a result of taken delimitations, provided techniques and tools in regards to the assessment of solutions and
pathways do not entail suﬃcient description to apply in a practical application without further investigation.
Without having conducted a complete application of the DTU backcasting methodology in practical terms, it
has to be acknowledged that uncertainties remain in regards to required resources such as budget and time.
Further uncertainties are related to the role of stakeholders. In regards to the complexity of the DTU backcast-
ing methodology, it can be stated that there is a need for suﬃcient expertise to facilitate the use of participation,
modelling or assessment tools. Further, the use of these tools is costly concerning time limitations. However,
these tools provide validity and hence, without the considerate application, the resulting outcome of the study
may lose credibility to inform policy.
As a concluding remark, a number of relevant topics should be explored in the future to support the presented
ﬁndings of this thesis. A deeper analysis of assessment techniques could be considered to expand the DTU
backcasting methodology. Ideally, the proposed methodology should be able to provide suﬃcient information
regarding the assessment of solutions, pathways and scenarios, to allow for a transparent and reproducible
application. Tools that could be considered in this context relate to risk analysis. Further research on the use
of integrated assessment models in backcasting is recommended. A relevant area of research that is suggested
to enhance the capabilities of the DTU backcasting methodology, relates to the ﬁeld of foresight techniques.
It should be investigated how existing techniques and tools from this ﬁeld could be applied to enhance the
outcomes of proposed backcasting methodology. Finally, it is recommended to conduct a comprehensible
practical application of the DTU backcasting methodology on relevant areas, such as energy, agriculture and
mobility. This will allow assessing the ﬂexibility and the extent of outlined limitations reﬂected prior.

A.2
[Unedited] Reﬂections from Students of Course "12240 Environmental Man-
agement and Ethics" at Technical University of Denmark
Group 1 (Barouta et al., 2020) - Food:
Generally, we liked the idea of backcasting, as this is a way to ensure that we achieve the goals, by an iterative
process. However, due to time constraints the iterative process was almost impossible to implement. The pace
we had to work in, prevented us from really iterating our pathways towards the goal. Moreover, it was chal-
lenging to create 3 completely diﬀerent pathways with complete analysis in this time frame. Also, it would
probably contribute to our understanding of the memo, if we saw an example of a political memo with path-
ways (not necessarily on this subject) to get a feeling of what is expected from us. The cost-beneﬁt analysis,
stakeholder analysis and DPSIR model’s presentations were not suﬃcient for us to really understand how to
use them correctly. Moreover, we felt along the process that some of the milestones we set were unfounded,
thus making it diﬃcult to analyse properly using decision-supporting tools. It was very time consuming and
tough to ﬁnd speciﬁc quantitative data to support some of the analyses, such as for the cost-beneﬁt analysis,
which caused to make a qualitative analysis, or to include analysis as part of the pathways.
Group 2 (Stefánsdóottir et al., 2020) - Food:
Overall, the usage of the backcasting method was suitable as an approach to deﬁne the three pathways and
how to reach the predeﬁned goals. By visualising the wanted outcomes of a sustainable food system (with
emphasis on production and consumption), by looking at the current situation and expected status in 2050,
it became clear which main goals must be met. The process allowed the group to discuss possible pathways
to follow in order to reach the desired vision, by moving back step by step from the goal. When discussing
possible pathways to reach the desired visions, multiple ideas came up and could be discussed from a realistic
point of view in regards to the goal. Does the pathway result in the exact goal? Is the pathway realistic? Could
the pathway be made more focused to ensure reaching the desired goals? These are a couple of questions the
group discussed for each pathway idea that came up during the thinking process. When applying the method,
especially step D, the group found it complicated to decide on the priorities on how to achieve the desired
goals. By enhancing four sustainability principles, core purposes and values, it was possible to become more
focused, but yet the broadness of the pathways made it complicated to choose where to draw the boundaries.
How deep to analyse each step? What are the most important steps in terms of sensitivity analysis?
In general, the group agrees that the backcasting method is a good tool to use when implementing visions for
goals on both small and large scales. It allows broad visualisation on how a desired goal can be reached and
welcomes innovative ideas to be discussed in diﬀerent terms.
Group 6 (Binderup et al., 2020) - Energy:
In general backcasting can be seen as the reverse process to forecasting when it comes to develop approaches
or solutions for technical, environmental or social aspect with reference to future developments. Hence, back-
casting is mainly characterised by an analysis which is not focusing on what is likely to happen but on how
desirable futures can be attained. Due to that, this method involves working backwards from a particular
point in the future which is desired to the present. Hereby the physical feasibility and the required political
measures to reach that goal need to be deﬁned. Whereas forecasting would mean using the current state as a
164
==PAGE:178==
DTU Environment
starting point and developing goals from that initial point. During the process of this report, the backcasting
method has been applied. One major concern of the group has been, that this method might not be as straight
forward as the forecasting method and therefore requires a lot more understanding before applying it. Hence,
both the developer of the solutions and the reader need to be aware of the structural characteristics of the
backcasting method. Typically backcasting is applied on long-term complex issues, involving many aspects
of society as well as technological innovations and change. When taking this into account, after writing this
report, two general conclusions could be drawn. On the one hand, the backcasting method is allowing for a far
more creative approach when developing ideas, due to the fact that in general everything is allowed in order
to reach the vision and goals. On the other hand, one needs to constantly remember the focus of the problem
which is to be solved. Since most of the time the focus of interest is on a perceived societal problem of great
importance such as the impacts of climate change on the environment and whole societal systems, one clearly
needs to set limits on the desired output of the backcasting method. Nevertheless, when implementing the
backcasting method in a group work, it can help seeing the analysed problem from other perspectives, since
diﬀerent people have diﬀerent approaches and ideas concerning the problem solving process. However, in the
end it clearly needs to be reﬂected on if the generated ideas are feasible from a technological, economical and
ethical point of view. Are the diﬀerent pathways robust and fairly well calculated? In general the backcasting
method should be implemented during the process in order to reﬂect and draw conclusions from the previous
ideas.
In conclusion it should be pointed out, that the backcasting method provides an as far as possible ﬂexible
approach to solve problems by focusing on the overall goal which should be reached. It allows for creativity
during the process and can help with analysing the problem from diﬀerent perspectives. However, the focus
should be clearly set during the whole process and feedback loops need to be implemented in order to avoid
misunderstandings. When using backcasting completely new ideas as part of the solution can be generated.
These can tend to seem very attractive but require large amount of information search or generation of com-
pletely new data to tell anything about how the new idea would actually function in real life. This appear
to be a largely time consuming step that is important to have conserved time for. Two recommendations are
given to improve the backcasting method for future use. One is to include a step after having described the
current situation, step B, where time is spend on answering questions such as "For what reason(s) has the
current system become as it is?", "What reasoning was behind its implementation?", "What advantages does it
oﬀer", and "What will happen if the system is changed or removed?". Our forbears can have had less available
information, but it is plausible that a lot of systems where implemented because they where the logical choice
at the time. If the system is set up in a way so that it solves important problems it is important to identify these
problem and oﬀer solutions to them. To give an example, a rural area might have plastic bottles accumulating
in its nature, and to protect the nature a ban is made on plastic bottles. However, the water bottles were the
main source of clean drinking water to the inhabitants of the area who are then forced to drink unﬁltered
water from a local well, with possible health risks. Better solutions could have been to install a water treat-
ment facility, so that it was not necessary to continue to import water bottles or create collection facilities
to recapture the bottles once used. Another recommendation to be added to the backcasting method is to go
through the pathways are to look at how robust they are and try to improve them in this regard. Back in the
2000’s the where growing and relatively strong public support for climate mitigation policies, then the ﬁnan-
cial crisis happened, followed by a refugee crisis, and soon environmental concerns were replaced in favour of
165
==PAGE:179==
DTU Environment
more "pressing" matters such as unemployment, debt, and immigration policies. It seems apparent then that
any long-term solutions to climate change would have to be so robust as to function even when faced with a
economical recession or even war. Climate policy needs to be more than a privilege from economic booms.
Some of the issues described above are solved with the use of the resource nexus, however we believe that
the steps of 1) identifying how the current systems might be solving important problems and 2) providing
robustness to the pathways could oﬀer a more systematic and thus though approach.
Group 8 (Abrahim et al., 2020) - Mobility:
While performing the analysis, there are several pros and cons of the process, which are listed in the following
Table 2. Most of the pros come from the holistic approach of integrating diﬀerent disciplines to reach the goals
for transportation while the cons are the results of the lack of knowledge in general and the lack of time.

Group 14 (Granheim et al., 2020) - Mobility:
This section will provide a thorough assessment of the beneﬁts and the consequences of using the backcasting
approach in general, and with respect to the topic of mobility in Europe. Dreborg (1996) argues that backcast-
ing is not a method in strict sense, but that it is more useful to think of this as an approach which is particularly
useful when:
• The problem to be studied is complex
166
==PAGE:180==
DTU Environment
• There is a need for major change
• The dominant trends are part of the problem
• The problem to a great extent is a matter of externalities
• The scope is very wide
• The time horizon is long enough to leave considerable room for deliberate choice
In complex systems like the ecosphere, and with complicated projects, like sustainable development, this is
an eﬀective methodology to align various measures with each other, so that each activity can be the logical
platform for the next. Following Dreborg, backcasting is an approach to facilitate discovery, diﬀerent from
the more commonly applied strategy forecasting, i.e. starting the planning procedure from today’s situation,
and projecting today’s problems and trends, and what are considered realistic solutions today in to the future.
The complexity of planning for sustainability, because current trends and actions are part of the problem, has
highlighted the importance of applying backcasting method to have an informed vision of one’s goal in order
to strategically deal with potential trade-oﬀfrom diﬀerent decisions. The points mentioned above nearly all
apply to the problem of transforming the mobility in Europe, and can therefore be used as arguments to why it
is advantageous to use in this context. Furthermore, the often very long time frame that backcasting operates
under, can in many cases be a positive feature since it can ease the collaboration between stakeholders who
have potentially conﬂicting interests. The long-term perspective can provide time to ﬁnd solutions acceptable
for all. This is also a distinct positive in the case of transforming mobility in Europe, where a lot of heavy
industry is build up around the sector as it has been for the past many years. It can give time for companies
and industry to prepare for change and in that way still be a part of the new market or structure. As described
so far, there are many reasons why using the backcasting approach might be relevant and useful, however,
there are also potential drawbacks and risks involved in the process. Although it is also mentioned as a pos-
itive feature about the backcasting method, the long time frame can also have negative consequences. This
can come to show if governments or political control change during the process and the needed support is no
longer given, this can cause delays, or even put the project to a halt. In order for the long-term plans to work,
the stakeholders must all commit and stick to their roles, honesty and integrity are important ingredients. If
after a number of years important stakeholders change their personal agenda, it could have dire consequences,
and for this reason, openness and honesty are important, but also one of the reasons why backcasting can be
hard to manage.
Another potential downside to backcasting is that the solutions found, often are expensive and also often in-
volve technological solutions, these two factors can be unwelcome for representatives or stakeholders and the
therefore the project can be hindered on this behalf. Lastly, although not a con as such, it should be mentioned
that when applying the backcasting method it is very often highly recommendable to follow up by monitoring
and evaluation of the diﬀerent processes put in place, this step can be demanding, expensive and diﬃcult, but
is none the less essential. Backcasting is not a ’one size ﬁts all’ solution, it is a social learning process and will
most likely require evaluation and adjustment along the way.
167
==PAGE:181==
DTU Environment
Group 15 (Hureau et al., 2020) - Energy: This memo uses Backcasting as a method for exploring the sus-
tainability, ethical and economic challenges in regards to the energy sector in the EU. Backcasting as a method
has some disadvantages, especially in terms of application to sustainable development. It is diﬃcult to develop
detailed pathways towards sustainable outcomes on which everyone agrees, especially when related to inter-
national plan (Beloﬀet al., 2005). Since the technological development is ongoing, meaning that technologies
will probably change in the future and new development arise, it is diﬃcult to plan the future based on this.
From our experience with Backcasting, it seems to be easier to use on projects smaller than the whole energy
system in EU. This would also make plans for dealing with stakeholders easier, since there would not be many
of them. Backcasting has, however, helped develop innovative solutions, and the pathways to the goals have
in the initial stage been large in number, and led to controversy.
Group 16 - Mobility:
In this section we have put our reﬂection of the backcasting method and how it was put to use in this course.
We hope you will ﬁnd some use in our reﬂections.
Using a method requires knowledge
It was the ﬁrst time using the backcasting method for all four group members. This means that it would require
a decent amount of time assigned to research on how the method should be interpreted and used. However,
it was not our ﬁrst priority to assign this time. One of the reasons for this is that it did not seem like it would
be included in the ﬁnal exam. A second reason is that we did not ﬁnd time, as the work with each step was
overwhelming and time consuming, so we felt, we would not have enough time to work on our speciﬁc case,
if we also had to investigate the method. Moreover, our own opinion is that there was not enough focus on the
method overall during the lectures to actively work and intertwine it in the work made. It would be good if
it was mentioned more often in relation to the subjects introduced, and maybe an exercise or example on the
method could be introduced. It would have been helpful to see an example of a project where the method had
been used to get a better "gut feeling" of the process. Moreover, we think that the introduction presentation
that was made was very informative. However, the ﬁrst part about the history did not feel as relevant as the
wish of understanding how it works, for example through practical examples.
What we liked
What we did like with the method was the ability to brainstorm solutions, instead of focusing straight away
on the way to the solutions, since that is often restraining. It is nice, that it emphasises both backcasting and
forecasting, and that it takes the current state into consideration. It is a strength that it forces you to consider
almost every possible situation, even though we have found this frustrating at times.
Challenges we faced
In the work with the backcasting method, we faced problems with how to deﬁne the pathways. It was diﬃcult
to ﬁnd out whether they should have an overall theme or just be a collection of solutions to each problem
with main focus on diﬀerent actions. This could be improved by giving some examples of pathways, or maybe
a deeper introduction to what a pathway is (We know it was ﬁrst time the method was tried, so this is only
suggestions for future work). Moreover, a miss in the planning from us resulted in that we did the brainstorm-
168
==PAGE:182==
DTU Environment
ing of the pathways outside oﬃce-hours, which meant that we could not ask for help. So, there was probably
good guidance to get, but we did not use it. Something that should be taken into account when evaluating our
lack of understanding.
Conclusion
The main drawback for us as students was that we did not fully understand how to implement the method,
resulting in a very little focus on it and an issue to evaluate the method. However, we do believe that it is an
interesting method that could be used for political purposes, especially since it does not limit the brainstorm-
ing session much, compared to conventional methods.
Group 17 (Pérot et al., 2020) - Mobility:
As the task of the project was to ﬁnd tangible solutions of how to reach a vision, and well-deﬁned goals,
backcasting seems to be a very valid method. The process has been eﬃcient to identify ﬁtting pathways.
Another method, forecasting, could have been applied too. But forecasting is centered around data, prediction
and probabilities of outcomes. This method is known not to be as ambitious as backcasting, as it is based on
the current situation. It would often tend to be unsuitable for working towards well deﬁned goals and the
prediction can be followed by a large range of error. The urgency of the environmental situation in Europe,
cannot rely on an uncertain method.

B
Additional Information on Backcasting Methods from
Case Study Reviews
B.1
[Unedited] Examples of Future Images
B.1.1
"Decreasing energy use in buildings by 50% by 2050 — A backcasting study using stakeholder
groups" - Svenfelt et al. (2011)
The future image described in Svenfelt et al. (2011) is heavily based on technical details, heavily supported
by numbers. However, descriptive information is available about diﬀerent aspects of the future, i.e. role of
professionals or management of rental spaces.
Future image (a): the building sector as a driving force for progress.
This image focuses on what can be accomplished via technical solutions and changes in infrastructure
in order to enhance energy performance. Those who plan, build and manage buildings professionally
have acquired knowledge about energy-eﬃcient solutions. There are means of control and incentives
that accomplish energy-eﬃcient construction and renovation of the building stock; the buildings are
equipped with energy-eﬃcient technological solutions and are managed in ways that utilise the possi-
bilities oﬀered by technology. Newly-built single houses use 30 kWh/m2 and existing renovated houses
90 kWh/m2 for heating (current level 155 kWh/m2), plus household electricity at 30 kWh/m2 per house-
hold (current level 46 kWh/m2). Property management is very ﬂexible, so that the heated space can
be adjusted in accordance with the current demand. Oﬃce and house space is smaller, and instead ad-
ditional space can be rented when needed. The changes do not to any great extent inﬂuence people’s
everyday life. They do not have to be conscious about their energy use, as all available appliances are
energy-eﬃcient. Flexible, smart homes enable people to base more work and free time in the home,
enabling more eﬃcient use of waste heat from people and electrical devices.
B.1.2
"Envisioning sustainability: Recent progress in the use of participatory backcasting approaches
for sustainability research" - Robinson et al. (2011)
Figure 1 depicts iconic local places in order to heighten the relevance of development decisions and their
climate implications relative to the four ’regional storylines’ ( 1) Do Nothing , 2) Adapt to risk & Vulnerability,
3) Eﬃcient Development, 4) Deep Sustainability), revealing the critical importance of scale. The future image
presentation by Robinson et al. (2011) relies on depicting modelling results in a direct manner relating climate
impacts to sea level rise.

response to climate change in four futures. (Images by David Flanders taken from Robinson et al. (2011)).
